Mu'tazila and Third Group on Acts
Presentation of Mu'tazila and third group on acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on human acts and the contrasting view of a third group that posits dual power.

Core Idea
Most Mu'tazila say acts are solely by the servant's power; a third group says acts are by both powers.

This passage introduces two theological positions on the origin of human acts. The Mu'tazila assert that the servant's power alone suffices, while another group holds that both divine and human power concur. These views form the backdrop to the Ash'ari doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Observe the concise style.
The Arabic uses parentheses for clarification.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the Mu'tazila position (most of them) and the third group's view. Note the key terms: الاستقلال (independence), الإيجاب (necessitation), الاختيار (choice), القدرتين (the two powers).
The third group is not named.
3
Reflect on significance
Consider how these positions differ from the Ash'ari view that acts are created by God and acquired by the servant. This comparison will appear in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : ( أي أكثرهم هي ( واقعة بقدرة العبد وحدها ) على سبيل الاستقلال بلا إيجاب بل باختيار ) وقالت طائفة ) هي واقعة (بالقدرتين)
And the Mu‘tazila said—meaning most of them—: 'They occur by the servant's power alone, independently, without necessitation but by choice.' And a group said: 'They occur by both powers.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage continues the original text by presenting two alternative positions. First, the Mu‘tazila (most of them) hold that voluntary acts are brought about solely by the servant's power, independently (istiqlal), without external necessitation (ila ijab), but with free choice (ikhtiyar). Then a group (ta'ifa) is mentioned that holds the act occurs by both powers (bi-l-qudratayn)—i.e., God's power and the servant's power together. This third position is a middle ground between the Ash‘ari view and the Mu‘tazila.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الاستقلال
الإيجاب
الاختيار
القدرتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila held that acts are solely by the servant's power.
Correction: The passage says 'most of them', indicating some Mu'tazila disagreed, such as al-Najjar.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of most Mu'tazila regarding human acts?
A) Acts occur by God's power alone.
B) Acts occur by the servant's power alone, independently and by choice.
C) Acts occur by both God's power and the servant's power.
D) Acts occur by the servant's power but with divine assistance.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila (most) assert human independence in acts, while a third group maintains a dual-power view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you honestly attribute it to your own independent choice (as the Mu‘tazila insist) or do you shift responsibility to circumstances or even to Allah? How would your repentance change if you fully accepted that the act was by your power alone?
?
The third group says your actions occur by both your power and Allah's power. Do you live as if that is true? Before you perform a good deed, do you consciously ask Allah for His help, recognizing that your ability is not enough?
?
If your every voluntary act is truly your own independent creation (istiqlal), then you bear full responsibility. Does that weight frighten you? Or do you prefer the comfort of sharing responsibility with Allah? What does your preference reveal about your heart?

Scope of Dispute on Voluntary Acts
Commentator clarifies the scope of dispute, listing what is not disputed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the dispute regarding voluntary acts of servants, including non-voluntary acts, the Prophet's speech, and dumb animals.

Core Idea
Not all acts are disputed; only voluntary acts of servants are in debate, with exceptions clarified.

The commentator begins by clarifying what is not disputed. There is agreement that non-voluntary acts of servants are created by Allah, and that the Prophet's speech (if considered originated) is also created. The Mu‘tazila have their own explanations. The inquiry even extends to dumb animals, but the commentator notes that this has little benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the scope
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three categories mentioned: non-voluntary acts, Prophet's speech, dumb animals. See how the commentator says there is no dispute on the first two.
The term 'non-voluntary acts' refers to involuntary bodily movements.
2
Understand the Mu‘tazila position
The passage mentions that for the Mu‘tazila, the Prophet's speech is either not voluntary or excepted from the general rule. Recall previous discussions about speech.
3
Consider the extension to dumb animals
Note that the inquiry includes dumb animals, but the commentator says it has little benefit and thus was not addressed in detail. Reflect on why this might be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في أن أفعال العباد الاختيارية إلخ لا خلاف في أن أفعال الغير الاختيارية للعباد مخلوقة له تعالى كما هو المشهور ولا في أن الكلام اللفظي القائم بالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام على تقدير حدوثه مخلوقة له تعالى أيضا، أما عندنا فظاهر وأما عند المعتزلة فإما بنفي اختياريته أو باستثنائه عن الكلية كما مر مع ما فيه في بحث الكلام ثم إن البحث يعم الحيوانات العجم صرح به في أبكار الأفكار والمنقذ ، ولما لم يكن للنزاع في أفعالها الاختيارية كثير فائدة لم يتعرض لها وقال في أن أفعال العباد إلخ . قوله : ( فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ قلت : هذه الشرطية مستدركة فإن الكلام مسوق على قواعد أهل السنة والقدرة عندهم مع الفعل البتة فبعد إيجاد القدرة في العبد لا مجال للمانع ، قلت : ليس قوله فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ متفرعا على إيجاد القدرة بل على جريان عادته تعالى بأن يوجد فيه ذلك، والمعنى لما كان عادته تعالى جارية بذلك فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع لصدور الفعل عن العبد أوجد الله تعالى فيه فعلاً مقارناً لإيجاد القدرة فعلى هذا الاستدراك فتدبر.
His saying: 'That the voluntary acts of servants etc.' There is no disagreement that the non-voluntary acts of servants are created by Him, as is well-known, nor that the verbal speech subsisting in the Prophet, on him be peace, on the assumption of its being originated, is also created by Him. As for us it is obvious; as for the Mu‘tazila it is either by negating its being voluntary or by excepting it from the generality, as has passed with its problems in the discussion of speech. Then the inquiry includes dumb animals; this is stated in Abkar al-Afkar and al-Munqidh. But since the dispute about their voluntary acts has little benefit, he did not address it. And he said 'That the acts of servants etc.' His saying: 'Then if there is no obstacle etc.' I said: This conditional is superfluous because the discussion proceeds on the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, and power for them is actually concomitant with the act; so after the creation of power in the servant there is no room for an obstacle. I said: His saying 'Then if there is no obstacle etc.' is not derived from the creation of power but from the continuation of His custom of creating that in him. The meaning: because His custom continues in that way, then if there is no obstacle to the act's occurring from the servant, God creates in him an act concurrent with the creation of power. So upon this, the surplusage is [resolved]; reflect.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a commentary gloss on the original text. It begins by clarifying the scope of the dispute: there is agreement that involuntary acts are created by God, and that the Prophet's speech, if considered originated, is also created. The Mu‘tazila hold either that such speech is not voluntary or that it is excepted from the general rule. The commentator then extends the inquiry to dumb animals, citing sources, but notes that the debate has little benefit for them. The second part addresses an objection to the conditional phrase 'if there is no obstacle' (in lam yakun hunaka mani‘). The objector argues that the conditional is superfluous (mustadraka) because, on Sunni principles, power (qudra) always accompanies the act, so once God creates power in the servant, no obstacle can prevent the act. The commentator responds that the conditional is not derived from the creation of power but from God's custom (jarayan ‘adatihi) of creating the act concurrently when there is no obstacle. The meaning is that God's custom is to create the act when there is no obstacle, even though power is simultaneous. Thus the conditional is not redundant but indicates the condition under which the act occurs.

Key Terms
أفعال الغير الاختيارية
الكلام اللفظي
الحيوانات العجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over voluntary acts includes all acts, even involuntary ones and the Prophet's speech.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that involuntary acts and the Prophet's speech (if originated) are not disputed; they are agreed to be created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is NOT part of the dispute over voluntary acts?
A) Non‑voluntary acts of servants
B) Voluntary acts of servants
C) The Prophet's speech (if originated)
D) Acts of dumb animals

Key Takeaway
The dispute over voluntary acts of servants does not extend to non‑voluntary acts, the Prophet's speech, or dumb animals, as these are either agreed upon or of little benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator notes that the dispute over dumb animals' voluntary acts has little benefit. Are there theological or practical debates you engage in that yield little spiritual fruit? Will you commit to abandoning them and redirecting that energy to more beneficial pursuits?
?
There is no dispute that your non-voluntary acts are created directly by Allah. Do you ever pause to thank Him for these constant blessings? How can you turn this knowledge into a daily habit of gratitude?
?
The Prophet's speech is also created by Allah according to this passage. How does this affect your view of his words? Are you treating them as divine revelation worthy of complete obedience, or merely as human advice?

Resolving the Objection on the Conditional Clause
Commentator answers an objection regarding the conditional clause in the original text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conditional phrase 'if there is no obstacle' is not superfluous in the Sunni framework, but rather based on Allah's custom.

Core Idea
The conditional clause is rooted in Allah's custom, not redundant.

The commentator addresses an objection: the conditional 'if there is no obstacle' seems redundant because in Sunni theology, power is always concomitant with the act, leaving no room for obstacles. The commentator explains that the conditional is not based on the creation of power, but on Allah's perpetual custom of creating the act when the servant has power and no obstacle prevents it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find where the objector says the conditional is 'superfluous' (mustadraka). Understand why: because on Sunni principles, power is with the act, so no obstacle can exist after power is created.
2
Study the commentator's response
The commentator clarifies that the conditional is based on God's custom (ada), not the creation of power. He says 'His custom continues that way' meaning God's typical action is to create the act when there is no obstacle.
This resolves the apparent redundancy.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this explanation maintains both divine causation and human responsibility. The act occurs by divine creation, but only when the human has power and no obstacle prevents it.
This is a subtle point in Sunni kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في أن أفعال العباد الاختيارية إلخ لا خلاف في أن أفعال الغير الاختيارية للعباد مخلوقة له تعالى كما هو المشهور ولا في أن الكلام اللفظي القائم بالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام على تقدير حدوثه مخلوقة له تعالى أيضا، أما عندنا فظاهر وأما عند المعتزلة فإما بنفي اختياريته أو باستثنائه عن الكلية كما مر مع ما فيه في بحث الكلام ثم إن البحث يعم الحيوانات العجم صرح به في أبكار الأفكار والمنقذ ، ولما لم يكن للنزاع في أفعالها الاختيارية كثير فائدة لم يتعرض لها وقال في أن أفعال العباد إلخ . قوله : ( فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ قلت : هذه الشرطية مستدركة فإن الكلام مسوق على قواعد أهل السنة والقدرة عندهم مع الفعل البتة فبعد إيجاد القدرة في العبد لا مجال للمانع ، قلت : ليس قوله فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع إلخ متفرعا على إيجاد القدرة بل على جريان عادته تعالى بأن يوجد فيه ذلك، والمعنى لما كان عادته تعالى جارية بذلك فإذا لم يكن هناك مانع لصدور الفعل عن العبد أوجد الله تعالى فيه فعلاً مقارناً لإيجاد القدرة فعلى هذا الاستدراك فتدبر.
His saying: 'That the voluntary acts of servants etc.' There is no disagreement that the non-voluntary acts of servants are created by Him, as is well-known, nor that the verbal speech subsisting in the Prophet, on him be peace, on the assumption of its being originated, is also created by Him. As for us it is obvious; as for the Mu‘tazila it is either by negating its being voluntary or by excepting it from the generality, as has passed with its problems in the discussion of speech. Then the inquiry includes dumb animals; this is stated in Abkar al-Afkar and al-Munqidh. But since the dispute about their voluntary acts has little benefit, he did not address it. And he said 'That the acts of servants etc.' His saying: 'Then if there is no obstacle etc.' I said: This conditional is superfluous because the discussion proceeds on the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, and power for them is actually concomitant with the act; so after the creation of power in the servant there is no room for an obstacle. I said: His saying 'Then if there is no obstacle etc.' is not derived from the creation of power but from the continuation of His custom of creating that in him. The meaning: because His custom continues in that way, then if there is no obstacle to the act's occurring from the servant, God creates in him an act concurrent with the creation of power. So upon this, the surplusage is [resolved]; reflect.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a commentary gloss on the original text. It begins by clarifying the scope of the dispute: there is agreement that involuntary acts are created by God, and that the Prophet's speech, if considered originated, is also created. The Mu‘tazila hold either that such speech is not voluntary or that it is excepted from the general rule. The commentator then extends the inquiry to dumb animals, citing sources, but notes that the debate has little benefit for them. The second part addresses an objection to the conditional phrase 'if there is no obstacle' (in lam yakun hunaka mani‘). The objector argues that the conditional is superfluous (mustadraka) because, on Sunni principles, power (qudra) always accompanies the act, so once God creates power in the servant, no obstacle can prevent the act. The meaning is that God's custom is to create the act when there is no obstacle, even though power is simultaneous. Key Terms
الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conditional 'if there is no obstacle' implies that obstacles can prevent God's act, contradicting Sunni determinism.
Correction: The conditional refers to God's custom, not a limitation on His power. It is consistent with Sunni theology that God creates the act when the servant has power and no obstacle.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the commentator say the conditional 'if there is no obstacle' is not superfluous?
A) It is based on the creation of power in the servant.
B) It is based on God's custom of creating the act when no obstacle exists.
C) It is a logical necessity.
D) It is a concession to the Mu‘tazila.

Key Takeaway
The conditional clause 'if there is no obstacle' is not a redundancy but a reference to Allah's consistent custom of creating the act when the servant has power and no hindrance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to do good despite having the ability, do you blame Allah's decree or do you honestly examine whether an obstacle (like laziness or distraction) has blocked His custom? How will you remove that obstacle tomorrow?
?
The conditional 'if there is no obstacle' reveals that Allah's custom is to enable action when nothing hinders. In which area of your life have you assumed Allah would not help you, even though no real obstacle existed? What step will you take to act, trusting His custom?

Knowing Power Without Effect
Objection-and-answer: how can the servant's power be known without any causal effect?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school affirms the existence of human power (qudra) without causal effect, and the rational basis for this position.

Core Idea
Power is known by necessity, not by its effect.

This passage addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of human power. The objector argues that power can only be known through its effect; if the servant's power has no effect, how can it be known? The answer appeals to direct necessity (darura) that testifies to the existence of power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, independent of any causal outcome.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
The passage begins with 'قوله' indicating a quote from the text being commented on.
2
Identify the objection and its two parts
The objection has two parts: (1) against Ash'ari, who denies effect; (2) against the Jabriyya, who deny power entirely. Also note the point about inner intuition (wijdan) testifying only to consciousness and will, not to power's effect.
The objection tries to show that denying power's effect leads to denying power itself.
3
Understand the answer based on necessity
The answer states that necessity (darura) directly testifies to the existence of power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, even if it does not testify to any causal effect. This is a direct epistemic claim.
Necessity is a foundational rational intuition, not inference from effect.
4
Reflect on the distinction between power and effect
Contrast the objector's assumption (power known by effect) with the Ash'ari position (power known by necessity). This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The passage ends with 'كما سيصرح به', indicating further clarification later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهذا مذهب الشيخ قيل عليه : ثبوت القدر إنما يعلم بأثرها من الفعل ولما لم يكن لقدرة العبد أثر عند الأشعري فمن أين يعلم ثبوتها ويرد على الجبرية النافين لها مطلقاً، والوجدان إنما يشهد بثبوت الشعور والإرادة فينا وليسا بأثرين للقدرة والجواب أن الضرورة تشهد بوجود القدرة منضماً إلى الإرادة في الأفعال الاختيارية دون غيرها وإن لم تشهد بتأثيرها أصلاً، كما سيصرح به .
His saying: 'And this is the doctrine of the Shaykh.' It was objected: The affirmation of power is known only through its effect in the act. Since the servant's power has no effect according to al-Ash‘ari, how is its affirmation known? And it also refutes the Jabriyya who negate it absolutely. Inner intuition only testifies to the existence of consciousness and will in us, and these are not effects of power. The answer is that necessity (darura) testifies to the existence of power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, even if it does not testify to its effect at all, as will be stated explicitly.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the Ash‘ari doctrine that the servant's power has no causal effect. The objection states that power (qudra) is known only through its effect in the act; if al-Ash‘ari denies any effect, how can he affirm the existence of such a power? The objection also applies to the Jabriyya who deny the servant's power altogether. Moreover, inner intuition (wijdan) testifies to consciousness (shu‘ur) and will (irada), which are not effects of power. The answer is that necessity (darura) itself testifies to the existence of power conjoined with will in voluntary acts, even though it does not testify to any causal effect. The power is known directly by necessity, not inferred from an effect. The commentator notes that this will be clarified further.

Key Terms
القدرة
الأثر
الضرورة
الوجدان
الجبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power must have a perceptible effect to be known.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer shows that power is known by direct necessity (darura) when conjoined with will in voluntary acts, independent of any observable effect.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, how is the servant's power known according to the Ash‘ari view?
A) Through its observable effect in the act.
B) Through necessity (darura) testifying to its existence conjoined with will.
C) Through inner intuition of consciousness and will.
D) Through scriptural evidence only.

Key Takeaway
The servant's power is known by necessity (darura) conjoined with will, not through any causal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your power is known by necessity and not by its effects, does that change how you view your daily successes and failures? Are you more prone to attribute success to your own ability or to Allah's grace? Commit to one intentional act today that acknowledges Allah as the sole creator of effects.
?
The passage distinguishes between the servant's power and its effect. Where in your life have you felt the tension between your effort and the outcome? How does knowing that power is real but causally inefficacious affect your trust in Allah over your own plan?
?
The objector thought power is known only by its effect. Are there areas in your life where you doubt your faith because you don't see immediate effects? How can the testimony of necessity (darura) reassure you about the reality of your iman even when you don't feel it?

Distinguishing Mu'tazila from Philosophers on Human Power
Commentary on the phrase 'most of them' and distinction between Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle differences between the Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding the creation and operation of human power, and the commentator's preferred interpretation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and philosophers agree on God creating power, but differ on how it relates to the act.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif examines two glosses: first, the qualification 'most of them' in the original text, and second, the phrase 'without necessitation but by choice.' The commentator explores the apparent similarity between Mu'tazila and philosophers on human power and proposes several ways to distinguish them, ultimately favoring a view based on direct vs. mediated creation and choice vs. necessity. This lesson unpacks that nuanced discussion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first gloss about 'most of them'
Read the opening lines where the commentator clarifies that 'most of them' excludes al-Najjar and possibly Abu al-Husayn. Note that this shows internal diversity among the Mu'tazila.
2
Grasp the initial attempt to distinguish
Next, the commentator notes the apparent lack of difference and proposes an initial distinction: Mu'tazila say act by choice, philosophers by necessity. Read this part carefully.
This is just a starting point.
3
Learn why the initial attempt is incomplete
The commentator then argues that the initial distinction fails because when all conditions are complete, necessity arises for both. Understand the criticism.
This pushes the discussion deeper.
4
Explore the two alternative possibilities
Two more refined possibilities are presented. The first concerns whether the essence of power necessitates the act or only the relation. The second sees the servant's power as an instrument for God's power. Identify both.
5
Recognize the commentator's preferred view
Finally, the commentator favors a view based on direct vs. mediated creation and choice vs. necessity. This is supported by references to al-Amidi and others.
This is the conclusion of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي أكثرهم احتراز عن النجار كما سيظهر بل عن أبي الحسين أيضاً كما يقتضيه سياق كلام المصنف وإن كان محل بحث . قوله : (بلا إيجاب بل (باختبار لا خفاء أنه لا يظهر من المتن فرق بين مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء لأن المعتزلة قائلون بأن الله تعالى خالق القوى والقدر فأشار الشارح إلى الفرق بينهما بأن وقوع الفعل عند المعتزلة على سبيل الاختيار، وعند الحكماء على سبيل الوجوب لكنه غير تام لأن عدم الإيجاب، إنما هو بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة. وأما مع تمام الشرائط من الإرادة وغيرها فليس إلا الوجوب، اللهم إلا أن يقال : مذهب المعتزلة أن صدور الفعل عن المختار ولو بعد تمام الشرائط على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب بناء على كفاية الرجحان في الوقوع وإن كان مردوداً عند المحققين كما سبق، وهاهنا احتمالان آخران أحدهما أن يقال : ذات القدرة لا توجب الفعل عند المعتزلة إنما الإيجاب من التعلق الذي يجوز أن يكون بدله تعلق آخر وعند الحكيم ذات القدرة توجب التعلق المخصوص الموجب للفعل بأن تكون القدرة المؤثرة عندهم مع الفعل، كما أن القدرة الكاسبة عندنا كذلك والاحتمال الثاني أن يريد الحكماء بتعلق قدرة الله تعالى بقدرة العبد تعلقها بها من حيث تأثيرها في الفعل حتى تكون قدرة العبد كالآلة لقدرة الله تعالى وهي توجب الفعل بواسطتها، وعلى هذا يحصل الفرق بين مذهب الحكماء والمعتزلة ويندفع الاعتراض على القاضي بأنه لا معنى لنفي كون قدرة العبد متعلقة بقدرة الله تعالى لكنه خلاف ما اشتهر عن من إثبات القدرة المؤثرة للعبد بالنسبة إلى فعله الاختياري كما يفهم من الضابطة المذكورة أيضاً ويدل عليه تنصيص الآمدي فى أبكار الأفكار على أن فعل العبد عند إمام الحرمين واقع بقدرته مع تصريح المصنف بأنه موافق للحكماء في المذهب فالأظهر أن الفرق بين المذهبين إنما هو باعتبار أن خلق الله تعالى قدرة العبد بلا واسطة وبالاختيار وعند الفلاسفة بها وبالإيجاب .
His saying: 'Meaning most of them' is a qualification excluding al-Najjar, as will appear, and also excluding Abu al-Husayn as the context of the author's words requires, although it is debatable. His saying: 'Without necessitation but by choice.' It is not hidden that there does not appear from the text a difference between the doctrine of the Mu‘tazila and the philosophers, because the Mu‘tazila say that God is the creator of powers and abilities. So the commentator indicated the difference between them: that for the Mu‘tazila the occurrence of the act is by choice, and for the philosophers it is by necessity. But this is not complete, because the absence of necessitation is only with respect to the power itself. When all conditions—will and others—are complete, it is nothing but necessity. Unless it is said: The doctrine of the Mu‘tazila is that the act occurs from the agent even after the completion of conditions in the mode of possibility (sihha) rather than necessity, based on the sufficiency of preponderance (rujhan) for occurrence, even though this is refuted by the verifiers as previously. And here there are two other possibilities. One: That the essence of power does not necessitate the act according to the Mu‘tazila; rather the necessitation comes from the relation (ta‘alluq) which could have another relation instead. According to the philosopher, the essence of power necessitates the specific relation that necessitates the act, so that power is effective with the act just as the 'acquiring' (kasiba) power is with us. The second possibility: The philosophers mean by the relation of God's power to the servant's power that it relates to it in terms of its effect in the act, so that the servant's power is like an instrument for God's power, and it necessitates the act through it. In this way the difference between the philosophers and Mu‘tazila is obtained and the objection against al-Qadi is repelled, namely that there is no meaning to denying that the servant's power is related to God's power. But this is contrary to what is well-known from Him concerning affirmation of the servant's effective power with respect to his voluntary act, as understood from the aforementioned rule. And it is supported by the specification of al-Amidi in Abkar al-Afkar that the act of the servant according to Imam al-Haramayn occurs by his power, with the author's explicit statement that he agrees with the philosophers in doctrine. So the most apparent is that the difference between the two schools is only that God's creation of the servant's power is without mediation and by choice, while according to the philosophers it is with mediation and by necessity.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two glosses from the original text. The first gloss clarifies that 'most of them' (aktharihim) excludes al-Najjar and possibly Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu‘tazila, indicating that not all Mu‘tazila held the same view. The second gloss addresses the phrase 'without necessitation but by choice' (bila ijab bal bi-ikhtiyar). The commentator notes that the original text does not explicitly distinguish between the Mu‘tazila and the philosophers (hukama'), since both affirm that God creates powers. The commentator initially suggests the difference is that for the Mu‘tazila acts occur by choice, for philosophers by necessity. But he finds this incomplete because necessity still follows from complete conditions. He then presents two alternative possibilities. First, for the Mu‘tazila the essence of power does not necessitate the act; the necessitation is from the relation, which could be otherwise, while for a philosopher the essence of power necessitates a specific relation. Second, the philosophers may conceive God's power relating to the servant's power as an instrument, making the servant's power an instrument of God's power. The commentator prefers that the difference lies in whether God's creation of power is direct and by choice (as in the Mu‘tazila view) or mediated and by necessity (as in the philosophers' view). He supports this with references to al-Amidi and the author's agreement with Imam al-Haramayn.

Misconception: Some conflate the Mu'tazila and philosophers on causality, thinking they hold identical views on human power.
Correction: The commentator shows that they differ in whether God's creation of power is direct and by choice (Mu'tazila) or mediated and by necessity (philosophers).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the most apparent difference between the Mu'tazila and the philosophers regarding the servant's power?
A) The Mu'tazila deny the servant's power; philosophers affirm it.
B) The Mu'tazila say God creates power directly and by choice; philosophers say it is mediated and by necessity.
C) The Mu'tazila affirm necessitation; philosophers affirm choice.
D) The Mu'tazila say the servant's power has no effect; philosophers say it has effect.

Key Takeaway
The most apparent difference is that Mu'tazila affirm direct creation by choice, while philosophers affirm mediated creation by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or struggle to do good, do you sense that your ability is directly gifted from Allah at that moment, or do you feel it as a natural capacity you own? This lesson distinguishes between God creating power directly (Mu'tazila) or through mediation (philosophers). Which description matches your lived experience? And how would your reliance on Allah change if you deeply believed that your very power to choose is created by Him at every instant?
?
Think of a moment when you felt proud of a right action you performed—perhaps helping someone or avoiding sin. Did you unconsciously credit yourself? The scholars in this lesson all affirm that God creates power, yet they differ on how that power relates to your act. What would it mean for your humility and gratitude to never take credit for your own ability, but to see it as a direct gift from Allah each time? Are you ready to sincerely repent for any pride in your own 'power'?

Positions of al-Usaadhi and al-Qadi on Two Powers
Commentary on the third group's subdivision: two views on how two powers cooperate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two different ways early theologians explained how God's power and human power both contribute to a single act.

Core Idea
Two theologians agreed both powers are involved but differed on how they relate.

This passage explores a key disagreement within the group that affirmed both divine and human powers contribute to human actions. Al-Usaadhi and al-Qadi offer two distinct models: one where both powers directly cause the act's essence, the other where God causes the essence and the servant causes its moral quality. Understanding these positions helps clarify later debates on predestination and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation together. Notice the structure of the disagreement: both agreed that both powers are involved, then they differed.
2
Identify al-Usaadhi's position
Al-Usaadhi held that the sum of the two powers (مجموع القدرتين) relates to the act itself, allowing two agents to produce a single effect.
Key phrase: 'بمجموع القدرتين على أن يتعلقا جميعاً بالفعل'.
3
Identify al-Qadi's position
Al-Qadi distinguished between the act's essence (أصل الفعل) attributed to God's power, and its moral attribute (الصفة) like obedience/disobedience attributed to the servant's power.
Key phrase: 'على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بأصل الفعل وقدرة العبد بصفته'.
4
Analyze the example
The example of slapping an orphan illustrates the division: the physical slap is from God, but its moral quality (discipline or harm) is from the servant.
This example shows how al-Qadi's model preserves human responsibility for moral choices.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it attempts to reconcile divine omnipotence with human accountability for sin and obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معاً ثم اختلفوا (فقال الأستاذ بمجموع القدرتين على أن يتعلقا جميعاً بالفعل) نفسه، وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد ( وقال القاضي : على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بأصل الفعل وقدرة العبد ) بصفته أعني بكونه طاعة ومعصية) إلى غير ذلك من الأوصاف التي لا توصف بها أفعاله تعالى ( كما في لطم اليتيم تأديباً أو إيذاء) فإن ذات اللطم واقعة بقدرة الله وتأثيره وكونه طاعة على الأول ومعصية على الثاني بقدرة
Then together they disagreed. Al-Usaadhi said: By the sum of the two powers, that both relate to the act itself, and he allowed the joining of two agents on one effect. Al-Qadi said: That God's power relates to the essence of the act and the servant's power to its attribute—meaning its being obedience or disobedience—and to other attributes that are not attributed to God's acts, as in the case of slapping an orphan: disciplining or harming. For the essence of the slap occurs by God's power and His effect, while its being obedience on the first [account] and disobedience on the second [occurs] by the servant's power.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the subdivisions within the third group (those who say acts occur by both powers). After agreeing that both powers are involved, they differed. Al-Usaadhi (al-Ustadh) held that the two powers together (majmu‘ al-qudratayn) relate to the same act itself, allowing two agents to produce a single effect. Al-Qadi (al-Qadi) proposed a different view: God's power relates to the essence (asl) of the act, while the servant's power relates to its attribute (sifa), specifically its being obedience or disobedience, or other moral qualities that are not attributed to God. The text gives the example of slapping an orphan: if done as discipline, it is obedience; if done to harm, it is disobedience. The essence of the slap (the physical act) is brought about by God's power, while the moral quality (being obedience or disobedience) is ascribed to the servant's power.

Key Terms

القاضي
مجموع القدرتين
اجتماع المؤثرين
أصل الفعل
الصفة
طاعة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Usaadhi and al-Qadi both affirm the servant's causal power equally, but al-Qadi restricts the servant's power to moral attributes, not the act's existence.
Correction: Al-Qadi explicitly states that God's power relates to the essence of the act, while the servant's power relates to its attribute (being obedience or disobedience). Thus the servant does not cause the act's existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi's position, what does the servant's power produce?
A) The entire act, including its essence.
B) The act's moral attribute (e.g., being obedience or disobedience).
C) The act's essence only.
D) Nothing; the servant's power has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Usaadhi and al-Qadi represent two Islamic theological models for reconciling divine omnipotence and human responsibility: one with joint causation of the act's essence, the other with divided causation of essence and moral quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you instinctively blame your environment or your own free choice? How does al-Qadi's division (God creates the act's essence, you create its moral quality) force you to reconsider who is truly responsible for the sin—and how might that change your repentance tonight?
?
Think of a recent action that could have been either obedience or disobedience depending on your intention (e.g., correcting a child, speaking to a colleague). According to this lesson, who determines whether that action is obedience or disobedience? Do you live as if that is true, or do you often avoid taking ownership of the moral 'label' on your actions? Commit to asking yourself before each action: 'What is my intention, and am I claiming the moral responsibility for it?'
?
Al-Usaadhi allowed two agents to produce one effect. If that is true, then both God and you are responsible for every act—God for its existence, you for its character. How does that make you feel about the times you thank God for a good deed? Are you honestly acknowledging your own contribution to the obedience, or do you subtly feel you deserve the credit? What would it look like to give God full credit while still owning your role?

Objection and Defense on Two Agents for One Effect
Objection to al-Usaadhi's view and a possible defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against al-Usaadhi's view on two agents producing one effect and a proposed defense that avoids the problem without contradicting his core doctrine.

Core Idea
Two agents can cooperate as partial causes without both being fully independent.

Al-Usaadhi's position that two agents (God and servant) can produce a single act faces a logical objection: two independent agents cannot have a single effect. This lesson presents the objection and a defense that reinterprets the agents' roles as partial causes or assistance-based independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get an overall sense of the content. Note the flow from statement to possible defense.
The passage is a commentary on a previous view.
2
Identify the implicit objection
The phrase 'it was said' hints at an objection to al-Usaadhi's view that two agents can produce one effect. Understand why this is problematic: if both are fully independent, a single effect would require two complete causes, which is impossible.
The objection is based on the principle of causality.
3
Understand the proposed defense
The defense suggests that the servant's power is not independent; it becomes effective only when joined with God's power, either as a partial cause or through divine assistance. This avoids the problem of two fully independent agents.
The terms 'juz' al-mu'aththir' and 'i'ana' are important here.
4
Note the commentator's caveat
The passage concludes by stating that the well-known view of al-Usaadhi's school is what the original commentator mentioned, implying that this defense may not represent his actual doctrine.
This shows scholarly caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجوز اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد قيل : لعل مراد الأستاذ أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير وإذا انضمت إليه قدرة الله تعالى صار المجموع مؤثراً في الفعل على أن كلاً منهما جزء المؤثر أو صارت قدرة العبد مستقلة بتوسط هذه الإعانة وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه لزوم اجتماع المؤثرين على أثر واحد لكن المشهور من مذهبه ما ذكره الشارح.
His saying: 'And he allowed the joining of two agents on one effect.' It was said: Perhaps al-Usaadhi meant that the servant's power is not independent in effecting, and when God's power is joined to it, the sum becomes effective in the act—in the sense that each is part of the effecting, or the servant's power becomes independent through this assistance. And on this basis, the necessary consequence of two agents on one effect does not arise against him. But the well-known view of his school is what the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a comment on al-Usaadhi's view that two agents can produce a single effect. An objection is noted: allowing two independent agents to produce one effect seems impossible. A possible defense (likely from another commentator) is suggested: perhaps al-Usaadhi did not mean that each power is fully independent. Instead, the servant's power might be non-independent in its effect; when combined with God's power, the totality becomes effective. In this case, each is a 'part of the effecting cause' (juz' al-mu'aththir), or the servant's power becomes independent through the assistance (bi-tawassut hadhihi al-i‘ana). Under this interpretation, the objection that two agents are acting on one effect does not apply. However, the commentator notes that the well-known (mashhur) doctrine of al-Usaadhi is what the original commentator (al-sharih) previously stated, implying that the defense may not represent his actual view.

Key Terms
اجتماع المؤثرين
أثر واحد
جزء المؤثر
الإعانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Usaadhi's view is clearly impossible because two independent agents cannot produce one effect.
Correction: The defense shows how it might be understood: the agents are not both fully independent; they cooperate as partial causes or through assistance, which avoids the logical problem.

Checkpoint
According to the possible defense mentioned, how can al-Usaadhi's view avoid the problem of two agents on one effect?
A) By denying that the servant's power has any effect.
B) By considering each power as a partial cause, together forming one effective cause.
C) By affirming that only God's power is effective.
D) By limiting the servant's power to the act's moral quality.

Key Takeaway
The defense of al-Usaadhi's view interprets the two agents as partial causes working together, avoiding the problem of two fully independent agents on one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
When you face a task that seems impossible with your own strength, do you truly believe that God's power can join with your partial effort to bring about the result? How would your prayer life and work ethic change if you fully embraced this partnership?

Moral Attributes as Relative: The Objection and Resolution
Commentary on the objection and resolution regarding moral attributes as relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the moral quality of acts (obedience/disobedience) is considered a relative attribute (amr i'tibari) rather than an existential effect of the servant's power, and how this preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Core Idea
Moral qualities are relative to the command, not effects of human power.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle point in al-Qadi's view: does the servant's power produce the moral quality of the act? An objection is raised that obedience and disobedience are merely relational concepts that follow from conformity or opposition to God's command. They are not real, existent attributes that could be caused by human power. The commentator then offers a resolution that allows us to speak of the act's moral quality in relation to the servant's power without making the servant a creator of anything. This clarifies the Ash'ari position on human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is stated and what the commentator offers as a possible resolution.
2
Identify the objection
Locate the line that says 'this attribute is a relative matter that follows the servant's act from its conformity or opposition.' Understand why this means it cannot be an effect of power.
An effect of power would be a created thing, but relative matters are not created.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila implication
The objection warns that if the attribute were existential and grounded in the servant's power, the servant would be a creator (mūjid) of something, which is a Mu'tazila view. This shows why Ash'aris reject that.
The commentator mentions 'some Mu'tazila' – this is a key doctrinal boundary.
4
Examine the resolution
The commentator says it is possible to attribute the act's moral quality to the servant's power based on its relation to the servant, even if it does not arise from the power. Distinguish this from causal production.
This resolution uses the concept of 'nisba' (relation) to avoid implying creation.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this preserves God's sole creative power while still allowing human acts to be described as obedience/disobedience. This is a classic Ash'ari solution to the problem of human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( أي بكونه طاعة ومعصية يرد عليه أن هذه الصفة أمر اعتباري يلزم فعل العبد من موافقته لما أمر الله تعالى به أو مخالفته له فلا وجه لجعله أثراً للقدرة كيف ولو قيل : بوجودية الصفة المذكورة واستنادها إلى قدرة العبد لكان العبد موجوداً لبعض الأشياء وهو مذهب بعض الاعتزال ويحتمل أن يقال كون الفعل طاعة أو معصية لما عرضه بالنسبة إلى محله نسبة إلى قدرة العبد وإن لم ينشأ منها .
His saying: 'meaning its being obedience and disobedience.' It is objected that this attribute is a relative (i‘tibari) matter that follows the servant's act from its conformity to what God commanded or its opposition to Him. So there is no justification for making it an effect of power. Moreover, if it were said that the mentioned attribute is existential and grounded in the servant's power, the servant would be an originator of some things—which is the doctrine of some Mu‘tazila. And it is possible to say that the act's being obedience or disobedience, due to its relation to its locus, is attributed to the servant's power even if it does not arise from it.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Qadi's view that the servant's power produces the attribute of the act (obedience/disobedience). An objection is raised: such moral attributes are merely relative (i‘tibari), arising from the act's conformity or opposition to God's command. Therefore, they are not real existential effects that could be attributed to a power. If one were to claim that these attributes are existential and dependent on the servant's power, that would imply the servant creates something—a view held by some Mu‘tazila (like the ones who affirm that the servant is a creator of some things). The commentator then offers a possible resolution: one can say that the act's being obedience or disobedience, due to its relation to its locus (the servant), is ascribed to the servant's power as a relative attribution, even though the attribute does not actually arise from the power in a causal sense. This preserves al-Qadi's language without committing to an existential effect.

Key Terms
طاعة
معصية
أمر اعتباري
موافقة
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might take al-Qadi's view literally as attributing a real created quality to the servant, thinking that the servant's power produces a real attribute of obedience or disobedience in the act.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that the moral quality is only a relative (i'tibari) attribution based on the act's relation to God's command, not an existential effect of the servant's power. The servant's power does not create any attribute; it only provides a basis for relational description.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why cannot the moral quality (obedience/disobedience) be an effect of the servant's power?
A) Because the servant's power has no effect at all.
B) Because moral quality is a relative matter following conformity or opposition to God's command.
C) Because only God can create moral qualities.
D) Because the act itself is not created by the servant.

Key Takeaway
The moral attributes of acts are relative (i'tibari) and not existential effects of the servant's power, thus avoiding the Mu'tazila view while affirming the act's relation to the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you think the 'disobedience' is a real thing God creates in you? Or do you see it as a relational label based on your choice to oppose His command? How does this understanding affect your sense of personal responsibility?
?
If moral attributes are merely relative and not existential, does that make your obedience less real to you? Or does it heighten the significance of your intention and alignment with God's command?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Are you tempted to excuse it by saying 'God created it in me'? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and attribution (nisba) challenge you to own your actions while still relying on God's grace?

The Third Argument: Servant's Act Is Compulsory
Al-Jurjani refutes independent agency via preponderator necessity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument from al-Jurjani that refutes the servant's independent creation of actions by showing that any act requires a preponderator, leading to either infinite regress or compulsion.

Core Idea
A truly independent act needs a preponderator that cannot come from the servant, so the act is compulsory.

In this third argument, al-Jurjani presents a logical refutation of the Muʻtazilite claim that the servant independently creates his own acts. The argument follows a chain: if the servant were independent, his act would need a preponderator to tip the balance between doing and not doing, but that preponderator cannot be chosen by the servant without leading to infinite regress. The result is that the act is necessary and compulsory, not voluntary in the claimed sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument, especially the conditional structure.
2
Identify the logical moves
The argument has four main premises: (1) independence requires ability to do and omit; (2) preferment needs a preponderator; (3) preponderator cannot be from servant; (4) with external preponderator, act becomes necessary. Trace each step.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the act is compulsory (idtirari). Consider how this counters the Muʻtazilite position. Note that this does not deny all human agency, just independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إن العبد لو كان موجداً لفعله بقدرته واختياره استقلالاً فلا بد أن يتمكن من فعله وتركه وإلا لم يكن قادراً عليه مستقلاً فيه وأن يتوقف ترجيح فعله على تركه على مرجح إذ لو لم يتوقف عليه كان صدور الفعل عنه مع جواز طرفيه وتساويهما اتفاقياً لا اختيارياً، ويلزم أيضاً أن لا يحتاج وقوع أحد الجائزين إلى سبب فينسد باب إثبات الصانع وذلك المرجح لا يكون منه أي من العبد باختياره وإلا لزم التسلسل لأنا ننقل الكلام إلى صدور ذلك المرجح عنه ويكون الفعل عنده أي عند ذلك المرجح واجباً أي واجب الصدور عنه بحيث يمتنع تخلفه عنه وإلا لم يكن الموجود أي ذلك المرجح المفروض تمام المرجح لأنه إذا لم يجب منه الفعل حينئذ جاز أن يوجد معه الفعل تارة ويعدم أخرى مع وجود ذلك المرجح فيهما فتخصيص أحد الوقتين بوجوده يحتاج إلى مرجح لما عرفت فلا يكون ما فرضناه مرجحاً تاماً هذا خلف، وإذا كان الفعل مع المرجح الذي ليس منه واجب الصدور عنه فيكون ذلك الفعل اضطرارياً لازماً لا اختيارياً بطريق الاستقلال كما زعموه.
The third face: If the servant were the creator of his act by his power and choice independently, then he must be able to do it and leave it, otherwise he would not be capable of it independently, and (he must) that the preferment of his act over its omission depend on a preponderator, because if it did not depend on it, the occurrence of the act from him while its two sides are possible and equal would be coincidental, not voluntary, and it would also follow that the occurrence of one of the two possibles would not need a cause, so the door of proving the Maker would be closed. And that preponderator cannot be from him, i.e., from the servant by his choice, otherwise infinite regress would follow, because we would transfer the discussion to the occurrence of that preponderator from him. And the act would be with it, i.e., with that preponderator, necessary, i.e., necessarily proceeding from him such that its non-occurrence is impossible, otherwise the existing (i.e., that supposed preponderator) would not be a complete preponderator, because if the act does not necessarily proceed from him then, it would be possible for the act to exist with him sometimes and not exist another time while that preponderator exists in both, so specifying one of the two times with its existence would need a preponderator, as you know, so what we supposed to be a preponderator would not be a complete preponderator; this is a contradiction. And when the act with the preponderator which is not from him is necessarily proceeding from him, then that act is compulsory, necessary, not voluntary independently, as they claimed.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the third argument) that refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the servant independently creates his own actions. The argument proceeds in a logical chain: First, if the servant were truly independent, he must be able to both perform and abstain from the act; otherwise, he is not truly capable. Second, since both possibilities are equal, the actual occurrence of the act over omission requires a preponderator (murajjih) that tips the scale. Without such a preponderator, the act would be coincidental, not voluntary, and moreover, any possible event would not need a cause, which would undermine the proof of a Creator. Third, that preponderator cannot itself be chosen by the servant (otherwise we ask why that choice occurred, leading to infinite regress). Fourth, with the preponderator (which is not from the servant), the act becomes necessary (wajib) — it must occur and cannot be otherwise. If it were not necessary, the preponderator would not be fully sufficient, and a further preponderator would be needed, again leading to regress. The conclusion is that the servant's act is compulsory (idtirari), not voluntary in the independent sense claimed by the Mu'tazila. This argument is an implicit concession that the servant does not create his own act; rather, God is the ultimate agent.

Key Terms
المرجح
التسلسل
الاضطرار
الاستقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that humans have no power or choice at all.
Correction: It only refutes the claim of independent creation (istidlal). The Ashʿarī position affirms that humans acquire (kasb) their actions, and God creates them. The argument shows that the act is not independently voluntary, but does not negate all human agency.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the third argument?
A) The servant is the independent creator of his act.
B) The servant's act is compulsory and not independent.
C) God's act is also compulsory.
D) Infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that a truly independent act is impossible because it would require a preponderator that either leads to infinite regress or makes the act compulsory, thus the servant's act is not independently voluntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your every action is created by God and that you cannot independently originate a single deed, how does that reshape your feelings of pride in your accomplishments? Can you still take credit for anything without falling into the very claim of independence this argument refutes?
?
Consider a recent moment when you felt you had full control over a choice. This argument says that even that choice required an external preponderator. Does that make you feel more dependent on God? Or does it create a sense of helplessness? How do you reconcile your daily experience of choosing with this theological truth?
?
When you sin, if you accept that the act was compulsory given a preponderator not from you, what is your responsibility? This argument removes independent agency but does not remove accountability. How can you still be guilty for a compulsory act? What does this demand of your repentance and reliance on God?

Understanding the Scope of the Preponderance Argument
The author limits the preponderance argument to the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument requiring a preponderant for voluntary action only refutes the Mu'tazila, not the Ash'aris.

Core Idea
The preponderance argument only targets those who insist on a necessary preponderant.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial limitation of a common argument in kalām. He explains that the argument about the necessity of a preponderant (murajjih) for voluntary actions only works against the Mu'tazila who hold that view. For the Ash'aris, divine choice itself suffices as a preponderant. This lesson will unpack that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: murajjih (preponderant), tarjih (preponderance), da'i (motive).
The passage is dense with technical terms; understanding them is essential.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts the Mu'tazila position (requiring a preponderant and making the action obligatory) with the Ash'ari position (allowing preponderance through mere choice without a motive).
The author names Abu al-Husayn as a representative of the Mu'tazila view.
3
Understand the logical consequence
The passage argues that if there is no requirement for a preponderant, then an action without a preponderant or motive is not necessarily accidental or without an efficient cause. The choice itself acts as the preponderant.
This directly addresses the misconception that Ash'aris deny causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال أي الوجه الثالث إنما يصلح لزاماً للمعتزلة القائلين بوجوب المرجح في الفعل الاختياري وكون الفعل معه واجباً كأبي الحسين وأتباعه، وإلا فعلى رأينا يجوز الترجيح بمجرد تعلق الاختيار بأحد طرفي المقدور من غير داع إلى ذلك الطرف كما مر، فلا يلزم من كون الفعل بلا مرجح وداع كونه اتفاقياً واقعاً بلا مؤثر، وحديث الترجيح بلا مرجح قد تكرر مراراً بما أغنانا عن إعادته.
The argument (i.e., the third aspect) is only valid as a necessary consequence for the Mu'tazila who assert the necessity of the preponderant (al-murajjih) in voluntary action, and that the action with it is obligatory, like Abu al-Husayn and his followers. Otherwise, according to our view, preponderance is permissible by mere attachment of choice to one of the two sides of the possible [action], without a motive to that side, as has passed. So it does not follow from the action being without a preponderant and motive that it is accidental, occurring without an efficient cause. And the discussion of preponderance without a preponderant has been repeated many times, sufficing us from repeating it.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a definitive statement about the scope of an argument concerning preponderance (tarjih) in voluntary actions. The author clarifies that the argument requiring a preponderant factor for any voluntary action only applies as a necessary consequence against the Mu'tazila, specifically those like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri who insist that a preponderant is obligatory. For the Ash'aris, however, preponderance can occur simply through the attachment of choice to one of two equally possible alternatives, without any external motive. Therefore, an action lacking a preponderant or motive is not necessarily accidental or without an efficient cause, because the agent's choice itself suffices. The author notes that this point has been covered sufficiently elsewhere.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny any role for motives or preponderants; in fact, they affirm choice as sufficient.
Correction: Ash'aris do not deny the concept of preponderance; they hold that the agent's free choice itself is the sufficient preponderant, without requiring an external motive.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, against whom does the argument about the necessity of a preponderant (murajjih) hold?
A) Against all Muslims
B) Only against the Mu'tazila who require a preponderant
C) Only against the Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The argument that voluntary actions require a preponderant only applies to the Mu'tazila, not to the Ash'aris who affirm choice as the sufficient preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice, do you often feel compelled by external pressures or motives, or do you recognize that your own choice is sufficient? How does this affect your sense of responsibility before Allah? Commit to one decision this week where you act solely based on your sincere choice, trusting that it is valid as a preponderant.
?
The Ash'ari view teaches that Allah's choice is sufficient without any external motive. Do you truly trust that Allah's decree is wise even when you cannot see a reason for your trials? This week, identify a situation where you struggled to see wisdom and consciously affirm that His choice alone is enough.
?
The argument only applies to those who insist on a necessary preponderant. Are there areas in your life where you demand a clear reason or sign before acting, rather than relying on your deliberate choice and trust in Allah? Repent from any excessive rationalism that undermines your reliance on Allah's will.

Abu al-Husayn's Claim of Necessary Knowledge
Abu al-Husayn argues for necessary knowledge of human agency via the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Husayn's claim that knowledge of human agency is necessary and his argument from the distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions.

Core Idea
What feels obvious may not be necessary knowledge.

Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a Mu'tazili theologian, argued that it is self-evident that humans create their own voluntary actions. He based this on the undeniable distinction we find between voluntary movements like climbing a minaret and involuntary ones like falling. This lesson presents his argument and sets the stage for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp Abu al-Husayn's argument.
Notice that he uses a phenomenological distinction.
2
Identify the distinction
Find the examples of voluntary and involuntary movements. Understand why Abu al-Husayn thinks this distinction is universally recognized.
3
Recognize the claim of necessity
Note that Abu al-Husayn goes further, calling denial of this distinction 'sophistry' (safsat). Reflect on why he considers this knowledge necessary.
This is a strong claim – he equates denial with irrationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة القائلون بأن العبد موجد لأفعاله الاختيارية صاروا فريقين فأبو الحسين ومن تبعه في إيجاد العبد لفعله الضرورة أي يزعم أن العلم بذلك ضروري لا حاجة به إلى استدلال وبيان ذلك أن كل أحد يجد من نفسه التفرقة بين حركتي المختار والمرتعش والصاعد باختياره إلى المنارة والهاوي أي الساقط منها ويعلم أن الأولين من هذين القسمتين يستندان إلى دواعيه واختياره وأنه لولا تلك الدواعي والاختيار لم يصدر عنه شيء منهما بخلاف الأخيرين إذ لا مدخل في شيء منهما لإرادته ودواعيه ويجعل أبو الحسين إنكاره أي إنكار كون العبد موجداً لأفعاله الاختيارية سفسطة مصادمة للضرورة والجواب إن الفرق بين الأفعال الاختيارية وغير الاختيارية ضروري لكنه عائد إلى وجود القدرة منضمة إلى الاختيار في الأولى وعدمها في الثانية لا إلى تأثيرها في الاختيارية وعدمه أي عدم تأثيرها في غيرها وذلك أنه لا يلزم من دوران الشيء كالفعل الاختياري مع غيره كالقدرة والدواعي وجوداً وعدماً وجوب الدوران لجواز أن يكون الدوران اتفاقياً ولا يلزم أيضاً من وجوب الدوران على تقدير ثبوته العلية أي كون المدار علة للدائر ولا من العلية إن سلم ثبوتها الاستقلال بالعلية لجواز أن يكون المدار جزءاً أخيراً من العلة ثم يبطل ما قاله أبو الحسين أمران الأول إن من كان الأمة كانوا بين منكرين لإيجاد العبد فعله ومعترفين به مثبتين له بالدليل فالموافق والمخالف له اتفقوا على نفي الضرورة عن هذا المتنازع فيه أما نفي المخالف فظاهر وأما نفي الموافق فلاستدلاله عليه فكيف يسمع منه نسبة كل العقلاء إلى إنكار الضرورة فيه الأمر الثاني إن كل سليم العقل إذا اعتبر حال نفسه
The Mu'tazila who say that the servant is the creator of his voluntary actions became two groups. Abu al-Husayn and those who followed him [affirmed] that the servant's creation of his action is necessary [knowledge], i.e., he claims that knowledge of that is necessary, needing no proof. The explanation of that is that everyone finds in himself the distinction between the movements of the voluntary agent and the tremulous, and the one ascending by his choice to the minaret and the one falling, i.e., descending from it. He knows that the first two of these two categories are based on his motives and choice, and that without those motives and choice nothing of them would have occurred from him, unlike the last two, since there is no role in either of them for his will and motives. And Abu al-Husayn considers its denial — i.e., denial that the servant is the creator of his voluntary actions — to be sophistry, conflicting with the necessary. The answer: The distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions is necessary, but it goes back to the existence of power conjoined with choice in the first and its absence in the second, not to its efficacy in the voluntary [actions] and its lack of efficacy in the others. That is because from the correlation of a thing — like voluntary action — with another — like power and motives — in existence and non-existence, necessary correlation does not follow, because it is possible that the correlation is coincidental. Nor does necessary correlation, if established, entail causality — i.e., that the correlated is a cause of the correlate. Nor does causality, if conceded, entail independence in causality, because it is possible that the correlated is a final part of the cause. Then he refutes what Abu al-Husayn said with two things. The first: The people of the community were divided between deniers of the servant's creation of his act and those who affirm it, establishing it by proof. So both those who agree and those who disagree with him agreed on negating the necessity of this disputed matter. As for the denier's negation, it is apparent; as for the affirmer's negation, it is because he uses proof for it. So how can he be heard to attribute to all rational people the denial of necessity in it? The second: Every sound-minded person, when he considers his own state...
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that it is self-evident (daruri) that the human servant creates his own voluntary actions. The author first reproduces Abu al-Husayn's argument: everyone distinguishes between voluntary and involuntary movements (e.g., voluntary ascent vs. falling), and this distinction shows that voluntary actions depend on motives and choice, implying that the agent is the creator. Abu al-Husayn goes so far as to call denial of this 'sophistry' that contradicts necessary knowledge. The answer then follows. The author concedes that the distinction is necessary, but locates it in the presence or absence of power (qudra) conjoined with choice, not in the efficacy of that power. Correlation between voluntary action and power+motive does not prove causation, nor does correlation imply independence. The author then gives two specific refutations. The first appeals to historical consensus: both those who deny and those who affirm human agency agree that this is not a matter of necessary knowledge, since even the affirmers prove it by argument. The second refutation is cut off, but it likely continues that self-perception does not yield necessary knowledge of creation.

Key Terms
الضرورة
الاختياري
المرتعش
الصاعد
الهاوي
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions is the proof that the agent creates the action.
Correction: Abu al-Husayn uses the distinction as evidence, but the refutation will show that it only proves the presence of power with choice, not its efficacy in creation.

Checkpoint
What does Abu al-Husayn claim about knowledge of the servant's creation of his actions?
A) It is acquired through proof
B) It is necessary (daruri) and needs no proof
C) It is based on scriptural evidence
D) It is impossible to know

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn claims that knowledge of human agency is necessary, based on a universal felt distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your most private moments, when you commit a sin that you deeply regret, do you truly feel that you were the one who chose and created that action? Or do you catch yourself shifting the blame to your circumstances, your upbringing, or even to Allah's decree? Abu al-Husayn says the distinction between voluntary and involuntary is so universally obvious that denying it is sophistry. But is your own conscience as clear as you pretend? Take one specific sin you have rationalized and ask yourself: can I honestly maintain that I was compelled, or was it my choice? What does your answer reveal about your faith in your own accountability before Allah? This week, before you act, pause and say to yourself: 'This is my choice, and I will answer for it.'
?
You know the difference between being pushed and walking on your own—Abu al-Husayn says this knowledge is built into you. But have you ever used that very distinction to excuse yourself, claiming you were 'forced' by your desires or passions? If the tremulous person is not blamed for shaking, then why do you blame others for their 'involuntary' weaknesses? Look at a relationship where you have held a grudge: did that person truly have no choice, or did they choose? And what about your own choices in that conflict? Are you ready to accept that your voluntary acts are truly yours—both the good and the bad—and that no one else can carry that burden for you? Commit to taking full ownership of one decision today, without externalizing responsibility.

Refuting the Claim: Correlation is Not Causation
The author refutes Abu al-Husayn's claim by reinterpreting the distinction and providing logical and historical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of Abu al-Husayn's argument by showing that correlation between action and motive does not prove causation or independence.

Core Idea
Correlation does not prove causation, especially in divine action.

The author responds to Abu al-Husayn by agreeing that the distinction is necessary but reinterpreting it. The real difference is the presence of power with choice, not the efficacy of that power. He then argues that correlation does not imply causality, and even if it did, it does not imply independence. Finally, he offers a historical argument that even those who agree with Abu al-Husayn's conclusion do not consider it necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer and first refutation
Read from 'والجواب' to the end of the first refutation. Notice how the author first reinterprets the distinction, then gives logical steps, then a historical argument.
2
Understand the reinterpretation
The author agrees the distinction is necessary, but says it indicates the presence of power (qudra) conjoined with choice in voluntary actions, not its efficacy.
This is subtle: the voluntary/involuntary distinction is real, but it doesn't prove the agent is a creator.
3
Follow the logical chain
The author makes three points: (1) correlation (dawaran) between action and power does not prove causation; (2) even if causation, it does not prove independence; (3) the power could be merely the final part of the cause.
This breaks the inference from distinction to creation.
4
Consider the historical argument
The first refutation (al-amr al-awwal) points out that both those who affirm and deny human agency agree that this is not necessary knowledge, because even the affirmers use proof.
This undercuts Abu al-Husayn's claim that it is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
