The Mu'tazila who say that the servant is the creator of his voluntary actions became two groups. Abu al-Husayn and those who followed him [affirmed] that the servant's creation of his action is necessary [knowledge], i.e., he claims that knowledge of that is necessary, needing no proof. The explanation of that is that everyone finds in himself the distinction between the movements of the voluntary agent and the tremulous, and the one ascending by his choice to the minaret and the one falling, i.e., descending from it. He knows that the first two of these two categories are based on his motives and choice, and that without those motives and choice nothing of them would have occurred from him, unlike the last two, since there is no role in either of them for his will and motives. And Abu al-Husayn considers its denial — i.e., denial that the servant is the creator of his voluntary actions — to be sophistry, conflicting with the necessary. The answer: The distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions is necessary, but it goes back to the existence of power conjoined with choice in the first and its absence in the second, not to its efficacy in the voluntary [actions] and its lack of efficacy in the others. That is because from the correlation of a thing — like voluntary action — with another — like power and motives — in existence and non-existence, necessary correlation does not follow, because it is possible that the correlation is coincidental. Nor does necessary correlation, if established, entail causality — i.e., that the correlated is a cause of the correlate. Nor does causality, if conceded, entail independence in causality, because it is possible that the correlated is a final part of the cause. Then he refutes what Abu al-Husayn said with two things. The first: The people of the community were divided between deniers of the servant's creation of his act and those who affirm it, establishing it by proof. So both those who agree and those who disagree with him agreed on negating the necessity of this disputed matter. As for the denier's negation, it is apparent; as for the affirmer's negation, it is because he uses proof for it. So how can he be heard to attribute to all rational people the denial of necessity in it? The second: Every sound-minded person, when he considers his own state...
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that it is self-evident (daruri) that the human servant creates his own voluntary actions. The author first reproduces Abu al-Husayn's argument: everyone distinguishes between voluntary and involuntary movements (e.g., voluntary ascent vs. falling), and this distinction shows that voluntary actions depend on motives and choice, implying that the agent is the creator. Abu al-Husayn goes so far as to call denial of this 'sophistry' that contradicts necessary knowledge. The answer then follows. The author concedes that the distinction is necessary, but locates it in the presence or absence of power (qudra) conjoined with choice, not in the efficacy of that power. Correlation between voluntary action and power+motive does not prove causation, nor does correlation imply independence. The author then gives two specific refutations. The first appeals to historical consensus: both those who deny and those who affirm human agency agree that this is not a matter of necessary knowledge, since even the affirmers prove it by argument. The second refutation is cut off, but it likely continues that self-perception does not yield necessary knowledge of creation.

Key Terms
القدرة
الدواعي
الدوران
العلية
الاستقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction between voluntary and involuntary is itself the proof that the agent is the creator.
Correction: The author shows the distinction only indicates the presence of power, not its efficacy. Correlation does not prove causation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the necessary distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions actually indicate?
A) That the agent is an independent creator
B) The presence of power conjoined with choice in the first and its absence in the second
C) That motives are the cause of the action
D) That correlation implies causation

Key Takeaway
Correlation between action and power does not prove that the agent is an independent creator; it only shows the presence of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something in your life always follows another thing (e.g., your effort leads to success), that it must be the cause? Where might this assumption hide a subtle denial of God's ultimate power? This week, catch yourself thinking 'I made this happen' and replace it with 'God allowed this through me.'
?
The passage shows that even those who affirm human creation of acts use proof—it isn't self-evident. Is there a belief you hold about God's decree that you've never critically examined, simply because it 'feels obvious'? What would it cost you to test that belief against scripture and reasoning?
?
If your power to act is only 'a final part of the cause' and not independent, does that change how you approach your daily decisions? Do you truly rely on God's power in your choices, or do you act as if you are the independent cause? Pause now and repent of any autonomy you have claimed for yourself.

Two Premises on Divine Will and Human Agency
Sanusi lays two divine-will premises, deduces that the servant is not creator, and rebuts a distinction objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two premises about divine will logically entail that the human servant is not the independent creator of his actions, and how the objection about efficacy vs. independence is answered.

Core Idea
Two premises about God's will prove humans are not independent creators of their actions.

Sanusi presents a key argument from earlier Ash'ari authorities (al-Juwayni) that the Mu'tazilite Abu al-Husayn al-Basri implicitly agreed with the Ash'ari position on human action, but hid his agreement. The argument rests on two premises about God's will. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author moves from premises to conclusion to objection and answer.
2
Identify the two premises and the conclusion
Find the two premises: (1) God's will for a thing does not depend on His willing that will, and (2) with decisive will and conditions, the object of will necessarily occurs. Then see how the author concludes that the servant cannot be the creator.
These premises are about divine will, not human will.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
Read the objection that acknowledging dependence on motive only negates independence, not efficacy. Then see the author's answer: the purpose is to negate independence, which is the Mu'tazilite claim.
This distinction between efficacy and independence is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمات علم أن إرادته للشيء لا تتوقف على إرادته تلك الإرادة بل تحصل له تلك الإرادة سواء أرادها أو لم يردها، وعلم أيضاً أنه مع الإرادة الجازمة الجامعة للشرائط وارتفاع الموانع يحصل المراد وبدونها لا يحصل ويلزم منها أي من التي علمها بوجدانه أنه لا إرادة منه ولا حصول الفعل عقيبها منها فكيف يدعي الضرورة في خلافه. قال الإمام في نهاية العقول والعجب من أبي الحسين أنه خالف أصحابه في قولهم القادر على الضدين لا يتوقف فعله لأحدهما دون الآخر على مرجح وزعم أن العلم بتوقف ذلك أي فعله لأحدهما دون الآخر على الداعي إلى أحدهما ضروري وزعم أن حصول الفعل عقيب الداعي وأجيب ولزمه للاعتراف بهاتين المقدمتين عدم كون العبد موجداً لفعله كما هو مذهبنا ثم بالغ في كون العبد موجداً وزاد على كل من تقدمه حتى ادعى العلم الضروري بذلك قال الإمام وعندي أن أبا الحسين ما كان ممن لا يعلم أن القول بتينك المقدمتين يبطل مذهب الاعتزال يعني في مسألة خلق الأعمال وما يبتني عليها لكنه لما أبطل الأصول التي عليها مدار الاعتزال خاف من تنبه أصحابه لرجوعه عن مذهبهم فلبس الأمر عليهم بمبالغته في ادعاء العلم الضروري بذلك وإلا فهذا التناقض أظهر من أن يخفى على المبتدئ فضلا عمن بلغ درجة أبي الحسين في التحقيق والتدقيق فظهر أنه في هذه المسألة جرى على مذهبنا لا يقال الاعتراف بتوقف صدور الفعل عن القادر على الداعي ووجوب حصوله عند حصوله لا ينافي القول بأن القدرة الحادثة مؤثرة في وجود الفعل وإنما ينافي استقلاله بالفاعلية على سبيل التفويض إليه بالكلية، وهو إنما ادعى العلم الضروري في الأول أي التأثير لا في الثاني أي الاستقلال حتى يتجه ما وردتموه عليه لأنا نقول غرضنا في هذا المقام سلب الاستقلال الذي يدعيه أهل الاعتزال كما هو مذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين فإن كان أبو الحسين ساعدنا عليه فمرحباً بالوفاق ولكن يلزم بطلان مذهب الاعتزال بالكلية
The premises: It is known that His will for a thing does not depend on His willing that will; rather, that will occurs to Him whether He wills it or not. And it is also known that with decisive will, comprising the conditions and the removal of impediments, the object of will occurs, and without it it does not occur. And it follows from them — i.e., from what he knows by his own finding — that there is no will from him and no occurrence of the action consequent upon it from him. So how can he claim necessity in opposition to this? Al-Imam said in Nihayat al-'Uqul: It is strange of Abu al-Husayn that he opposed his companions in their statement that the agent capable of opposites does not depend his action for one without the other on a preponderant, and he claimed that knowledge of the dependence of that — i.e., his action for one without the other — on the motive to one of them is necessary, and he claimed that the occurrence of the action consequent upon the motive... and it was answered, and it is necessary for him, by his acknowledgment of these two premises, that the servant is not the creator of his action, as is our doctrine. Then he exaggerated in [affirming] that the servant is creator and surpassed all who preceded him until he claimed necessary knowledge of that. Al-Imam said: In my view, Abu al-Husayn was not among those who do not know that affirming those two premises invalidates the Mu'tazilite doctrine, meaning in the issue of creation of actions and what is built upon it. But when he had invalidated the foundations on which Mu'tazilism turns, he feared that his companions would realize his return from their doctrine, so he obfuscated the matter for them by his exaggeration in claiming necessary knowledge of that. Otherwise, this contradiction is more apparent than to be hidden from a beginner, let alone someone who has reached the degree of Abu al-Husayn in investigation and precision. So it appears that in this issue he followed our doctrine. It cannot be said: The acknowledgment of the dependence of the occurrence of the action from the capable agent on the motive and the necessity of its occurrence when it occurs does not negate the statement that the originated power is effective in bringing the action into existence; rather, it negates its independence in agency, on the basis of complete delegation to it. And he only claimed necessary knowledge in the first — i.e., the efficacy — not in the second — i.e., independence — so that what you have objected to him may be directed. For we say: Our purpose in this context is to negate independence, which the Mu'tazila claim, as is the doctrine of al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn. So if Abu al-Husayn has agreed with us on it, then welcome to agreement, but it necessitates the complete invalidation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a two-part argument. First, the author establishes two premises about divine will: (1) God's will for something does not depend on His willing that will; it simply occurs. (2) With a decisive will (al-irada al-jazima) that meets conditions and lacks impediments, the object of will necessarily occurs. From these premises, it follows that for the human servant, there is no will from him (since his will is created) and no occurrence of action from him independently. Therefore, the claim of necessary knowledge (darura) against the Ash'ari position is unfounded. The author then quotes Al-Imam (al-Juwayni) from Nihayat al-'Uqul, who expresses astonishment at Abu al-Husayn: he contradicted his own Mu'tazilite companions on the issue of the preponderant, and his acknowledgment of the two premises logically entails that the servant is not the creator of his action, which is the Ash'ari doctrine. Yet he insisted on claiming necessary knowledge for human agency, likely to conceal his implicit agreement from his followers. The passage concludes with an objection and answer. The objector argues that acknowledging the dependence of action on motive only negates independence (istiqlal) in agency, not efficacy (ta'thir). Abu al-Husayn only claimed necessary knowledge for efficacy, not independence, so the refutation misses its mark. The author replies that the whole purpose is to negate independence, which the Mu'tazila claim. Therefore, if Abu al-Husayn concedes this, he effectively abandons Mu'tazilism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that admitting human power has efficacy (ta'thir) is the same as claiming independence (istiqlal) in creating actions.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes efficacy from independence. Efficacy means the power contributes to the action, but independence means it alone creates without need for God. The Ash'ari refutation targets independence, not efficacy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main aim of the argument against Abu al-Husayn regarding human agency?
A) To prove that human power has no efficacy at all
B) To negate the independence of the human agent in creating his actions
C) To affirm that the human will is uncaused
D) To show that motives are irrelevant to action

Key Takeaway
Two premises about God's will prove that humans cannot be independent creators, and the Mu'tazilite attempt to salvage independence by distinguishing it from efficacy fails because the Ash'ari argument targets independence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily actions—your decisions, your worship, your interactions—do you genuinely feel that your ability to act is entirely dependent on God's will at every moment, or do you unconsciously assume independence? Take a specific example from today and re-evaluate it in light of this lesson.
?
The passage highlights that admitting dependence on God's will seems obvious yet many resist it. Is there an area of your life where you resist this dependence and insist on your own autonomy? What would change if you fully surrendered that area to God's will right now?
?
If you truly believed that your actions are not independently created by you but are brought into existence by God's will, how would your sense of pride, guilt, or gratitude shift? Commit to one concrete change in how you attribute your successes and failures starting today.

Al-Razi's Critique of Abu al-Husayn on the Necessity of the Motive
Imam al-Razi critiques Abu al-Husayn's contradictory position on the necessity of the motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi argues that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's position on the necessity of the motive logically entails Ash'arism, and how Abu al-Husayn's exaggerated claims reveal his actual agreement with the Ash'arites.

Core Idea
Abu al-Husayn's own premises prove the servant is not the creator of his act, contradicting his Mu'tazilism.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a devastating critique of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's theory of action. He shows that the very premises Abu al-Husayn affirms—the necessity of the motive and the necessary occurrence of the act upon the motive—logically lead to the conclusion that the human agent is not the true creator of his actions. This conclusion is precisely the Ash'arite position, which Abu al-Husayn ostensibly opposed. Yet al-Razi observes that Abu al-Husayn's vehement insistence on human agency was likely a rhetorical cover for his actual doctrinal shift.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of argument. Notice how al-Razi builds from known truths to a logical implication.
Pay attention to the contrast between the premises and the conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage starts with two known facts about divine will and the decisive will. Understand what they are: God's will does not depend on prior will, and a decisive will with conditions leads to the willed object.
These are presented as self-evident.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Al-Razi then draws the implication: from these premises it follows that the servant is not the creator of his act. This is the key point.
This conclusion contradicts Mu'tazilite free will.
4
Analyze al-Razi's interpretation
Al-Razi explains Abu al-Husayn's contradictory behavior as deliberate obfuscation: he knew his premises undercut Mu'tazilism but feared his followers.
Reflect on the psychological and political dimension.
5
Reflect on the contradiction
Consider why this matter is significant for the theology of human acts and the relationship between divine and human agency.
This is a classic case of philosophical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم أن إرادته للشيء لا تتوقف على إرادته تلك الإرادة بل تحصل له تلك الإرادة سواء أرادها أو لم يردها، (و) علم أيضاً ( أنه مع الإرادة الجازمة ) الجامعة للشرائط وارتفاع الموانع ( يحصل المراد وبدونها لا يحصل ويلزم منها ) أي من التي علمها بوجدانه ( أنه لا إرادة منه ولا حصول الفعل عقيبها منها فكيف يدعـ الضرورة في خلافه . قال الإمام في نهاية العقول : والعجب من أبي الحسين أنه خالف أصحابه في قولهم : القادر على الضدين لا يتوقف فعله لأحدهما دون الآخر على مرجح وزعم أن العلم بتوقف ذلك ) أي فعله لأحدهما دون الآخر ( على الداعي ) إلى أحدهما ) ضروري وزعم أن حصول الفعل عقيب الداعي وأجيب ولزمه للاعتراف بهاتين المقدمتين عدم كون العبد موجداً لفعله ) كما هو مذهبنا ( ثم بالغ في كون العبد موجداً وزاد على كل من تقدمه حتى ادعى العلم الضروري بذلك قال ( الإمام : ) وعندي أن أبا الحسين ما كان ممن لا يعلم أن القول بتينك المقدمتين يبطل مذهب الاعتزال) يعني في مسألة خلق الأعمال وما يبتني عليها ( لكنه لما أبطل الأصول التي عليها مدار الاعتزال خاف من تنبه أصحابه لرجوعه عن مذهبهم فلبس الأمر عليهم بمبالغته في ادعاء العلم الضروري بذلك ) وإلا فهذا التناقض أظهر من أن يخفى على المبتدئ فضلا عمن بلغ درجة أبي الحسين في التحقيق والتدقيق ) فظهر أنه في هذه المسألة جرى على مذهبنا
It is known that His will for something does not depend on His willing that will, but that will occurs to Him whether He wills it or not. And it is also known that with the decisive will that meets the conditions and the removal of obstacles, the willed object occurs, and without it it does not occur. And it follows from that—i.e., from what one knows by his own experience—that there is no will from him and no occurrence of the act consequent upon it from him; so how can he claim necessity contrary to that? The Imam said in Nihayat al-'Uqul: Amazing is Abu al-Husayn, for he opposed his companions in their statement that the one who has power over two opposites does not have his act for one rather than the other depend on a preponderant, and he claimed that the knowledge that this—i.e., his act for one rather than the other—depends on the motive is necessary, and he claimed that the occurrence of the act consequent upon the motive... and it was answered, and it necessarily followed from his acknowledgment of these two premises that the servant is not the creator of his act—as is our doctrine. Then he went to extremes in affirming that the servant is the creator, exceeding everyone before him, until he claimed necessary knowledge of it. The Imam said: In my view, Abu al-Husayn was not one who did not know that affirming those two premises invalidates the Mu'tazilite doctrine—meaning in the matter of the creation of acts and what is based upon it. But when he had invalidated the principles on which Mu'tazilism turns, he feared that his followers would become aware of his return from their doctrine, so he confused the matter for them by his exaggeration in claiming necessary knowledge of that. Otherwise, this contradiction is too obvious to be hidden from a beginner, let alone one who has reached the level of Abu al-Husayn in investigation and scrutiny. Thus it became clear that in this matter he followed our doctrine.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Imam al-Razi (the commentator) of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's position on the relationship between divine will, motive, and human agency. The passage begins by stating two known facts: (1) God's will for something does not depend on His willing that will; it occurs necessarily. (2) With a decisive will that meets conditions and removes obstacles, the object willed occurs; without it, it does not. From these, it follows that God's will and its effect are not themselves willed or produced by a prior will. Then al-Razi quotes Imam al-Razi's 'Nihayat al-'Uqul', expressing amazement at Abu al-Husayn. Abu al-Husayn broke with his fellow Mu'tazilites by denying that the agent's act for one opposite over another requires a preponderant (murajjih) and claimed that the necessity of the motive (da'i) for the act is evident. He also claimed that the occurrence of the act follows the motive necessarily. Al-Razi argues that these two premises—the necessity of the motive and the necessary occurrence of the act upon the motive—entail that the servant is not the creator of his act, which is the Ash'arite view. Yet Abu al-Husayn vehemently insisted that the servant is the creator, even claiming necessary knowledge of this. Al-Razi interprets this as deliberate confusion: Abu al-Husayn knew that his premises undercut Mu'tazilism, but he feared his followers' reaction, so he exaggerated his claims to obscure his actual agreement with the Ash'arites. The passage concludes that, despite his rhetoric, Abu al-Husayn effectively adopted the Ash'arite position on this issue.

Key Terms
الإرادة الجازمة
الداعي
العلم الضروري
المرجح
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the necessity of the motive implies human independence and free will, because the motive is internal.
Correction: On the contrary, when the motive is necessary and the act necessarily follows, the agent is not free; the servant is not the creator of his act, as al-Razi argues.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Razi claim logically follows from Abu al-Husayn's two premises about the motive and the occurrence of the act?
A) That the servant creates his own acts independently
B) That the servant is not the creator of his act
C) That the servant has free will with no need for a motive
D) That God's will is contingent on human will

Key Takeaway
This passage reveals how a Mu'tazilite argument, when pressed, collapses into Ash'arism, and how a theologian may obscure a doctrinal shift through rhetorical excess.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief or position publicly that you knew deep down was inconsistent, because you feared how others would react? What would it take for you to honestly acknowledge the truth, even if it meant disagreeing with your community?

What is one specific step you can take today to align your actions with what you truly believe?
?
Al-Razi argues that Abu al-Husayn's own premises lead to the conclusion that the servant is not the creator of his act, affirming total dependence on Allah. When you reflect on your own actions, do you truly feel that they are created by Allah and that you are not their ultimate origin? How does that affect your sense of gratitude and humility?

Objection and Answer: Negating Independence of Human Power
Objection and answer on negating independence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite objection clarifies that the purpose is to negate the servant's independence in agency, not to deny all causal efficacy of human power.

Core Idea
The goal is to negate independence, not to deny human power's role entirely.

This passage addresses a critical objection from the Mu'tazilite perspective. The objection claims that acknowledging the necessity of the motive does not contradict the effectiveness of human power. The Ash'ari reply clarifies that their aim is specifically to negate the servant's independence in agency, not to deny that human power has a role as an instrument. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari position on qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objection: that acknowledging motive necessity does not negate human power's effect, only independence.
2
Understand the Ash'ari answer
The answer states that the purpose is to negate independence, as per al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn.
This is the central claim of the passage.
3
Examine the elaboration on 'no will from him'
The commentary clarifies that even if will is not dependent, it doesn't mean it's not from God; it can be without choice.
This reinforces the negation of independence.
4
Reflect on the agreement and invalidation of Mu'tazilism
If Abu al-Husayn agrees on negating independence, Mu'tazilism is invalidated because it would contradict their own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : الاعتراف بتوقف صدور الفعل عن القادر على الداعي ووجوب حصوله عند حصوله لا ينافي القول بأن القدرة الحادثة مؤثرة في وجود الفعل وإنما ينافي استقلاله بالفاعلية ) على سبيل التفويض إليه بالكلية ، ( وهو إنما ادعى العلم الضروري في الأول ( أي التأثير ( لا في الثاني ) أي الاستقلال حتى يتجه ما وردتموه عليه ) لأنا نقول : غرضنا في هذا المقام (سلب الاستقلال ) الذي يدعيه أهل الاعتزال ) كما هو مذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين فإن كان أبو الحسين ساعدنا عليه فمرحباً بالوفاق ولكن يلزم بطلان مذهب الاعتزال بالكلية إذ
قوله : ( لا إرادة منه فيه بحث إذ لا يلزم من عدم توقف إرادته على إرادة أخرى أن لا يكون الإرادة منه لجواز أن يكون منه لا بالاختيار ولك أن تقول : سيصرح أن الغرض في هذا المقام سلب الاستقلال وإذا يحصل على تقدير صدور إرادته منه فمعنى قوله : لا الإرادة منه أنها ليست منه بالإرادة فحينئذ يندفع البحث . قوله : ( كما هو مذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين عدم الاستقلال عند الأستاذ ظاهر، وأما عند إمام الحرمين فلان قدرة العبد لما كانت موجبة للفعل عنده لم يكن هو مستقلاً في التمكن من فعله وتركه، وفي ذكر إمام الحرمين بحث يظهر مما مر في أول المقصد فليتأمل. قوله: (فمرحباً بالوفاق) أي في نفي الاستقلال ولا مرحباً بإسناد الإيجاد إلى غير الله تعالى .
It cannot be said: The acknowledgment that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent depends on the motive and the necessity of its occurrence when the motive occurs does not contradict the statement that the contingent power is effective in bringing about the act; rather, it contradicts its independence in agency, on the basis of complete delegation to it. And he only claimed necessary knowledge concerning the first—i.e., the effect—not the second—i.e., independence—so that what you have objected to him would be directed. For we say: Our purpose in this context is the negation of independence that the Mu'tazilites claim, as is the doctrine of al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn. If Abu al-Husayn has agreed with us on this, then welcome to agreement, but it necessarily follows that the Mu'tazilite doctrine is entirely invalidated. His saying: 'There is no will from him'—there is a discussion about it, for the fact that His will does not depend on another will does not entail that the will is not from Him, because it is possible that it is from Him not by choice. But you can say: He will clearly state that the purpose in this context is the negation of independence, and if it occurs on the assumption that His will originates from Him, then the meaning of his saying 'there is no will from him' is that it is not from him by will. Then the discussion is rebutted. His saying: 'As is the doctrine of al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn': The negation of independence is clear with al-Ustadh. As for Imam al-Haramayn, because the servant's power, since it necessitates the act according to him, he is not independent in being able to do it or leave it. There is a discussion about mentioning Imam al-Haramayn that appears from what has passed at the beginning of the Goal, so let it be considered. His saying: 'Welcome to agreement' means in negating independence, and not welcome to attributing bringing into existence to other than God Almighty.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the negation of the servant's independence. The objection states that acknowledging the necessity of the motive and the necessary occurrence of the act upon the motive does not contradict the claim that the contingent power (al-qudra al-haditha) is effective in producing the act; it only contradicts the servant's independence (istiqlal) in agency, meaning complete delegation (tafwid). The objector argues that Abu al-Husayn claimed necessary knowledge only for the effect (ta'thir) of the motive, not for independence, so the earlier criticism does not apply. The answer begins with 'For we say' (lana naqul): The purpose in this context is to negate independence (salb al-istiqlal) which the Mu'tazilites claim, and this is the doctrine of al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). If Abu al-Husayn agrees on negating independence, then there is agreement, but this entirely invalidates Mu'tazilism. Then the commentary elaborates on phrases: regarding 'no will from him', it explains that even if the will is not dependent on another will, it can still be from God without being by choice. The purpose is to negate independence. Regarding 'as is the doctrine of al-Ustadh and Imam al-Haramayn', it notes that independence is negated for both, but for Imam al-Haramayn because power necessitates the act, so the servant is not independent in choosing to act or refrain. Finally, 'welcome to agreement' means agreement only on negating independence, not on attributing creation to other than God.

Key Terms

التأثير
التفويض
القدرة الحادثة
الأستاذ
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'arites deny that human power has any effect on actions.
Correction: Ash'arites negate independence (istiqlal), not all causal efficacy; human power is effective as a locus for God's creation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what exactly is the goal of the argument in this context?
A) To negate the effect of the motive
B) To negate the servant's independence in agency
C) To affirm the complete delegation of creation to the servant
D) To prove that the servant creates his act

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument against Mu'tazilism does not deny human power's effectiveness outright but specifically targets the claim of the servant's independence in agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you achieved something you were proud of. In that moment, did you feel that your ability and effort were entirely your own? How does this lesson's distinction between effectiveness and independence challenge that feeling? What can you do today to acknowledge your dependence on Allah without diminishing your effort?
?
The Ash'ari position affirms that human power is effective but not independent. Where in your life do you most struggle to accept that your power is a gift from Allah and not self-originating? What would change if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
If the negation of independence is the goal, how would you know if you have achieved it in your heart? Examine your reactions when you face failure or difficulty – do you feel a sense of helplessness or a trust that Allah's power is over all? What step can you take this week to strengthen your conviction that you are never independent of Him?

No Rational Difference Between Commanding an Act and Its Necessary Consequence
Argument that commanding a cause and its necessary effect are rationally equivalent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational theology finds no distinction between commanding an act and commanding its necessary consequence, and how this undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Rationally, commanding a cause is the same as commanding its necessary effect.

In this passage, the author presents a sharp logical argument against the Mu'tazilite position. He argues that there is no rational difference between God commanding a servant to do an act and commanding him to do something that necessarily follows from that act. Similarly, punishing for a directly caused act is no different from punishing for a necessary consequence. Understanding this equivalence is key to seeing why the Mu'tazilite insistence on human autonomy collapses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two comparisons
Read the passage carefully. Notice that it makes two parallel comparisons: one about commanding, one about punishing. In both, the author claims reason sees no difference between the direct and the indirect.
The Arabic says 'لا فرق' (no difference) twice.
2
Identify the rational principle
Why is there no difference? Because in both cases the servant has ability to do or refrain, and the doer of evil and the doer of what necessitates evil are considered the same in rational judgment.
This principle undercuts attempts to distinguish between God's direct and indirect roles.
3
Apply to Mu'tazilism
The author then concludes that anyone who admits the act necessarily follows God's decisive will cannot consistently be a Mu'tazilite. Reflect on why this would be.
Mu'tazilites typically stress human free will and justice of punishment.
4
Consider the conclusion about Abu al-Husayn
The author claims that Abu al-Husayn's denial of this conclusion was mere deception. Ask yourself: what does this reveal about the internal tensions in Mu'tazilism?
This is a polemical point within the theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير لا فرق في العقل بين أن يأمر الله عبده بما يفعله هو بنفسه وبين أن يأمره بما يجب عند فعله ويمتنع عند عدمه فإن المأمور على كلا التقديرين متمكن من الفعل أو الترك، وأيضاً لا فرق بين أن يعذب الله العبد على ما أوجده فيه وبين أن يعذبه على فعل يجب حصوله عندما وجده فيه، لأنه لا فرق في العقول بين فاعل القبيح والظلم وبين فاعل ما يوجب القبيح والظلم فمن اعترف بوجوب حصول الفعل عند حصوله الإرادة الجازمة انسد عليه باب القول بالاعتزال، فظهر أن أبا الحسين أنكر الاعتزال في هذه المسألة وأن تلك المبالغة منه تمويه وتلبيس انتهى كلامه
Otherwise, there is no difference in reason between God commanding His servant to do what he himself does and commanding him to do what necessarily occurs when he does it and is impossible when he does not do it, for the commanded person in both cases is capable of doing or refraining. Also, there is no difference between God punishing the servant for what He brings into existence in him and punishing him for an act that necessarily occurs when He brings it into existence in him, because there is no difference in intellects between the doer of evil and injustice and the doer of that which necessitates evil and injustice. So whoever acknowledges the necessity of the occurrence of the act upon the occurrence of the decisive will, the door to Mu'tazilism is closed to him. Thus it became clear that Abu al-Husayn denied Mu'tazilism in this matter and that his exaggeration was deception and obfuscation — end of his speech.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that there is no rational distinction between two cases: (1) God commanding the servant to do an act he himself does, and (2) God commanding the servant to do something that necessarily follows from that act. In both, the servant is capable of obedience or disobedience. Similarly, there is no difference between punishing a servant for something God brings into existence in him (like an act) and punishing him for something that necessarily follows from that act. The reason is that, rationally, the doer of evil and the doer of what necessitates evil are treated alike. Therefore, anyone who accepts that the act necessarily occurs with the decisive will (from God) cannot consistently hold the Mu'tazilite position. The conclusion is that Abu al-Husayn's claims of necessary knowledge of human creation were merely deception; in reality, his premises lead to the Ash'arite position. This shows he effectively abandoned Mu'tazilism.

Key Terms
الإرادة الجازمة
القبيح
الظلم
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a moral difference between commanding an action and commanding its necessary consequence, so God's justice can avoid blame for evil consequences.
Correction: The author argues that reason sees no distinction; the commanded person is equally capable, and the doer of evil and the doer of what necessitates evil are treated the same.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that acknowledging the necessary occurrence of the act upon the decisive will closes the door to Mu'tazilism?
A) Because Mu'tazilites deny the decisive will
B) Because it makes God's command and punishment analogous to His direct causation, which the Mu'tazilites reject
C) Because it proves the servant is the creator of his act
D) Because it shows the servant has no power at all

Key Takeaway
Rationally, commanding an act and commanding its necessary consequence are equivalent, which undermines the Mu'tazilite distinction between direct and indirect causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the distinction between causing evil and causing what necessitates evil collapses in reason, then what excuses do you have when your choices lead to harm that you claim you never intended? Can you truly separate yourself from the consequences of your actions? Take one area of your life where you have blamed circumstances or others for outcomes you helped produce, and write down a plan to take full responsibility starting today.
?
You believe that God's will is decisive in your life. But do you live as if your choices matter? This lesson shows that rational equivalence between cause and effect does not remove your capacity to act or refrain. When was the last time you used your capacity to do good despite the seeming necessity of your habits? What one habit will you consciously break tomorrow, knowing that you are fully capable of refraining?

Mu'tazilite Arguments for Human Independence in Acts
Enumerates Mu'tazilite arguments from commands, praise, reward, and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite arguments that human independence in creating acts is necessary for the validity of divine commands, praise, reward, and prophethood.

Core Idea
Without human independence, divine commands and prophethood become meaningless.

This lesson presents the core Mu'tazilite case for human creation of acts. They argue that without the servant being an independent creator of his actions, the entire structure of religious obligation, moral praise, reward, and the purpose of sending prophets collapses. The passage reduces all their arguments to one central point: the necessity of human independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Note that it begins by stating that all arguments reduce to one point.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the five arguments
The passage lists five consequences if the servant is not independent: invalidity of commands/prohibitions, loss of discipline, lifting of praise/blame, loss of reward/punishment, and futility of sending prophets. Find each in the text.
Look for keywords like 'بطل التكليف', 'بطل التأديب', 'ارتفع المدح والذم', 'ارتفع الثواب والعقاب', 'لم يبق للبعثة فائدة'.
3
Understand the logical chain
Each argument follows the same logic: if the servant is not the independent creator of his act, then attributing the act to him for moral or religious purposes is irrational. Reflect on why each step depends on independence.
This is the heart of the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غيره أي غير أبي الحسين فيستدل عليه أي على أن العبد موجد لأفعاله بوجوه كثيرة مرجعها إلى أمر واحد وهو أنه لولا استقلال العبد بالفعل على سبيل الاختيار لبطل التكليف بالأوامر والنواهي لأن العبد إذا لم يكن موجداً لفعله مستقلا في إيجاده لم يصح عقلا أن يقال له افعل كذا ولا تفعل كذا وبطل التأديب الذي ورد به الشرع، إذ لا يستقتل بإيجاد فعله وارتفع المدح والذم إذ ليس الفعل مستند إليه مطلقاً حتى يمدح به أو يذم وارتفع الثواب والعقاب الوارد بهما الوعد والوعيد ولم يبق للبعثة فائدة لأن العباد ليسوا موجودين لأفعالهم فمن أين لهم استحقاق الثواب والعقاب عليها بل هي مخلوقة لله تعالى، فيجوز حينئذ أن يعكس فيعاقب الأنبياء وأتباعهم ويثبت الفراعنة فلا وأشياعهم يتصور منفعة للبعثة أصلا . والجواب : منع الملازمات المذكورة وهو المدح والذم باعتبار المحلية لا باعتبار الفاعلية حتى يشترط فيهما الاستقلال بالفعل وذلك كما يمدح الشيء ويذم بحسنه وقبحه وسلامته من الآفة وعاهته فإن ذلك باعتبار أنه محل لها لا مؤثر فيها، وأما الثواب والعقاب المترتبان على الأفعال الاختيارية فكسائر العاديات المترتبة على أسبابها بطريق العادة من غير لزوم
قوله : عند حصول الإرادة الجازمة أي مع اعترافه بأنها من غيره، وكذا الداعي الذي يتوقف عليه الفعل كما هو المذهب عندهم، فإن الداعي عبارة عن العلم بالمصلحة ولا قائل بأن العلم مخلوق للعبد
قوله : فكسائر العاديات قيل : هذا يؤدي إلى القول بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي كما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة لإيجاب العادة، وذلك لأن قسمة العادة إلى الموجب وغيره لم تعهد عن أحد ولا جهة له أيضاً والجواب أما أولاً فجواز إرادة التنظير من قوله فكسائر العاديات أي جميعها، والتنظير في مجرد عدم اللزوم العقلي واتجاه السؤال لا أنهما من قبيل العاديات وأما ثانياً فهو أن مراد المعتزلة من الوجوب عليه تعالى لزوم القبح العقلي في نفس تركهما وهو الذي
As for other than him—i.e., other than Abu al-Husayn—he argues for it—i.e., for the servant's being the creator of his acts—by many arguments, all of which reduce to one point: that if the servant were not independent in the act by choice, then obligation by commands and prohibitions would be invalid, because the servant, if he is not the creator of his act, independent in creating it, it would not be rational to say to him 'do this' and 'do not do this'. And discipline that the Law has brought would be invalid, since he is not independent in creating his act. And praise and blame would be lifted, since the act is not attributed to him at all so that he be praised or blamed for it. And reward and punishment that promise and threat have brought would be lifted, and there would be no benefit in sending prophets, because the servants are not the creators of their acts; whence would they deserve reward and punishment for them? Rather, they are created by God Almighty. Then it would be permissible to reverse things, so prophets and their followers might be punished and Pharaohs and their followers rewarded; no benefit from the sending could be conceived at all. The answer: Denial of the aforementioned implications. And that is that praise and blame are with respect to being the locus, not with respect to being the agent, such that independence in the act would be required in them. This is like how a thing is praised or blamed for its goodness or badness, its being free from defect or having a defect; for that is with respect to it being a locus for them, not an efficient cause. As for reward and punishment that follow upon voluntary actions, they are like other customary things that follow upon their causes by way of habit, without necessity. His saying: 'Upon the occurrence of the decisive will' — i.e., while acknowledging that it is from other than him, and likewise the motive on which the act depends, as is the doctrine with them, for the motive is knowledge of the benefit, and no one says that knowledge is created by the servant. His saying: 'Like other customary things' — it was said: This leads to the statement that the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient are necessary, as the Mu'tazilites held, due to the necessity of custom. This is because the division of custom into that which necessitates and other has not been known from anyone, and it has no justification either. The answer: First, it is possible that by his saying 'like other customary things' he intends analogy, i.e., all of them, and the analogy is merely in the absence of rational necessity and the direction of the question, not that they are of the category of customary things. Second, what the Mu'tazilites mean by obligation upon Him Almighty is the necessity of rational evil in the very omission of them, which is...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the general Mu'tazilite arguments for human creation of acts and the Ash'arite response. The arguments reduce to one point: without the servant's independence in his act, divine commands and prohibitions, discipline, praise and blame, reward and punishment, and the purpose of sending prophets all become meaningless. If acts are created by God, then God could punish prophets and reward tyrants, making prophethood useless. The response first denies the implications: praise and blame are based on being the locus (mahall) of the act, not on being the agent (fa'il). A thing is praised for its goodness or blamed for its defect regardless of who caused it. Similarly, reward and punishment are like customary occurrences: they follow from actions by God's habit, not by rational necessity. This avoids the charge of injustice. The marginal notes clarify: the decisive will is from God, the motive is knowledge of benefit (not created by man). Regarding 'like other customary things', an objection was raised that this would entail necessity of reward/punishment like the Mu'tazilites claim. The answer: the phrase is an analogy only in the absence of rational necessity, and the Mu'tazilites' 'obligation' upon God means the rational evil of omitting reward/punishment, which is what the Ash'arites deny.

Key Terms
الاستقلال
التكليف
المدح والذم
الثواب والعقاب
البعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite arguments, what would happen if the servant is not independent in his act?
A) Only commands and prohibitions would be invalid
B) All of commands, discipline, praise, reward, and prophethood would be invalid
C) Only reward and punishment would be invalid
D) Only prophethood would be futile

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that human independence in creating acts is the foundation of religious obligation and moral accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for your sins—'I was tired', 'everyone does it', 'God willed it'—are you really treating yourself as the independent creator of your actions the way the Mu'tazilites demand? Which verse from this lesson's argument pierces that excuse the most?
?
If you truly believed that your every action is directly created by God with no input from you, how would you feel when you read the command 'Do not approach zina'? Would you feel personally addressed, or would you think it doesn't apply to you because God would never command what He creates?
?
The Mu'tazilites say that without human independence, even the sending of prophets becomes meaningless. When you hear or read the Quran, do you receive it as a personal address from God to you—a call you can choose to obey or reject—or do you treat it as mere information? What would change in your daily life if you truly accepted that you are solely responsible for your response?

Ash'arite Response: Locus and Custom
Ash'arite response denying implications and redefining praise/reward in terms of locus and custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response that praise and blame are based on the act being a locus of value, and that reward and punishment follow by divine custom, not rational necessity.

Core Idea
Praise and blame attach to acts as loci of value, not to agents as creators.

The Ash'arite response directly addresses the Mu'tazilite arguments by denying the necessary implications. It introduces a crucial distinction: praise and blame are based on the act's quality as a locus (mahall), not on agency (fa'iliyya). Reward and punishment are then explained as customary consequences ordained by God, not rationally necessitated. This lesson unpacks that response and its defense against a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Begin with 'والجواب' and read through to the end of the available text. Notice the two main parts: the denial of implications and the explanation of reward/punishment.
The response is concise; pay attention to the distinction between locality and agency.
2
Understand the locus vs agent distinction
The response states that praise and blame are 'باعتبار المحلية لا باعتبار الفاعلية'. This means a thing is praised for being good or free from defect, regardless of who made it so. Reflect on examples like praising a beautiful painting without knowing the painter.
This is the key Ash'arite insight.
3
Analyze the custom analogy for reward/punishment
Reward and punishment are likened to other customary things ('عاديات') that follow their causes by God's habit, not by logical necessity. Consider how fire burns by habit, not by inherent necessity.
This avoids the Mu'tazilite charge of injustice.
4
Consider the objection and answer
An objection is raised: does this analogy not imply necessity of reward/punishment, as the Mu'tazilites claim? The answer clarifies that the analogy is only about the absence of rational necessity, not about the nature of the connection.
The marginal notes add further clarification about the decisive will and motive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غيره أي غير أبي الحسين فيستدل عليه أي على أن العبد موجد لأفعاله بوجوه كثيرة مرجعها إلى أمر واحد وهو أنه لولا استقلال العبد بالفعل على سبيل الاختيار لبطل التكليف بالأوامر والنواهي لأن العبد إذا لم يكن موجداً لفعله مستقلا في إيجاده لم يصح عقلا أن يقال له افعل كذا ولا تفعل كذا وبطل التأديب الذي ورد به الشرع، إذ لا يستقتل بإيجاد فعله وارتفع المدح والذم إذ ليس الفعل مستند إليه مطلقاً حتى يمدح به أو يذم وارتفع الثواب والعقاب الوارد بهما الوعد والوعيد ولم يبق للبعثة فائدة لأن العباد ليسوا موجودين لأفعالهم فمن أين لهم استحقاق الثواب والعقاب عليها بل هي مخلوقة لله تعالى، فيجوز حينئذ أن يعكس فيعاقب الأنبياء وأتباعهم ويثبت الفراعنة فلا وأشياعهم يتصور منفعة للبعثة أصلا . والجواب : منع الملازمات المذكورة وهو المدح والذم باعتبار المحلية لا باعتبار الفاعلية حتى يشترط فيهما الاستقلال بالفعل وذلك كما يمدح الشيء ويذم بحسنه وقبحه وسلامته من الآفة وعاهته فإن ذلك باعتبار أنه محل لها لا مؤثر فيها، وأما الثواب والعقاب المترتبان على الأفعال الاختيارية فكسائر العاديات المترتبة على أسبابها بطريق العادة من غير لزوم
قوله : عند حصول الإرادة الجازمة أي مع اعترافه بأنها من غيره، وكذا الداعي الذي يتوقف عليه الفعل كما هو المذهب عندهم، فإن الداعي عبارة عن العلم بالمصلحة ولا قائل بأن العلم مخلوق للعبد
قوله : فكسائر العاديات قيل : هذا يؤدي إلى القول بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي كما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة لإيجاب العادة، وذلك لأن قسمة العادة إلى الموجب وغيره لم تعهد عن أحد ولا جهة له أيضاً والجواب أما أولاً فجواز إرادة التنظير من قوله فكسائر العاديات أي جميعها، والتنظير في مجرد عدم اللزوم العقلي واتجاه السؤال لا أنهما من قبيل العاديات وأما ثانياً فهو أن مراد المعتزلة من الوجوب عليه تعالى لزوم القبح العقلي في نفس تركهما وهو الذي
As for other than him—i.e., other than Abu al-Husayn—he argues for it—i.e., for the servant's being the creator of his acts—by many arguments, all of which reduce to one point: that if the servant were not independent in the act by choice, then obligation by commands and prohibitions would be invalid, because the servant, if he is not the creator of his act, independent in creating it, it would not be rational to say to him 'do this' and 'do not do this'. And discipline that the Law has brought would be invalid, since he is not independent in creating his act. And praise and blame would be lifted, since the act is not attributed to him at all so that he be praised or blamed for it. And reward and punishment that promise and threat have brought would be lifted, and there would be no benefit in sending prophets, because the servants are not the creators of their acts; whence would they deserve reward and punishment for them? Rather, they are created by God Almighty. Then it would be permissible to reverse things, so prophets and their followers might be punished and Pharaohs and their followers rewarded; no benefit from the sending could be conceived at all. The answer: Denial of the aforementioned implications. And that is that praise and blame are with respect to being the locus, not with respect to being the agent, such that independence in the act would be required in them. This is like how a thing is praised or blamed for its goodness or badness, its being free from defect or having a defect; for that is with respect to it being a locus for them, not an efficient cause. As for reward and punishment that follow upon voluntary actions, they are like other customary things that follow upon their causes by way of habit, without necessity. His saying: 'Upon the occurrence of the decisive will' — i.e., while acknowledging that it is from other than him, and likewise the motive on which the act depends, as is the doctrine with them, for the motive is knowledge of the benefit, and no one says that knowledge is created by the servant. His saying: 'Like other customary things' — it was said: This leads to the statement that the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient are necessary, as the Mu'tazilites held, due to the necessity of custom. This is because the division of custom into that which necessitates and other has not been known from anyone, and it has no justification either. The answer: First, it is possible that by his saying 'like other customary things' he intends analogy, i.e., all of them, and the analogy is merely in the absence of rational necessity and the direction of the question, not that they are of the category of customary things. Second, what the Mu'tazilites mean by obligation upon Him Almighty is the necessity of rational evil in the very omission of them, which is...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the general Mu'tazilite arguments for human creation of acts and the Ash'arite response. The arguments reduce to one point: without the servant's independence in his act, divine commands and prohibitions, discipline, praise and blame, reward and punishment, and the purpose of sending prophets all become meaningless. If acts are created by God, then God could punish prophets and reward tyrants, making prophethood useless. The response first denies the implications: praise and blame are based on being the locus (mahall) of the act, not on being the agent (fa'il). A thing is praised for its goodness or blamed for its defect regardless of who caused it. Similarly, reward and punishment are like customary occurrences: they follow from actions by God's habit, not by rational necessity. This avoids the charge of injustice. The marginal notes clarify: the decisive will is from God, the motive is knowledge of benefit (not created by man). Regarding 'like other customary things', an objection was raised that this would entail necessity of reward/punishment like the Mu'tazilites claim. The answer: the phrase is an analogy only in the absence of rational necessity, and the Mu'tazilites' 'obligation' upon God means the rational evil of omitting reward/punishment, which is what the Ash'arites deny.

Key Terms
المحلية
الفاعلية
العاديات
المدح والذم
الثواب والعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if reward and punishment are customary, they become necessary and God is bound by custom.
Correction: The Ash'arites distinguish between customary occurrence and rational necessity. Custom does not entail logical obligation; it is God's free habit, not a constraint.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite response, praise and blame are based on what?
A) The agent's independence
B) The act being a locus of goodness or badness
C) The intention of the doer
D) The outcome of the act

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite response reorients moral attribution to the quality of the act as a locus, and views reward/punishment as divine custom, not rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something good, do you unconsciously assume that you are the independent author of that good act, and that this entitles you to praise? How would your humility deepen if you truly saw yourself as only a locus for God's created quality of goodness?
?
If reward and punishment are not rationally necessary but come from God's free custom, does your hope in His mercy and fear of His justice become more personal? Would you still obey Him if you knew no reward was guaranteed?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'owes' you for your worship or obedience? How does the Ash'arite teaching that He is under no obligation, even by His own custom, challenge that sense of entitlement? What would it mean for your heart to serve Him with no expectation of recompense?

Third Objection: Equilibrium of Motives and the Power of the Servant
Third objection: When motives are balanced, act is impossible; when one outweighs, act is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against human power when motives are balanced or preponderant, and the rebuttal that necessity from power and motive does not negate maqdūriyya.

Core Idea
An act necessitated by power and motive is still an act of power.

This passage presents a key objection in the qadar debates: if motives are equal, no act occurs; if one outweighs, the act is necessary. This seems to eliminate human power and obligation. The response clarifies that the necessity arising from the combination of power and motive does not remove the act from being a potential object of power (maqdūriyya). Understanding this rebuttal is crucial for the Ashʿarī position on human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage that states the third objection: when motives are equal, act is impossible; when one outweighs, preponderant act necessary, outweighed impossible — thus no power for the servant.
Notice the logical structure: equilibrium → impossibility, preponderance → necessity.
2
Study the rebuttal
Read the solution given: the necessity of the act by the combination of power and motive does not remove it from being maqdūriyya. It actually realizes it. The meaning of 'qadir' is defined: when decisive will is obtained through motive and impediment removed, the agent effects the act.
The key point: necessity here is conditional, not coercive.
3
Analyze the commentary critique
The commentary notes that this objection applies only to Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī and his followers, not to those who do not suspend God's act. The solution from Lubāb al-Arbaʿīn is critiqued because if neither motive nor power originates from the servant, maqdūriyya is not realized.
Consider the intra-school debate: the objection is meant to force a conclusion against a specific group.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this objection and rebuttal fit into the larger discussion of human free will and divine omnipotence in kalām. This is a classic impasse: if human power is real, how can necessity coexist? The response reframes necessity as conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : إن الفعل عند استواء . . لم يرده من الداعي إلى الفعل والترك يمتنع لأن الرجحان يناقض الاستواء (وعند رجحان أحدهما يجب الراجح ويمتنع (الآخر) المرجوح فلا قدرة للعبد على فعله فبطل تكلیفه به، ويرد عليه ذلك النقض وحله أن وجوب الفعل بمجموع القدرة والداعية لا يخرجه عن المقدورية بل يحققها، وكذا امتناعه لعدم الداعي فإن معنى كونه قادراً أنه إذا حصل له الإرادة الجازمة بواسطة الداعية مع ارتفاع المانع أثر فيه . قوله : ( الثالث الفعل عند استواء الداعي إلخ) فيه بحث لأن طريقة الاستدلال لغير أبي الحسين وتوابعه وغيرهم لا يقولون بتوقف فعل الباري فلا وجه وجيهاً للوجه الثالث، اللهم إلا أن يقال : توابع أبي الحسين في وجوب الداعي يخالفه في ضرورية المذهب والإلزام عليهم فتأمل . قوله : ( وحله أن وجوب الفعل إلخ ) هذا الحل لصاحب لباب الأربعين وفيه بحث لأن شيئاً من الداعي والقدرة إذا لم يكن من العبد كما هو مذهبهم ووجب الفعل بعدهما لم يتحقق المقدورية.
(The third: that the act, when the motive to act and to refrain are equal . . he does not will it, it is impossible because preponderance contradicts equality (and when one of them outweighs, the preponderant becomes necessary and the outweighed becomes impossible) so the servant has no power over his act, so his obligation is invalidated, and that rebuttal is brought against it and its solution is that the necessity of the act by the combination of power and motive does not remove it from being a potential object of power (maqdūriyya) but rather realizes it, and likewise its impossibility due to lack of motive. For the meaning of being capable is that when he obtains decisive will through a motive together with removal of the impediment, he effects it. His saying: (The third: the act when the motive is equal etc.) in it there is discussion because the method of reasoning for other than Abū al-Ḥusayn and his followers and others—they do not speak of the suspension of God's act, so there is no valid face for the third aspect, unless it be said: the followers of Abū al-Ḥusayn, regarding the necessity of the motive, oppose him in the necessity of the doctrine and the obligation upon them, so reflect. His saying: (And its solution that the necessity of the act etc.) This solution is from the author of Lubāb al-Arbaʿīn, and in it there is discussion because if neither the motive nor the power is from the servant, as is their doctrine, and the act becomes necessary after them, then maqdūriyya is not realized.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: when the motives (dawāʿī) for action and inaction are equal, the act is impossible because preponderance contradicts equality; when one motive outweighs, the preponderant act becomes necessary and the other impossible, so the servant has no power at all and obligation is invalidated. The rebuttal and solution are then given: the necessity of the act arising from the combination of power and motive does not remove it from being a potential object of power (maqdūriyya); rather, it confirms it. The meaning of being capable is that when decisive will (irāda jāzima) is obtained through a motive and the impediment is removed, the agent effects the act. The commentary then critiques this objection, noting that the method of reasoning used here does not apply to those other than Abū al-Ḥusayn (al-Baṣrī) and his followers, who do not suspend God's act, so the third aspect has no valid basis unless to oblige Abū al-Ḥusayn's followers. The solution from Lubāb al-Arbaʿīn is also discussed, pointing out that if neither motive nor power originates from the servant, then maqdūriyya is not realized.

Key Terms

استواء
رجحان

إرادة جازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an act is necessitated by a preponderant motive, it cannot be under human power (maqdūriyya).
Correction: The necessity of an act from the combination of power and motive does not remove it from being a potential object of power; rather, it realizes it. Power is defined as the ability to effect an act when decisive will is present and impediments are absent.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what is the status of an act when motives are perfectly balanced?
A) It is necessary
B) It is impossible
C) It is possible
D) It is optional

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because the necessity of an act arising from power and motive does not negate the act's being a maqdūr; rather, it is the very condition for power to be effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself 'I couldn't help it' or 'it was meant to be'? This lesson shows that even when your motives are strong, the act remains within your power. What sin will you now stop excusing and instead work to change the motives behind it?
?
The passage defines power as having decisive will through motive with no impediment. Think of a recent choice you regret: were your motives truly sound or did you act impulsively? Will you commit to examining your motives before every significant decision this week?
?
Do you live as though your actions are determined by external circumstances and your own desires, or do you take full ownership of your choices? This lesson insists that necessity from power and motive does not remove responsibility. What is one area of your life where you need to reclaim responsibility today?

The Contradiction in Commanding Abu Lahab to Believe
Fourth objection: Abu Lahab is commanded to believe, but God informed he will not—contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the command for Abu Lahab to believe creates a logical contradiction when combined with God's foreknowledge of his disbelief.

Core Idea
Commanding Abu Lahab to believe forces him to affirm his own disbelief.

This is the fourth objection in a series of arguments about divine predestination and human obligation. The objection presents a puzzle: if God commands Abu Lahab to believe, but also reveals through His Prophet that Abu Lahab will never believe, then what exactly is Abu Lahab being commanded to do? The passage shows that the content of the commanded belief becomes self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the objection states what is commanded, and then shows why it's impossible.
2
Identify the three key elements
Find the command, the reason for impossibility, and the contradiction. The command is to believe perpetually, the impossibility arises from divine information, and the contradiction is that belief requires affirming the prophet's news of disbelief.
3
Understand the logical trap
Work through the reasoning: to obey the command, Abu Lahab must believe everything the Prophet brought. Among that is the statement 'Abu Lahab will not believe.' So he must believe that he does not believe, which is a paradox.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This objection challenges the coherence of obligation (taklif) when divine foreknowledge is absolute. It forces the theologian to reconcile God's command with His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع : إيمان أبي لهب مأمور به ) أي أمر بأن يؤمن دائما ( وهو ممتنع لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن والإيمان تصديق الرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ) ، ومما جاء به أنه لا يؤمن ( فيكون ) هو حال إيمانه على الاستمرار ( مأموراً بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن ويصدق بأنه لا يصدق
(The fourth: The faith of Abu Lahab is commanded) i.e. he is commanded to believe perpetually (and it is impossible because God informed that he will not believe, and faith is affirmation of the messenger regarding what he is known to have brought), and among that which he brought is that he will not believe (so) his state of faith perpetually (he is commanded to believe that he does not believe and to affirm that he does not affirm).
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection regarding Abu Lahab. It states that he is commanded to have faith (īmān), but God has informed that he will never believe. Faith involves affirming (taṣdīq) the messenger regarding everything he brought, including the news that Abu Lahab himself will not believe. Therefore, for Abu Lahab to obey the command to believe, he would have to affirm that the messenger's report is true, which includes the report that he does not believe, resulting in a contradiction: he would be commanded to believe that he does not believe and to affirm that he does not affirm. This shows the impossibility of obligation in such a case.

Key Terms
إيمان
أمر
ممتنع
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the contradiction is simply that Abu Lahab cannot believe, but the real issue is the logical impossibility of the content of belief – believing that one does not believe.
Correction: The command is not impossible because of inability, but because the object of belief becomes self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction in commanding Abu Lahab to believe?
A) He is commanded to do the impossible
B) He must believe that he does not believe
C) God's knowledge changes
D) He lacks the ability

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that commanding Abu Lahab to believe results in a logical paradox because he would have to affirm his own disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt commanded by Allah to believe something that seemed to contradict what you knew about yourself? How did you respond—did you let the command change your self-perception, or did you ignore it?
?
This passage shows that even a command can become paradoxical. In your own life, are there commands of Allah that you struggle with because they seem to conflict with what you think you know? What would it mean for you to submit to that command despite the tension?
?
If you were in Abu Lahab's position—knowing that you will never believe—would you still strive to obey the command? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on your own knowledge versus trust in Allah's command?
