The Sunni Position on Sealing Verses
Sanusi lists the Quranic terms for sealing and states the Sunni interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni interpretation of Quranic terms like طبع and ختم, and the linguistic argument that they refer to the real creation of misguidance in hearts.

Core Idea
The sealing terms in the Quran are literal acts of Allah that prevent faith.

This lesson examines the first issue of the section: how Sunni theologians understand Quranic terms such as الطبع, الختم, الأكنة, and الأقفال. The passage presents the Sunni position that these terms refer to the actual creation of misguidance in hearts, supported by a linguistic principle about prevention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
The passage is compact but clear.
2
Identify the four Quranic terms
The passage lists four specific terms from the Quran. Find each one and note the verse cited.
These are: الطبع, الختم, الأكنة, الأقفال.
3
Understand the Sunni claim
The Sunnis (أهل الحق) say these terms are literal expressions for the creation of misguidance. Notice the reasoning: the terms denote prevention, and misguidance prevents guidance.
4
Examine the linguistic argument
The passage argues that the original state (الأصل) is continuity (الاطراد) until a preventer appears. The Sunnis claim that creation of misguidance is such a preventer, so the terms apply literally.
This places the burden of proof on those who claim otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الطبع : قال الله تعالى {بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم} [النساء: 155] ( والختم ) {ختم الله على قلوبهم} [البقرة: 7] ، ( والأكنة ) {وجعلنا على قلوبهم أكنة أن يفقهوه} [الأنعام: 25]، ( ونحوها كالأفعال في قوله تعالى : {أم على قلوب أقفالها} [محمد: 24] ، فذهب أهل الحق إلى أنها عبارة عن خلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة، وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة وخلق الضلال في القلوب مانع من الهدى، فصح تسميته بهذه الأسماء لأن الأصل هو الاطراد إلى أن يمنع مانع والأصل عدمه، فمن ادعاه يحتاج إلى البيان والمعتزلة أولوها بوجوه : الأول : ( وهو لأوائل المعتزلة ) ختم الله على قلوبهم إلى آخر الآيات أي سماها مختوماً عليها ومطبوعاً عليها ومجعولاً عليها أكنة وأقفال ووصفها بذلك كما قال {وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً} [الزخرف: 19]، أي سموهم بذلك ووصفوهم بالأنوثة إذ لا قدرة لهم على الجعل الحقيقي . ( الثاني : ( وهو للجائي وابنه ومن تابعهما ) وسميها ) أي الله على قلوب الكافر ( بسمات ) وعلامات ) تعرفها الملائكة فيتميز بها الكافر عن المؤمن)، وذلك لأن الختم والطبع في اللغة هو الوسم ولا يمتنع أن يخلق الله في قلوب الفجار سمة تتميز بها عن قلوب الأبرار ونتبين تلك السمة للملائكة فيذمون من اتسم بها، وذلك في مصلحة دينية لأنه إذا علم العبد أنه إذا كفر وسم بسمة يتحقق بها ذمه ولعنه من الملائكة كان ذلك سبباً لانزجاره عنه. ( الثالث : ) وهو للكعبي (منع الله منهم اللطف المقرب إلى الطاعة ) المبعد عن المعصية ( لعلمه أنه ينفعهم ) ولا يؤثر فيهم ، ( فلما لم يوفقوا لذلك ) اللطف ( فكأنهم ختم على قلوبهم) لأن قطع اللطف مانع من دخول الإيمان كما أن الختم والطبع والأكنة والأقفال موانع من الدخول . ( الرابع ) وهو لبعض أصحاب عبد الواحد من المعتزلة (منعهم الله الإخلاص الموجب لقبول العمل فكانوا لذلك ) كمن يمنع دخول الإيمان قلبه بالختم عليه
قوله : ( لعلمه أنه لا ينفعهم إلخ فيه دلالة على أن المعتزلة وإن أوجبوا اللطف على الله تعالى لكن لا يوجبونه إذا علم أنه لا ينفعهم.
لأن الفعل بلا إخلاص كلا فعل وهو أي ما ذكروه من التأويل ( مع الابتناء على أصلهم أن منع الإيمان وخلق الضلال قبيح فلا يجوز إسناده إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى سيأتيك بيان فساده يبطله ذكر الله تعالى هذه الأشياء في معرض امتناع الإيمان منهم لأجل ذلك حيث قال : {سواء عليهم أأنذرتهم أم لم تنذرهم يؤمنون - ختم الله على قلوبهم} [البقرة: 6-7]، أي لا يؤمنون لأجل الختم وذلك لأن قوله : ختم استئناف لبيان السبب ( وشيء مما ذكرتم لا يصلح لذلك ) أي لكونه سبباً لامتناع الإيمان فإن مجرد الوصف بالختم والطبع وجعل الأكنة والأقفال على قلوبهم لا يمنع من الإيمان وكذا الوسم بعلامة مميزة، ومنع اللطف والإخلاص لا يقتضي امتناع الإيمان فلا يصح الحمل عليها .
First: 'al-tab'' (stamping): Allah said {But Allah has stamped them with their disbelief} [4:155]; (and 'al-khatm' (sealing)) {Allah has sealed their hearts} [2:7]; (and 'al-akinnah' (coverings)) {And We have placed upon their hearts coverings so that they do not understand it} [6:25]; (and similar things like 'al-aqfal' (locks) in His statement {Or are there locks upon their hearts?} [47:24]. Then the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) held that they are expressions for the creation of misguidance in the hearts, because these things in language are real preventions, and they are named thus because they are preventing, and the creation of misguidance in the hearts prevents guidance. So it is correct to name it with these names, because the original state is continuity until a preventer prevents, and the original is its absence, so whoever claims it needs proof. The Mu'tazila interpreted them in ways: The first (which is for the early Mu'tazila): 'Allah sealed their hearts' and the rest of the verses means He named them sealed, stamped, and made coverings and locks upon them, and described them as such, just as He said {And they make the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females} [43:19], i.e., they named them thus and described them with femininity, having no power to make them truly. The second (which is for al-Ja'i, his son, and their followers): 'and He named them' i.e., Allah placed on the hearts of the disbelievers marks and signs that the angels recognize, so the disbeliever is distinguished from the believer, because 'al-khatm' and 'al-tab'' in language are branding, and it is not impossible for Allah to create in the hearts of the wicked a mark by which they are distinguished from the hearts of the righteous, and We manifest that mark to the angels so they condemn whoever is marked with it. That is a religious benefit, because if a servant knows that if he disbelieves he is branded with a mark by which his condemnation and curse by the angels is realized, that becomes a deterrent for him. The third (which is for al-Ka'bi): 'Allah withholds from them the grace (al-lutf) that brings close to obedience' far from disobedience, 'because He knows that it would benefit them' and does not affect them, 'so when they were not granted that grace, it is as if their hearts were sealed', because cutting off the grace prevents entry of faith just as sealing, stamping, coverings, and locks prevent entry. The fourth (which is for some of the followers of 'Abd al-Wahid of the Mu'tazila): 'Allah withholds from them the sincerity (al-ikhlas) that necessitates acceptance of deeds, so they are like one whose heart is prevented from entering faith by sealing.' His statement: 'because He knows that it does not benefit them etc.' indicates that the Mu'tazila, though they obligate grace upon Allah, do not obligate it when He knows it would not benefit them. Because action without sincerity is like no action, and it (i.e., what they mentioned of interpretation) is based on their principle that preventing faith and creating misguidance is ugly, so it is not permissible to attribute it to Allah, may He be exalted. The proof of its corruption will come to you. It is refuted by Allah's mention of these things in the context of the impossibility of faith from them because of that, when He said: {It is the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them – they will not believe – Allah has sealed their hearts} [2:6-7], i.e., they do not believe because of the sealing, because His statement 'sealing' is a resumption to express the cause. 'And nothing of what you mentioned is suitable for that', i.e., for being a cause of the impossibility of faith, because mere description of sealing, stamping, and placing coverings and locks on their hearts does not prevent faith, and likewise branding with a distinguishing mark, and withholding grace and sincerity does not necessitate the impossibility of faith, so it is not correct to attribute it to them.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the first issue of the third section of the sixth observation: a group of Quranic terms that the Mu'tazila interpret metaphorically. It first lists the terms: الطبع (stamping), الختم (sealing), الأكنة (coverings), الأقفال (locks). Then it states the Sunni position (أهل الحق): these terms are real expressions for the creation of misguidance in the hearts, because in language these words denote real preventions. The author provides a linguistic argument: the original state of a heart is that it is disposed to guidance unless a preventer prevents; the Sunni claim that creation of misguidance is such a preventer, so the terms apply properly. Then the passage enumerates four Mu'tazili interpretations in order: 1) The early Mu'tazila: these verses merely describe the hearts as if sealed, similar to calling angels females – it is a metaphorical description, not a real act of sealing. 2) Al-Ja'i and his followers: Allah creates actual marks on the hearts visible to angels to distinguish believers from disbelievers; this serves a religious benefit of deterrence. 3) Al-Ka'bi: withholding of divine grace (اللطف) that would lead to obedience; since the grace is withheld, it is as if the hearts are sealed – this is also metaphorical. 4) Some followers of 'Abd al-Wahid: withholding of sincerity (الإخلاص) that makes deeds acceptable; thus they are like sealed hearts. After presenting these interpretations, the passage includes a brief note that the Mu'tazila only withhold grace when they know it will not benefit. Then the passage refutes all these interpretations: the Quran itself presents the sealing as the direct cause of unbelief in the verse {ختم الله على قلوبهم} as an explanation of why they do not believe. The Mu'tazili interpretations – mere description, marks, withholding of grace or sincerity – do not constitute a real prevention of faith, so they cannot be the cause mentioned in the Quran. Therefore the Sunni interpretation (real creation of misguidance) is correct.

Key Terms
الطبع
الختم
الأكنة
الأقفال
الضلال
أهل الحق

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Sunni claim that the terms 'طبع' and 'ختم' refer to real creation of misguidance?
A) Because the Quran uses them in other contexts
B) Because these words in language denote real prevention, and creation of misguidance prevents guidance
C) Because the Mu'tazila agree
D) Because the Prophet interpreted them that way

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that Quranic sealing terms are literal expressions for the divine creation of misguidance, supported by the linguistic principle of prevention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah places coverings on hearts so they do not understand, do you sense any place in your own heart that has become hard or resistant to guidance? What specific verse or truth do you find yourself avoiding?
?
The passage says the original state is continuity of guidance until a preventer prevents. What 'preventers' have you allowed into your life—habits, distractions, or sins—that may be sealing your heart from deeper faith? What will you remove today?
?
If the sealing terms are literal expressions for the creation of misguidance, then your faith is entirely dependent on Allah's grace. Does this thought fill you with humility and urgent supplication, or does it lull you into complacency? How will you actively seek His mercy to keep your heart open?

Divine Decree

Mu'tazili Interpretations and Their Refutation
Sanusi enumerates four Mu'tazili interpretations and then refutes them using the Quranic context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four Mu'tazili interpretations of the sealing verses and the Sunni refutation based on the Quranic context.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila interpreted sealing metaphorically, but the Quran itself shows that sealing is the real cause of unbelief.

This lesson presents the four Mu'tazili interpretations of the sealing verses, each attempting to avoid attributing the creation of misguidance to Allah. The passage then refutes all four by pointing to the Quranic verse (2:6-7) that presents sealing as the direct reason for disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the four interpretations
Read the Arabic passage from 'والمعتزلة أولوها' to the end. Note each interpretation is numbered.
The interpretations are attributed to specific Mu'tazili figures/schools.
2
Note the variety of views
Each interpretation differs: metaphorical description, visible marks, withholding grace, withholding sincerity. Observe the common thread: they avoid literal creation of misguidance.
3
Understand the common Mu'tazili principle
The passage notes that the Mu'tazila consider creating misguidance as ugly (قبيح). This is the theological motive behind their interpretations.
This principle is stated in the refutation section.
4
Read the refutation
The refutation uses Quran 2:6-7 to argue that the sealing is presented as the cause of unbelief. The Mu'tazili alternatives do not genuinely prevent faith.
The refutation is concise but decisive.
5
Connect to the Quranic evidence
The verse 'ختم الله على قلوبهم' is cited as a resumption (استئناف) that explains the cause of their unbelief. This shows that the sealing is real and effective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الطبع : قال الله تعالى {بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم} [النساء: 155] ( والختم ) {ختم الله على قلوبهم} [البقرة: 7] ، ( والأكنة ) {وجعلنا على قلوبهم أكنة أن يفقهوه} [الأنعام: 25]، ( ونحوها كالأفعال في قوله تعالى : {أم على قلوب أقفالها} [محمد: 24] ، فذهب أهل الحق إلى أنها عبارة عن خلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة، وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة وخلق الضلال في القلوب مانع من الهدى، فصح تسميته بهذه الأسماء لأن الأصل هو الاطراد إلى أن يمنع مانع والأصل عدمه، فمن ادعاه يحتاج إلى البيان والمعتزلة أولوها بوجوه : الأول : ( وهو لأوائل المعتزلة ) ختم الله على قلوبهم إلى آخر الآيات أي سماها مختوماً عليها ومطبوعاً عليها ومجعولاً عليها أكنة وأقفال ووصفها بذلك كما قال {وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً} [الزخرف: 19]، أي سموهم بذلك ووصفوهم بالأنوثة إذ لا قدرة لهم على الجعل الحقيقي . ( الثاني : ( وهو للجائي وابنه ومن تابعهما ) وسميها ) أي الله على قلوب الكافر ( بسمات ) وعلامات ) تعرفها الملائكة فيتميز بها الكافر عن المؤمن)، وذلك لأن الختم والطبع في اللغة هو الوسم ولا يمتنع أن يخلق الله في قلوب الفجار سمة تتميز بها عن قلوب الأبرار ونتبين تلك السمة للملائكة فيذمون من اتسم بها، وذلك في مصلحة دينية لأنه إذا علم العبد أنه إذا كفر وسم بسمة يتحقق بها ذمه ولعنه من الملائكة كان ذلك سبباً لانزجاره عنه. ( الثالث : ) وهو للكعبي (منع الله منهم اللطف المقرب إلى الطاعة ) المبعد عن المعصية ( لعلمه أنه ينفعهم ) ولا يؤثر فيهم ، ( فلما لم يوفقوا لذلك ) اللطف ( فكأنهم ختم على قلوبهم) لأن قطع اللطف مانع من دخول الإيمان كما أن الختم والطبع والأكنة والأقفال موانع من الدخول . ( الرابع ) وهو لبعض أصحاب عبد الواحد من المعتزلة (منعهم الله الإخلاص الموجب لقبول العمل فكانوا لذلك ) كمن يمنع دخول الإيمان قلبه بالختم عليه

لأن الفعل بلا إخلاص كلا فعل وهو أي ما ذكروه من التأويل ( مع الابتناء على أصلهم أن منع الإيمان وخلق الضلال قبيح فلا يجوز إسناده إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى سيأتيك بيان فساده يبطله ذكر الله تعالى هذه الأشياء في معرض امتناع الإيمان منهم لأجل ذلك حيث قال : {سواء عليهم أأنذرتهم أم لم تنذرهم يؤمنون - ختم الله على قلوبهم} [البقرة: 6-7]، أي لا يؤمنون لأجل الختم وذلك لأن قوله : ختم استئناف لبيان السبب ( وشيء مما ذكرتم لا يصلح لذلك ) أي لكونه سبباً لامتناع الإيمان فإن مجرد الوصف بالختم والطبع وجعل الأكنة والأقفال على قلوبهم لا يمنع من الإيمان وكذا الوسم بعلامة مميزة، ومنع اللطف والإخلاص لا يقتضي امتناع الإيمان فلا يصح الحمل عليها .

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are there 'interpretations' in your own life that make you feel better about sin but do not actually change your heart? What is the real barrier keeping you from submission?

Divine Decree

Three Arguments Against Interpreting Guidance as Creation
The author refutes interpreting guidance and enabling as creation or call, with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine guidance (huda) and enabling (tawfiq) cannot be reduced to the mere call to Islam (da'wa), based on three rational and scriptural arguments.

Core Idea
Guidance is not just an invitation; it is a divine gift.

This lesson examines three arguments from classical Sunni theology that refute the idea that Allah's guidance and enabling are simply the creation of guidance in a person or the universal call to Islam. These arguments draw on consensus, supplication, and linguistic convention to show that guidance is a specific, differentiating gift.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage uses three numbered arguments; notice the structure.
2
Understand the first argument: consensus
The author argues that the Ummah agrees people differ in receiving guidance and enabling, while the call to Islam is universal. Since the call does not differentiate, guidance must be something else.
3
Examine the second argument: supplication
We supplicate for guidance, but supplication is for what is not yet attained. The call is already present for everyone, so guidance cannot be the call.
This argument uses the daily prayer 'Guide us to the straight path'.
4
Analyze the third argument: praise
Being guided is a reason for praise, while being the recipient of a general call is not. Therefore, guidance cannot be the call.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider why these arguments matter for understanding divine action and human responsibility in Sunni theology.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن
المراشد وتيسير مقاصدها والزجر عن طريق الغواية، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وأما ثمود فهديناهم ﴾ [فصلت : ١٧ ]، إذ لا شبهة في امتناع حمله على خلق الهدى فيهم ) والذي يبطله ) أي هذا التأويل ( أمور : الأول : إجماع الأمة على اختلاف الناس فيهما ) أي في التوفيق والهداية فبعضهم موفق مهدي وبعضهم ليس كذلك ( والدعوة عامة ) لجميع الأمة ( لا اختلاف فيها فلا يصح تأويلهما بها ( الثاني : الدعاء بهما نحو اللهم اهدنا الصراط المسقتيم ﴾ [الفاتحة: ٦] اللهم وفقنا لما تحب وترضى، والطلب إنما يكون لما ليس بحاصل ( والدعوة ) المذكورة ( حاصلة فلا يتصور طلبها، ( واختلاف الناس ) ليس في الدعوة نفسها بل (في) وجود (الانتفاع بها وعدمه). الثالث : كونه مهدياً وموفقاً من صفات المدح يمدح بهما في المتعارف ( دون كونه مدعواً)، إذ لا يمدح به أصلاً فلا يصح حملها على الدعوة.
The sixth observation - the third objective: Matters explicitly mentioned in the Quran. The guides and facilitation of their objectives and deterrence from the path of error, as in His statement, the Exalted: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them' [Fussilat: 17], for there is no doubt in the impossibility of interpreting it as the creation of guidance in them. And what invalidates it, i.e., this interpretation, are matters: First: The consensus of the Ummah on the difference of people regarding them, i.e., regarding tawfiq (divine enabling) and hidaya (guidance), for some are given tawfiq and guided and some are not, while the call (da'wa) is general for all the Ummah with no difference in it, so it is not correct to interpret them by it. Second: Supplication (du'a) for them, such as 'Guide us to the straight path' [Al-Fatiha: 6] and 'O Allah, grant us tawfiq to what You love and are pleased with', and request is only for what is not attained, while the mentioned call is attained, so its request is inconceivable. And the difference among people is not in the call itself, but in the existence of benefit from it and its absence. Third: Being guided and given tawfiq are attributes of praise by which one is praised in common usage, unlike being called (mad'uww), for one is not praised by it at all, so it is not correct to interpret them as the call.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage refutes the interpretation of divine guidance (huda) and enabling (tawfiq) as merely the creation of guidance in the person, which is the view of some rationalists. The author presents three arguments against this interpretation from the context of the verse about Thamud. First: There is consensus (ijma') that people differ in receiving tawfiq and guidance (some are given success, some are not), whereas the call (da'wa) to Islam is general and undifferentiated for all. Therefore, guidance cannot be identical to the general call. Second: People supplicate for guidance and tawfiq, but supplication is only for something not yet attained. Since the call (da'wa) is already present for everyone, one would not ask for it. The actual difference is in benefiting from the call, not in the call itself. Third: Being 'guided' and 'given success' are terms of praise in common usage, whereas being 'called' (i.e., being the recipient of the da'wa) is not a basis for praise. Thus, interpreting guidance as the mere call would be linguistically inconsistent. The passage uses clear reasoning from consensus, practical religious practice, and linguistic convention.

Key Terms
هدى
توفيق
دعوة
مدح
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Guidance is merely being invited to Islam (the call/da'wa).
Correction: Guidance is a distinct, enabling gift from Allah that goes beyond the universal call; it is something for which people differ and are praised.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can't 'guidance' mean the call (da'wa)?
A) Because the call is not a supplication
B) Because supplication is for something not yet attained, but the call is already present
C) Because the call is general while guidance is specific
D) Because guidance is a created attribute

Key Takeaway
The three arguments show that divine guidance is a specific, differentiating gift that cannot be equated with the general call to Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own guidance (هدى) as a settled reality or as a daily need that you must beg Allah for? How often do you recite 'Ihdina as-sirat al-mustaqim' with the desperate awareness that without His tawfiq you are lost?
?
The third argument says being guided is praiseworthy, while mere exposure to the truth is not. Are you living as someone who has truly been gifted guidance, or are you coasting on the fact that you were born into or know Islam? What one habit will you change to prove you are not just called but truly guided?
?
If guidance were merely the call, then every Muslim who hears the Quran would be equally guided, but you know that is false. What is blocking you from benefiting from the call? Will you today ask Allah specifically for tawfiq to act on what you know?

The Ash'ari Position on the Fixed Term of Life
Defines ajal and asserts the Ash'ari position with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ajal (appointed term) and why the Ash'aris hold that the slain person dies at his predetermined time, not earlier or later.

Core Idea
The term of life is fixed by God's knowledge and cannot be changed.

The concept of ajal (term of life) is central to discussions of divine decree. The Ash'aris affirm that every person, including one who is killed, dies exactly at the time God has decreed. This lesson examines the definition of ajal and the scriptural evidence for this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and verse
Read the Arabic passage once straight through, then read the translation. Notice how the author defines ajal as the time known to God and immediately quotes the Quranic verse as evidence.
The verse from Surah al-Hijr asserts that no nation can hasten or delay its term.
2
Understand why the term cannot change
The author states that it is inconceivable for the decree to be changed by advance or delay. Reflect on why this must be so: God's knowledge is perfect and unchangeable.
3
Note the Ash'ari claim about the slain
The author explicitly says that the slain person dies at his appointed term. This contradicts the idea that the killer cuts short the victim's life. Consider the theological implication: the killer's action is within God's decree.

(The third) among the matters: the ajal (term of life), and it, in animals, is the time which God knows that it will die therein. The slain person, according to the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq), dies at his ajal which God decreed for him and knew that he would die at it, and his death is by His act, the Exalted. It is inconceivable that this decree be changed by advance or delay. He, the Exalted, said: 'No nation can outstrip its term, nor can they delay it' [Al-Hijr: 5] ... [garbled phrase] ... This answer, though not free of strain, but resorting to it for directing speech is not the first vial broken in Islam. His statement: 'so its request is not conceivable' – if you say: The likes of what was mentioned is only for seeking confirmation and continuity, I say: For seeking confirmation on the call, and it is apparent. His statement: 'nor can they anticipate it' is coordinated with the whole conditional and consequent, not with the consequent alone, for it is inconceivable to anticipate something at its coming, so there is no justification for restricting its negation by the condition. This is the well-known view, and we have mentioned in the glosses of al-Mutawwal that it is permissible to coordinate it with the consequent alone based on the meaning of His statement: 'they cannot delay it by an hour, nor can they anticipate it' [Yunus: 49] – they cannot change it, on the pattern of His statement: 'and not a moist thing nor a dry thing but it is in a clear Book' [Al-An'am: 59], and from this category is their saying: 'I spoke to him and he replied with neither black nor white.' And it may be answered regarding the inference from the two verses by the possibility that by ajal in them is meant the fixed ajal, so it does not contradict the division of ajal into fixed and suspended. And you are aware that it is a specification without evidence and necessity. If you say: His statement: 'Then He decreed a term and a named term' [Al-An'am: 2] indicates multiple terms, because when a definite noun is repeated indefinitely, the second is other than the first. I say: This is denied because of His statement: 'And it is He who is God in the heaven and God on the earth' [Al-Zukhruf: 84], and His statement: 'And they said: Why is not a sign sent down to him from his Lord? Say: Indeed God is able to send down a sign' [Al-An'am: 37], and the like of this is more than can be counted. And if it is granted, perhaps what is meant by them is the term of this world and the term of the Hereafter.

Commentary
This passage defines ajal (the appointed term of life) and defends the Ash'ari view that the slain person dies exactly at his predetermined time, neither earlier nor later. It begins by stating that ajal is the time known only to God, and the person's death is by God's act, not the killer's. The author then quotes the Qur'anic verse asserting that no nation can hasten or delay its term. A garbled phrase follows, perhaps a marginal note about the usage of the verse. Then the author addresses a grammatical point: the coordination of 'nor can they anticipate it' in the verse. The common view is that it is joined to the whole conditional clause, but the author notes an alternative analysis allowing it to be joined to the consequent alone. The passage then responds to an objection: the request for guidance (mentioned earlier) might be for confirmation (tathbit) and continuity, not for acquisition. The answer is that it is for confirmation on the call (da'wa), which is already present. Next, the author deals with an objection from two verses that might imply a distinction between a fixed and a suspended term. He argues this interpretation is an arbitrary specification without proof. Finally, he addresses the objection from the verse 'then He decreed a term and a named term' which seems to indicate two terms. The response is that the repetition of an indefinite noun does not necessarily imply two distinct referents, citing examples from the Qur'an. If it did, the two terms could refer to the term of this world and the term of the Hereafter. Throughout, the author maintains the Ash'ari position that the term of life is singular and fixed, not subject to change.

Misconception: Some believe the killer shortens the victim's life by causing early death.
Correction: According to the Ash'aris, the victim's term is fixed and unchangeable; the killer's act does not alter it. The victim dies exactly at God's appointed time.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm that ajal, the term of life, is exactly as God knows it; the slain person dies at that predetermined time, and no change is possible.

Mu'tazilite Position and the Blame Argument
Mu'tazilite claim and first proof from blame

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that the killer's act generates death and their first proof based on the killer's blameworthiness.

Core Idea
The killer's blame proves he is the true agent of death according to Mu'tazilites.

The Mu'tazilites present a reasoned challenge to the Ash'ari view that all deaths are by God's decree. They assert that the killer's act directly generates death, and they support this with a powerful intuition: if the killer were not the real cause, he would deserve no blame. This lesson introduces their core position and this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the terms 'generation' (tawallud) and 'necessity' (darura).
The Mu'tazilites consider the killer's act the direct cause of death.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is: if the death were by God's decree, the killer would have no causal role and should not be blamed. But the killer is blamed, so the death must be his act.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the strength of the proof
Think about why the Mu'tazilites think this is a necessary (daruri) judgment. The author of the matn will later critique this.
The proof relies on the obviousness of blame.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the killer is the true agent, then God's predestination (qadar) would be limited. This is a major theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة قالوا : بل تولد موته من فعل القاتل فهو من أفعاله لا من فعل الله تعالى ( و ) قالوا : ( إنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أمد هو أجله ) الذي قدره الله تعالى له فالقاتل عندهم غير بالتقديم الأجل الذي قدره الله تعالى له ) وادعوا فيه ) أي في تولده من فعل القاتل وبقائه لولا القتل (الضرورة) كما ادعوها في تولد سائر المتولدات وانتفائها عند انتفاء أسبابها ) واستشهدوا عليه بذم القاتل) والحكم بكونه جانياً ( ولو كان ( المقتول ( ميتاً بأجله ) الذي قدره الله له (لمات وإن لم يقتله فهو ) أي القاتل ( لم يجلب ) حينئذ ( بفعله أمراً لا مباشرة ولا توليداً فكان لا يستحق الذم) عقلاً ولا شرعاً لكنه مذموم فيهما قطعاً، إذا كان القتل بغير حق (و) استشهدوا أيضاً ( بأنه ربما قتل في الملحمة الواحدة ألوف ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أن موت الجم الغفير في الزمان القليل بلا قتل مما تحكم العادة بامتناعه ولذلك ) أي ولحكم العادة بالامتناع في الخلق الكثير دون غيره (ذهبت جماعة منهم إلى أن ما لا يخالف العادة كما في قتل واحد وما يقرب منه ( واقع بالأجل منسوب إلى القاتل والفرق غير بين في العقل)، لأن الموت في كلتا الصورتين متولد من فعل القاتل عندهم فلما إذا كان أحدهما بأجله دون الآخر ) ولولا روم الهرب من الإلزام الشنيع وهو القدح في المعجزات لما قالوا (به وبيان ذلك أنه لما حكمت العادة بامتناع موت خلق كثير دفعة امتنع أن ينسب موتهم بقتلهم في ساعة إلى الله تعالى وإلا كان فعلاً منه خارقاً
قوله لما قالوا (به قدر جواب لو لئلا يتوهم تعلقها بماقبلها فإنه فاسد من جهة المعنى ولذا لم يقدره من جنس ما قبلها . قوله : ( وبيان ذلك أنه لما حكم بالعادة إلخ) المفهوم من هذا البيان هو أنهم لم ينسبوا الموت في كلتا الصورتين إليه تعالى بل فرقوا بأن نسبوا موت الجم الغفير بقتلهم في ساعة إلى القاتل ليندفع عنه شبهة القدح في المعجزات ونسبوا موت جماعة قليلة في لحظة إليه سبحانه لعدم المحذور في هذه النسبة وهو القدح في المعجزات لكونه غير خارق للعادة، ولولا ذلك المحذور لنسبوا الكل إليه وفيه نظر لأن هذا شرح لا يطابق المشروح فإن المفهوم من قول المصنف ذهب جماعة منهم إلى أن ما لا يخالف العادة واقع بالأجل منسوب إلى القاتل ومن سياق كلامه إلى هذا القول أنهم قائلون بأن النسبة في كلتا الصورتين إلى القاتل غاية الأمر أن الموت في صورة موت الجماعة القليلة في لحظة واقع بالأجل وفي صورة الجم الغفير لا بالأجل وهذا الفرق تحكم بجت لا يدعو إليه داع إذ لا يلزم القدح في المعجزات على تقدير انتفائه لأنه لما كان موت الجم الغفير منسوباً عندهم إلى القاتل لم يكن فعلاً لله تعالى سواء كان بالأجل أو لا بالأجل فيلزم القدح ليحتاج إلى دفعه بالفرق بكون الموت في إحدى الصورتين بالأجل وفي الأخرى لا به، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إذا كان موت الجم الغفير في ساعة بالأجل يكون لله تعالى دخل فيه في الجملة وإن كان منسوباً إلى القاتل فيلزمهم الإلزام ففرقوا ليندفع عنهم الإلزام، وأنت خبير
And the Mu'tazilites said: Rather, his (the slain person's) death is generated (mutawallid) from the act of the killer, so it is from his acts, not from the act of God the Exalted. And they said: If he had not been killed, he would have lived to a limit which is his ajal that God the Exalted decreed for him. So the killer, according to them, changes by advancement the ajal which God the Exalted decreed for him. And they claimed regarding it, i.e., regarding its generation from the killer's act and its remaining had it not been for the killing, necessity (darura), just as they claimed it in the generation of other generated things and their disappearance upon the disappearance of their causes. And they adduced in support of it the blame of the killer and the judgment that he is a wrongdoer. For if the slain person died at his ajal which God decreed for him, he (the killed person) would have died even if he had not killed him, so the killer would not have brought about by his act anything directly or by generation, and thus he would deserve no blame, rationally or legally, but he is blameworthy in both unequivocally, when the killing is without right. And they also adduced that sometimes thousands are killed in one battle, and we know with necessity that the death of a multitude in a short time without killing is something that custom (ada) judges impossible. Therefore, a group of them went to hold that what does not violate custom, as in a single killing or something close to it, occurs by ajal and is attributed to the killer, and the difference is not clear in reason, because death in both forms is generated from the killer's act according to them. So why is one of them by his ajal and the other not? And were it not for seeking escape from the shameful implication (ilzam) which is casting doubt on miracles (al-qadḥ fi al-mu'jizat), they would not have said it. And the explanation of that is that when custom judged the impossibility of the death of a great many at once, it becomes impossible to attribute their death by killing them in an hour to God the Exalted, for otherwise it would be a miraculous act from Him. His statement: 'when they said it' is the answer to 'law' (if) so that one might not think its connection to what precedes it, for it is corrupt in meaning, so he did not interpret it as of the same genus as what precedes it. His statement: 'and the explanation of that is that when custom judged...' The understood from this explanation is that they did not attribute death in both forms to Him the Exalted, but rather they differentiated by attributing the death of the multitude by killing them in an hour to the killer to ward off the suspicion of casting doubt on miracles, and they attributed the death of a small group in a moment to Him, the Exalted, because there is no harm in this attribution, which is casting doubt on miracles, because it is not customary-breaking. And were it not for that harm, they would have attributed all to Him. But there is consideration, because this explanation does not match the explained. For the understanding from the author's statement: 'A group of them went to hold that what does not violate custom occurs by ajal and is attributed to the killer' and from the context of his speech to this statement is that they are saying that the attribution in both forms is to the killer, the only difference being that the death in the case of the death of a small group in a moment occurs by ajal, and in the case of the multitude not by ajal. And this difference is a pure arbitrary judgement (taḥakkum) with no cause calling for it, because casting doubt on miracles does not follow on the supposition of its absence, because when the death of the multitude is attributed by them to the killer, it is not an act of God the Exalted, whether it is by ajal or not by ajal. So the casting doubt would have to follow to need to ward it off by the difference that the death in one of the two forms is by ajal and in the other not. Unless it is said: If the death of the multitude in an hour is by ajal, then God the Exalted has some involvement in it in general, even if attributed to the killer, then the implication (ilzam) would apply to them, so they differentiated to ward off the implication from themselves. And you are aware.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position on the term of life (ajal). The Mu'tazilites argue that the killer's act generates death, making it the killer's act, not God's. They claim that without the killing, the person would have lived to his appointed term, so the killer advances the term. They invoke necessity (darura) for this generation, similar to other generated effects. Their first proof is the blame and legal wrongdoing of the killer: if the death were by God's decree as the Ash'aris hold, the killer would deserve no blame, but he is blameworthy. Second, they point to mass killings: the death of thousands in a short time is naturally impossible without killing, so it must be attributed to the killer, not God, to avoid claiming a miracle. Some Mu'tazilites then distinguish: small-scale killings (within normal custom) occur by ajal and are attributed to the killer, while large-scale killings (custom-breaking) are not by ajal. The author criticizes this as arbitrary (taḥakkum) and motivated only by avoiding the implication that miracles would otherwise be denied. The commentary explains that the Mu'tazilite distinction is unsound: if both types of death are generated from the killer, the attribution to the killer is the same; the issue of casting doubt on miracles only arises if God were directly causing the mass death. The author concludes with a nuanced point: if the large-scale death is by ajal, then God would have some involvement, which would force the Mu'tazilites to differentiate to escape that implication. The passage demonstrates careful dialectical engagement with the opponent's arguments.

تولد
ضرورة
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that blame proves the killer is the sole cause, ignoring God's ultimate sovereignty.
Correction: The Ash'ari response is that blame can be based on intention and wrong use of free will, even if God creates the death.

Checkpoint

B) The killer deserves blame

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites use the blame intuition to argue that the killer, not God, is the real agent of death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson unsettle your natural instinct to assign ultimate responsibility? Will you now pause and reflect before blaming, recognizing that causation is a theological claim, not just a moral one?
?

Are you willing to forgive someone even when you believe they 'caused' harm, knowing that ultimately all acts are from Allah? How does this reshape your daily interactions and your heart's relationship with the people around you?

Mu'tazilite Second Proof from Custom and Their Distinction
Mu'tazilite second proof and their ad hoc distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite second proof from mass killings and their distinction between small-scale and large-scale killings regarding the term (ajal).

Core Idea
Custom dictates that mass death without killing is impossible, so it must be attributed to the killer, but then they make an arbitrary distinction.

The Mu'tazilites strengthen their case by pointing to mass killings: thousands die in battles, which would be impossible naturally. They argue this proves death is generated from the killer's act. However, some Mu'tazilites then distinguish between small-scale killings (which occur by ajal) and large-scale ones (which do not). This lesson covers these arguments and sets up the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from custom
Notice how the Mu'tazilites use the uniformity of nature (custom) to claim that mass death without killing is impossible, so killing must be the cause.
This is an argument from necessity.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite distinction
Some Mu'tazilites say: killings that do not violate custom (like a single death) occur by ajal, but mass killings (custom-breaking) are not by ajal, yet both are attributed to the killer.
The distinction is based on custom-breaking.
3
Identify the motivation
The author points out that this distinction is made to avoid an embarrassing implication: that mass death attributed to God would be a miracle and thus cast doubt on miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة قالوا : بل تولد موته من فعل القاتل فهو من أفعاله لا من فعل الله تعالى ( و ) قالوا : ( إنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أمد هو أجله ) الذي قدره الله تعالى له فالقاتل عندهم غير بالتقديم الأجل الذي قدره الله تعالى له ) وادعوا فيه ) أي في تولده من فعل القاتل وبقائه لولا القتل (الضرورة) كما ادعوها في تولد سائر المتولدات وانتفائها عند انتفاء أسبابها ) واستشهدوا عليه بذم القاتل) والحكم بكونه جانياً ( ولو كان ( المقتول ( ميتاً بأجله ) الذي قدره الله له (لمات وإن لم يقتله فهو ) أي القاتل ( لم يجلب ) حينئذ ( بفعله أمراً لا مباشرة ولا توليداً فكان لا يستحق الذم) عقلاً ولا شرعاً لكنه مذموم فيهما قطعاً، إذا كان القتل بغير حق (و) استشهدوا أيضاً ( بأنه ربما قتل في الملحمة الواحدة ألوف ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أن موت الجم الغفير في الزمان القليل بلا قتل مما تحكم العادة بامتناعه ولذلك ) أي ولحكم العادة بالامتناع في الخلق الكثير دون غيره (ذهبت جماعة منهم إلى أن ما لا يخالف العادة كما في قتل واحد وما يقرب منه ( واقع بالأجل منسوب إلى القاتل والفرق غير بين في العقل)، لأن الموت في كلتا الصورتين متولد من فعل القاتل عندهم فلما إذا كان أحدهما بأجله دون الآخر ) ولولا روم الهرب من الإلزام الشنيع وهو القدح في المعجزات لما قالوا (به وبيان ذلك أنه لما حكمت العادة بامتناع موت خلق كثير دفعة امتنع أن ينسب موتهم بقتلهم في ساعة إلى الله تعالى وإلا كان فعلاً منه خارقاً
قوله لما قالوا (به قدر جواب لو لئلا يتوهم تعلقها بماقبلها فإنه فاسد من جهة المعنى ولذا لم يقدره من جنس ما قبلها . قوله : ( وبيان ذلك أنه لما حكم بالعادة إلخ) المفهوم من هذا البيان هو أنهم لم ينسبوا الموت في كلتا الصورتين إليه تعالى بل فرقوا بأن نسبوا موت الجم الغفير بقتلهم في ساعة إلى القاتل ليندفع عنه شبهة القدح في المعجزات ونسبوا موت جماعة قليلة في لحظة إليه سبحانه لعدم المحذور في هذه النسبة وهو القدح في المعجزات لكونه غير خارق للعادة، ولولا ذلك المحذور لنسبوا الكل إليه وفيه نظر لأن هذا شرح لا يطابق المشروح فإن المفهوم من قول المصنف ذهب جماعة منهم إلى أن ما لا يخالف العادة واقع بالأجل منسوب إلى القاتل ومن سياق كلامه إلى هذا القول أنهم قائلون بأن النسبة في كلتا الصورتين إلى القاتل غاية الأمر أن الموت في صورة موت الجماعة القليلة في لحظة واقع بالأجل وفي صورة الجم الغفير لا بالأجل وهذا الفرق تحكم بجت لا يدعو إليه داع إذ لا يلزم القدح في المعجزات على تقدير انتفائه لأنه لما كان موت الجم الغفير منسوباً عندهم إلى القاتل لم يكن فعلاً لله تعالى سواء كان بالأجل أو لا بالأجل فيلزم القدح ليحتاج إلى دفعه بالفرق بكون الموت في إحدى الصورتين بالأجل وفي الأخرى لا به، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إذا كان موت الجم الغفير في ساعة بالأجل يكون لله تعالى دخل فيه في الجملة وإن كان منسوباً إلى القاتل فيلزمهم الإلزام ففرقوا ليندفع عنهم الإلزام، وأنت خبير
And the Mu'tazilites said: Rather, his (the slain person's) death is generated (mutawallid) from the act of the killer, so it is from his acts, not from the act of God the Exalted. And they said: If he had not been killed, he would have lived to a limit which is his ajal that God the Exalted decreed for him. So the killer, according to them, changes by advancement the ajal which God the Exalted decreed for him. And they claimed regarding it, i.e., regarding its generation from the killer's act and its remaining had it not been for the killing, necessity (darura), just as they claimed it in the generation of other generated things and their disappearance upon the disappearance of their causes. And they adduced in support of it the blame of the killer and the judgment that he is a wrongdoer. For if the slain person died at his ajal which God decreed for him, he (the killed person) would have died even if he had not killed him, so the killer would not have brought about by his act anything directly or by generation, and thus he would deserve no blame, rationally or legally, but he is blameworthy in both unequivocally, when the killing is without right. And they also adduced that sometimes thousands are killed in one battle, and we know with necessity that the death of a multitude in a short time without killing is something that custom (ada) judges impossible. Therefore, a group of them went to hold that what does not violate custom, as in a single killing or something close to it, occurs by ajal and is attributed to the killer, and the difference is not clear in reason, because death in both forms is generated from the killer's act according to them. So why is one of them by his ajal and the other not? And were it not for seeking escape from the shameful implication (ilzam) which is casting doubt on miracles (al-qadḥ fi al-mu'jizat), they would not have said it. And the explanation of that is that when custom judged the impossibility of the death of a great many at once, it becomes impossible to attribute their death by killing them in an hour to God the Exalted, for otherwise it would be a miraculous act from Him. His statement: 'when they said it' is the answer to 'law' (if) so that one might not think its connection to what precedes it, for it is corrupt in meaning, so he did not interpret it as of the same genus as what precedes it. His statement: 'and the explanation of that is that when custom judged...' The understood from this explanation is that they did not attribute death in both forms to Him the Exalted, but rather they differentiated by attributing the death of the multitude by killing them in an hour to the killer to ward off the suspicion of casting doubt on miracles, and they attributed the death of a small group in a moment to Him, the Exalted, because there is no harm in this attribution, which is casting doubt on miracles, because it is not customary-breaking. And were it not for that harm, they would have attributed all to Him. But there is consideration, because this explanation does not match the explained. For the understanding from the author's statement: 'A group of them went to hold that what does not violate custom occurs by ajal and is attributed to the killer' and from the context of his speech to this statement is that they are saying that the attribution in both forms is to the killer, the only difference being that the death in the case of the death of a small group in a moment occurs by ajal, and in the case of the multitude not by ajal. And this difference is a pure arbitrary judgement (taḥakkum) with no cause calling for it, because casting doubt on miracles does not follow on the supposition of its absence, because when the death of the multitude is attributed by them to the killer, it is not an act of God the Exalted, whether it is by ajal or not by ajal. So the casting doubt would have to follow to need to ward it off by the difference that the death in one of the two forms is by ajal and in the other not. Unless it is said: If the death of the multitude in an hour is by ajal, then God the Exalted has some involvement in it in general, even if attributed to the killer, then the implication (ilzam) would apply to them, so they differentiated to ward off the implication from themselves. And you are aware.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position on the term of life (ajal). The Mu'tazilites argue that the killer's act generates death, making it the killer's act, not God's. They claim that without the killing, the person would have lived to his appointed term, so the killer advances the term. They invoke necessity (darura) for this generation, similar to other generated effects. Their first proof is the blame and legal wrongdoing of the killer: if the death were by God's decree as the Ash'aris hold, the killer would deserve no blame, but he is blameworthy. Second, they point to mass killings: the death of thousands in a short time is naturally impossible without killing, so it must be attributed to the killer, not God, to avoid claiming a miracle. Some Mu'tazilites then distinguish: small-scale killings (within normal custom) occur by ajal and are attributed to the killer, while large-scale killings (custom-breaking) are not by ajal. The author criticizes this as arbitrary (taḥakkum) and motivated only by avoiding the implication that miracles would otherwise be denied. The commentary explains that the Mu'tazilite distinction is unsound: if both types of death are generated from the killer, the attribution to the killer is the same; the issue of casting doubt on miracles only arises if God were directly causing the mass death. The author concludes with a nuanced point: if the large-scale death is by ajal, then God would have some involvement, which would force the Mu'tazilites to differentiate to escape that implication. The passage demonstrates careful dialectical engagement with the opponent's arguments.

Key Terms
ملحمة
عادة
معجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the distinction is logically necessary, but it is actually an ad hoc move to avoid a theological difficulty.
Correction: If both types of death are generated from the killer's act, there is no need to differentiate regarding ajal; it is a weak argument.

Checkpoint
Why do some Mu'tazilites distinguish between small-scale and large-scale killings?
A) Because the Quran distinguishes them
B) To avoid the implication that God performs miracles
C) Because large-scale killings are not really deaths
D) Because custom distinguishes them automatically

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' distinction based on custom is an ad hoc defense to avoid a theological problem, not a rational conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a large-scale tragedy, do you instinctively search for a human cause to make sense of it? How does the Mu'tazilite dilemma—attributing death to a killer versus to God—challenge your own default explanation? Would you be willing to see every death, even in war, as directly from Allah's decree?
?
The Mu'tazilites made a forced distinction to avoid an uncomfortable conclusion. In your own life, are there beliefs or explanations you hold onto that are actually ad hoc defenses against admitting God's absolute control? Can you identify one area where you resist full submission to qadar?
?
If all events, including mass death, are from Allah, how does that reshape your reaction when you face personal tragedy? Will you practice saying 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' with a heart that truly trusts His wisdom, even when you cannot see the cause?

Author's Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Distinction
Author's critique of the arbitrary distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's detailed critique of the Mu'tazilite distinction between small and large killings, revealing its arbitrariness.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite distinction is arbitrary: if both deaths are from the killer, the ajal attribution is irrelevant.

The author of the sharh provides a careful analysis of the Mu'tazilite position, showing that their distinction between small-scale and large-scale killings is not supported by reason. He argues that if both types of death are generated from the killer's act, as the Mu'tazilites claim, then the attribution to the killer is the same, and the issue of ajal does not affect the blame or the generation. The only reason for the distinction is to avoid a logical consequence, but it remains a pure arbitrary judgment (taḥakkum).

Guided Path
1
Read the author's explanation
The author explains the Mu'tazilite reasoning: they attribute mass death to the killer to avoid miracles, and small-scale death to God because it doesn't break custom.
Note the author's careful parsing of their position.
2
Identify the inconsistency
The author notes that the Mu'tazilites actually attribute both types to the killer (according to the matn), but then they differentiate by ajal. This is contradictory.
If both are from the killer, ajal doesn't change attribution.
3
Understand the charge of taḥakkum
The author calls this distinction a pure arbitrary judgment (taḥakkum) with no rational basis.
The threat of casting doubt on miracles does not arise if the death is attributed to the killer.
4
Consider the final nuance
The author concedes that if the mass death is by ajal, God would have some involvement, and then the implication would apply, so the Mu'tazilites had to differentiate. But this still shows their position is unstable.

What does the author consider the Mu'tazilite distinction between small and large killings regarding ajal?

Divine Decree

Reply to the Foolishness Objection: Three Aspects
Al-Jurjani replies to the foolishness objection with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how commanding contrary to will can be rationally justified by purposes beyond the mere occurrence of the commanded act, as demonstrated through three everyday examples.

Core Idea
Commanding contrary to will is not foolish if it serves a higher purpose like testing or justice.

Al-Jurjani responds to the objection that if God wills all things, then commanding faith while willing unbelief would be foolish. He argues that foolishness only follows if the sole purpose of commanding is to bring about the act. He then provides three real-world examples where commanding contrary to will serves other legitimate purposes, thus showing the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and the key premise
Read the opening lines to grasp the objection: that if God wills all things, commanding contrary to that will would be foolish. Notice Al-Jurjani's initial reply: the charge of foolishness only holds if the purpose of command is limited to enacting the commanded act.
The premise that foolishness is confined to commands where the sole purpose is the act's occurrence is crucial.
2
Examine the first example: the master testing his slave
Consider the scenario where a master commands his slave to test his obedience. Here, the command is genuine, but the master's true purpose is the test, not necessarily the act. If the slave obeys or disobeys, the test is achieved, so no foolishness.
This shows that a command can have a purpose beyond the act itself.
3
Examine the second example: the king and the slave's beater
Analyze the scenario where a king commands an act as a pretext to provide an excuse for punishment, while actually willing the opposite (disobedience). This serves justice, not foolishness. Then consider the counter-objection about the form versus reality of command, and its refutation.
The counter-objection is addressed by noting that a rational person may command knowing the act will not occur if there is benefit in the command itself.
4
Examine the third example: the compelled command
Look at the case where one is compelled to command (e.g., by necessity) and does not will the performance of the act, but rather its opposite. This is also not considered foolish, as the command serves another purpose.
This further illustrates that commands can be divorced from the will of the commander without foolishness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات فيلزم السفه في أحكام الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، قلنا: لانسلم أن الآمر بخلاف ما يريده يعد سفيهاً وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان الغرض من الأمر منحصراً في إيقاع المأمور به يوضحه وجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن الممتحن لعبده هل يطيعه أم لا قد يأمره ولا يريد منه الفعل، أما الأول وهو أن الصادر منه أمر حقيقة فلأنه إذا أتى العبد بالفعل يقال: امتثل أمر سيده، وأما الثاني: وهو أنه لا يريد الفعل منه يحصل مقصوده وهو الامتحان أطاع أو عصى فلا سفه في الأمر بما لا يريده الأمر. الثاني : إنه إذا عاتب الملك ضارب عبده فاعتذر بعصيانه والملك يتوعده بالقتل إن لم يظهر عصيانه فإنه يأمره بفعل تمهيداً لعذره، ويرد عصيانه فيه، فإن أحداً لا يريد ما يفضي إلى قتله بل ما يخلصه عنه فقد أمر بخلاف ما يريده ولا سفه. فإن قيل: الموجود هاهنا صورة الأمر لا حقيقته فإن العاقل لا يأمر بما يؤدي حصوله إلى هلاكه. أجيب: بأنه قد يأمر به إذا علم أنه لا يحصل وكان في الأمر به فائدة بخلاف الإرادة فإنها لا تتعلق به أصلاً. الثالث : إن الملجأ إلى الأمر قد يأمر ولا يريد فعل المأمور به بل يريد خلافه ولا يعد سفيهاً.
The Sixth Observation - The Fourth Purpose: That He, exalted, is the willer of all contingent things. So foolishness would follow in the judgments of God—exalted is He above that, greatly. We said: We do not accept that the one who commands contrary to what He wills is considered foolish; rather, that would only be if the purpose of the command was confined to the occurrence of the commanded act. It is clarified by three aspects: First, that the examiner of his slave whether he obeys him or not may command him and not will the act from him. As for the first point—that what issues from him is truly a command—because if the slave performs the act, it is said: He obeyed his master's command. And as for the second—that he does not will the act from him—his purpose (which is the test, whether he obeys or disobeys) is achieved. So there is no foolishness in commanding what he does not will. Second: That if a king rebukes the beater of his slave and the beater excuses himself by the slave's disobedience, and the king threatens him with death if his disobedience does not appear, then he commands him to perform an act as a pretext for his excuse, and he wishes for his disobedience in it. For no one wills what leads to his death, but rather what saves him from it. So he has commanded contrary to what he wills, and there is no foolishness. If it is said: What exists here is the form of command, not its reality, for a rational person does not command what leads to his destruction. The answer: That he may command it when he knows that it will not occur, and there is benefit in commanding it, unlike will which is not attached to it at all. Third: That one who is compelled to command may command and not will the performance of the commanded act, but rather will its opposite, and he is not considered foolish.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the Ash'ari doctrine that God wills all contingent things, including unbelief, and the reply using three illustrative examples. The objection claims that if God commands faith but wills unbelief, then He is acting foolishly (safah), since commanding something one does not will is futile. The reply first establishes that foolishness only follows if the sole purpose of commanding is to bring about the commanded act. The three aspects show that commanding contrary to will can serve other purposes. The first aspect: a master tests his slave by commanding him, but his true purpose is to see whether the slave obeys, not necessarily to have the act done. If the slave obeys, he has obeyed the command; if not, the master still achieves his test. So commanding without willing the act is not foolish. The second aspect: a king may command a slave to do something that will provide an excuse for punishment, while actually willing that the slave disobey, so that the punishment is deserved. This is not foolish because it serves justice. The third aspect: one compelled to command (e.g., by necessity) may issue a command without willing its performance, aiming at another benefit. In each case, the command is genuine but the will is absent, yet no foolishness follows. The reply also addresses a possible objection: that such cases involve only the form of command, not its reality. The answer is that a rational person may command knowing the act will not occur, if there is benefit in the command itself—unlike will, which cannot attach to an impossible object.

Key Terms
السفه
الأمر
الإرادة
الممتحن
الامتحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that commanding without willing the commanded act is always futile and therefore foolish.
Correction: The passage shows that commands can serve purposes beyond the act itself, such as testing, providing excuses, or fulfilling necessities, so they are not foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect, what is the master's true purpose in commanding his slave?
A) To make the slave perform the act.
B) To test whether the slave obeys.
C) To punish the slave.
D) To fulfill his own need.

Key Takeaway
Commanding contrary to one's will is not inherently foolish; the rationality of a command depends on its purpose, which may extend beyond the mere performance of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey a command of God that seems clear, do you ever consider that His command is still real and binding, even if His will allowed your disobedience for a greater purpose? How does that change how you approach repentance?
?
Are you willing to trust God's wisdom when you cannot see the purpose behind His commands? This passage shows that even human commanders have purposes beyond the obvious. Can you surrender your need to always understand 'why' and simply obey?

The Objection and the Core Distinction
Al-Jurjani presents the second objection and its refutation by distinguishing obedience from will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari view on divine will and the response that distinguishes obedience from will.

Core Idea
Obedience is conformity to command, not conformity to will.

The second objection attempts to show that if God wills all acts, then even unbelief would be obedience. Al-Jurjani refutes this by clarifying that obedience is defined by command, not will. This distinction is crucial for understanding Ash'ari theology on human action and divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: if God wills unbelief, then the act of the unbeliever conforms to God's will, so it would be obedience.
Notice that this objection relies on equating obedience with conformity to will.
2
Identify the reply
Find Al-Jurjani's response: obedience is conformity to command, not will. Command and will are distinct and can separate.
This is the core distinction that defuses the objection.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why command and will can separate. The previous examples (e.g., commanding what one does not will) illustrate this.
Understanding this distinction is key to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من وجوه استدلالاتهم لو كان الكفار مراد الله تعالى لكان فعله والإتيان به موافقة لمراد الله تعالى فيكون طاعة به وإنه باطل ضرورة من الدين قلنا : الطاعة موافقة الأمر، والأمر غير الإرادة وغير مستلزم لها لانفكاكها عنه في الصور المذكورة، قال الآمدي : ويدل على أن موافقة الإرادة ليست طاعة أنه لو أراد شخص شيئاً من آخر فوقع المراد من الآخر على وفق إرادة المريد ولا شعور للفاعل بإرادته فإنه لا يعد منه طاعة له كيف والإرادة كامنة والأمر ظاهر ولهذا يقال في العرف : فلان مطاع الأمر ولا يقال : مطاع الإرادة وقد ضايق بعض أصحابنا في العبارة فقال : الكفر مراد بالكافر غير مراد من الكافر، لأن القول الثاني ينبئ عن الرضاء بالكفر دون الأول وهو لفظي لا طائل تحته
Second: From the aspects of their arguments: If the unbelievers were willed by God, then their act and their doing it would be in accordance with God's will, and thus it would be obedience by it, and that is false by necessity of religion. We said: Obedience is conformity to command, and command is other than will and does not entail it, because they separate from each other in the aforementioned cases. Al-Amidi said: That conformity to will is not obedience is indicated by the fact that if a person wills something from another, and the willed object occurs from the other in accordance with the willer's will, but the actor has no awareness of his will, then it is not considered obedience from him to him. For will is hidden and command is apparent, and for this reason it is said in custom: 'So-and-so is obeyed in command,' but it is not said: 'So-and-so is obeyed in will.' And some of our colleagues were precise in expression, saying: Unbelief is willed in the unbeliever, but not willed from the unbeliever, because the second expression implies approval of unbelief while the first does not, and it is a verbal matter with no substance.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its reply. The objection argues that if God wills all things, including unbelief, then the acts of unbelievers would be in accordance with God's will, and hence would count as obedience—which is impossible by religious consensus. The reply distinguishes between obedience and will: obedience is defined as conformity to command (amr), not to will (irada). Command and will are different; one can command without willing (as shown in the previous examples). Al-Amidi's proof: If someone wills an action from another, and that action occurs without the agent's knowledge of the will, it is not obedience—because will is hidden, whereas command is explicit. Customary usage confirms this: one says 'he is obeyed in command' but not 'he is obeyed in will.' The passage also notes a terminological refinement by some Ash'ari scholars: they say 'unbelief is willed in the unbeliever' (as an act of God) but 'not willed from the unbeliever' (as an attribute of the slave). The second phrasing ('not willed from') might imply God's disapproval, but the first ('willed in') avoids that nuance. However, the commentator dismisses this as a mere verbal quibble.

Key Terms
الطاعة
الأمر
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills an act, then performing it is obedience because it fulfills His will.
Correction: Obedience is defined by command, not by will. One can obey a command without God willing it, and vice versa.

Checkpoint
According to the reply, what defines obedience?
A) Conformity to God's will
B) Conformity to God's command
C) Conformity to God's power
D) Conformity to God's love

Key Takeaway
Obedience is defined by command, not by divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something happened in your life (good or bad), it must be what God 'wants' for you, and therefore it is pleasing to Him? How does distinguishing God's will from His command change how you view that event?
?
Think of a specific command from the Qur'an or Sunnah that you find difficult to follow. Are you tempted to substitute it with something you think God 'wills' for you instead? How can you realign your obedience with His actual commands, even when your feelings or circumstances suggest otherwise?

Al-Amidi's Proof and Verbal Nuance
Al-Jurjani cites Al-Amidi's proof and discusses a terminological refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Amidi's argument that will alone does not constitute obedience and the verbal nuance about 'willed in' vs 'willed from'.

Core Idea
Will is hidden; command is explicit — obedience requires knowledge of the command.

Al-Amidi reinforces the distinction with a practical example: if someone does something according to another's will but without awareness, it is not obedience. Then some scholars offered a verbal refinement, but the author dismisses it as a quibble.

Guided Path
1
Understand Al-Amidi's proof
Al-Amidi argues that conformity to will does not constitute obedience because the actor may be unaware of the will.
This shows that will is hidden, while command is manifest.
2
See the example
If a person wills something from another, and the act occurs without the actor's knowledge of the will, it is not considered obedience.
Customary usage supports this: we say 'obeyed in command' not 'obeyed in will'.
3
Evaluate the verbal nuance
Some scholars distinguished 'willed in the unbeliever' from 'willed from the unbeliever', but the author dismisses this as a verbal matter.
The refinement aims to avoid implying divine approval of unbelief.

The reply distinguishes between obedience and will: obedience is defined as conformity to command (amr), not to will (irada). Pleasure with God's Decree vs. Decreed: Resolving the Objection on Unbelief
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between decree and decreed to resolve the objection that being pleased with God's decree would require being pleased with unbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinguishing between God's decree (al-qada') and the decreed thing (al-maqdi) resolves the problem that being pleased with God's decree would require being pleased with unbelief.

Core Idea
We approve of God's act of decreeing, not the evil qualities He decrees for creatures.

This passage addresses a powerful objection: if God wills unbelief, then being pleased with His decree would require being pleased with unbelief—an impossibility. Al-Jurjani provides a subtle distinction that saves the doctrine of qadar without falling into moral contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection and note its logic: God's will implies His decree, pleasure with decree is obligatory, so pleasure with unbelief would be obligatory, but that is self-contradictory because pleasure with unbelief is itself unbelief.
The objection uses 'by consensus' to strengthen each premise.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the reply's central move: obligatory pleasure is with the decree (al-qada') not with the decreed (al-maqdi). Understand the two perspectives: unbelief as an act of God (fa'iliyya) and as an attribute of the slave (mahalliya).
This distinction allows disapproval of unbelief from the slave's perspective while approving it from God's creative perspective.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of the death of prophets: if we had to be pleased with every decreed thing, we would have to be pleased with their deaths, which is false. This shows the necessity of the distinction.
The analogy reinforces that pleasure with the decree does not extend to every particular outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الكفر مراد الله تعالى لكان واقعاً بقضائه والرضاء بالقضاء واجب إجماعاً، فكان الرضاء بالكفر واجباً واللازم باطل لأن الرضاء بالكفر كفر اتفاقاً، قلنا : الواجب هو الرضاء بالقضاء لا بالمقضي، والكفر مقضي لا قضاء والحاصل أن الإنكار المتوجه نحو الكفر إنما هو بالنظر إلى المحلية لا إلى الفاعلية يعني للكفر نسبة إلى الله سبحانه باعتبار فاعليته له وإيجاده إياه ونسبة أخرى إلى العبد باعتبار محليته له واتصافه به وإنكاره باعتبار النسبة الثانية دون الأولى . والرضاء بالعكس أي الرضاء به إنما هو باعتبار النسبة الأولى دون الثانية والفرق بينهما ظاهر وذلك لأنه ليس يلزم من وجوب الرضاء بشيء باعتبار صدوره عن فاعله وجوب الرضاء به باعتبار وقوعه صفة لشيء آخر، إذ لو صح ذلك لوجب الرضاء بموت الأنبياء وهو باطل إجماعاً.
Third: If unbelief were willed by God, then it would occur by His decree, and being pleased with the decree is obligatory by consensus, so being pleased with unbelief would be obligatory; but the consequent is false because being pleased with unbelief is unbelief by agreement. We said: The obligatory is being pleased with the decree (al-qada'), not with the decreed (al-maqdi). Unbelief is decreed, not the decree. The result is that the disapproval directed towards unbelief is only from the perspective of its being a locus (al-mahalliya), not from the perspective of its being an act (al-fa'iliyya). That is, unbelief has a relation to God Almighty in terms of His causing it and bringing it into existence, and another relation to the slave in terms of its being a locus in him and his being described by it. Its disapproval is from the perspective of the second relation, not the first. And being pleased is the opposite, i.e., being pleased with it is only from the perspective of the first relation, not the second. The difference between them is clear, because it does not follow from the obligation to be pleased with something in terms of its issuing from its agent that it is obligatory to be pleased with it in terms of its occurring as an attribute of something else. For if that were valid, then being pleased with the death of prophets would be obligatory, which is false by consensus.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its reply. The objection argues: if God wills unbelief, it occurs by His decree. Since being pleased with God's decree is obligatory, being pleased with unbelief would be obligatory—but being pleased with unbelief is itself unbelief, a contradiction. The reply distinguishes between the decree (al-qada', God's act of judging) and the decreed (al-maqdi, the thing decreed, i.e., unbelief). The obligatory pleasure is with the decree (God's act), not with the decreed as an attribute of the slave. Unbelief is decreed by God but is not His decree; it is the object of decree. Thus we disapprove of unbelief from the perspective of its being a quality of the slave (mahalliya), but we approve of it from the perspective of its being an act of God (fa'iliyya). The passage illustrates this by analogy: if we were obliged to be pleased with death simply because it is decreed by God, we would have to be pleased with the death of prophets, which is false. Therefore, the distinction is necessary.

Key Terms
القضاء
المقضي
الرضاء
المحلية
الفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being pleased with God's decree means being pleased with everything that happens, including evil.
Correction: We are pleased with God's act of decreeing, not with the evil qualities that occur as attributes of creatures. The passage distinguishes between the decree and the decreed.

Checkpoint
According to the reply, what is obligatory to be pleased with?
A) Unbelief itself.
B) The decreed (al-maqdi).
C) The decree (al-qada').
D) The attributes of the slave.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between God's decree and the decreed thing allows us to be pleased with God's will without approving of evil acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or witness evil in the world, do you find yourself resenting God's decree because you focus on the painful reality (the decreed) rather than on God's wise creative act (the decree)? How can you train your heart to separate the two perspectives starting today?
?

?
This lesson teaches that we can approve of God's act of decreeing unbelief while hating the unbelief itself. Is there any area of your life where you have allowed your dislike of a created thing to turn into resentment of the Creator? What specific repentance or change of heart is needed right now?
