Commanding the Impossible – The Ash‘ari Reply
Al-Jurjani answers the fourth objection by defining impossible commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘ari criterion for what constitutes an impossible command (taklif ma la yutaq) and how it differs from the Mu‘tazilite objection based on divine will.

Core Idea
Impossibility for command depends on human capacity, not on God's will.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the fourth objection against the Ash‘ari position on human capacity. The objection claims that if God wills unbelief, faith becomes impossible, so commanding faith is unjust. The Ash‘ari reply redefines impossibility: only what is intrinsically or customarily beyond human power is impossible to command, not what God merely prevents. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Ash‘ari view on divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection assumes that if God wills something, its opposite is impossible; therefore commanding the opposite is unjust. Notice how the Mu‘tazilites build their argument on the Ash‘ari principle that what God wills must occur.
The objection targets the Ash‘ari position on divine will and human capacity.
2
Identify the Ash‘ari reply
The reply distinguishes between impossibility due to lack of capacity and impossibility due to divine will. It states that the impossible command is only what is not within human acquired power habitually.
The key term is 'al-qudra al-kasiba' (acquired power).
3
Note the two categories of true impossibility
The reply lists two types: intrinsic impossibility (like combining opposites) and customary impossibility (like flying). Faith does not fall into either, so the objection fails.
Intrinsic impossibility is absolute; customary impossibility is normal but not absolute.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This reply defends divine justice while affirming that human acts are within capacity, even if God's will overrides. Understand why this distinction matters for the Ash‘ari theory of action.
This connects to the broader debate on human responsibility and divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: لو أراد الله الكفر وخلاف مراد الله ممتنع عندكم كان الأمر بالإيمان تكليفا بما لا يطاق لأن الإيمان ممتنع الصدور عنه حينئذ، قلنا: الذي يمتنع التكليف به عندنا ما لا يكون متعلقاً للقدرة الكاسبة عادة إما لاستحالته في نفسه كالجمع بين النقيضين، وإما لاستحالة صدوره عن الإنسان في مجاري العبادة كالطيران في الجو لا ما يكون مقدوراً
Fourth: If God willed unbelief and that which is contrary to God's will is impossible according to you, then the command to faith would be a charge with what cannot be borne, because faith would be impossible to occur from him at that time. We said: That which it is impossible to charge with according to us is what is not a possible object for the acquisitive power habitually—either because it is intrinsically impossible like combining two opposites, or because it is impossible to occur from a human in customary ways like flying in the air—not what is within capacity.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its reply. The objection argues on a principle the Ash'aris accept: that what God wills must occur, and what He does not will cannot occur. So if God wills unbelief, faith (being contrary to God's will) would be impossible. Thus commanding faith would be a charge with what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq), which is unjust. The reply defines the criterion for a valid charge: what cannot be charged is only what is not within the human's acquired power (al-qudra al-kasiba) habitually—either because it is intrinsically impossible (like combining two opposites) or because it is not within human power in normal circumstances (like flying). Faith, however, is within human capacity (it is possible), even if God wills otherwise. The objection's error is equating impossibility due to God's will with impossibility due to lack of capacity. For the Ash'aris, the relevant impossibility is that which the human cannot do, not that which God prevents.

Key Terms
التكليف بما لا يطاق
القدرة الكاسبة
الاستحالة العادية
الاستحالة الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God does not will an act, it becomes impossible for the human to perform it.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between impossibility due to lack of capacity (which renders a command unjust) and impossibility due to divine will (which does not). An act can be within human capacity even if God does not will it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash‘ari reply, what makes a command impossible to fulfill?
A) God's will preventing it.
B) The act being intrinsically or customarily impossible for the human.
C) The act being contrary to God's decree.
D) The act being sinful.

Key Takeaway
The Ash‘ari criterion for an impossible command is based on human capacity, not divine will; only acts that are intrinsically or customarily beyond human power are impossible to command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find a command of God difficult, do you ever tell yourself that it is 'impossible' and therefore not required of you? How does the Ash'ari distinction between intrinsic and customary impossibility expose the weakness of that excuse?
?
If God has willed that some people will disbelieve, does that give you a reason to abandon calling them to faith? What does this lesson say about the relationship between God's will and human responsibility?
?
Think of a specific sin you struggle with. Do you believe it is 'impossible' for you to stop? How does this lesson's criterion of impossibility challenge that belief and call you to action?

The Continuity of Compensation and the Infinite Regress Objection
Introduction of the first issue about compensation: obligation of continuity and the infinite regress objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that compensation must be continuous in the Hereafter, the infinite regress objection, and the refutation based on lack of sensation.

Core Idea
Compensation's continuity is argued via infinite regress, but refuted by absence of pain.

This lesson examines the first issue concerning divine compensation (al-iwad). Al-Jubba'i and many early Mu'tazilites held that compensation must be continuous in the Hereafter, similar to reward. They supported this with an argument based on infinite regress. The refutation, however, suggests the absence of sensation breaks the chain. Let's delve into the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the flow of argument.
Focus on the positions and the refutation.
2
Identify the main claim
Identify that the Mu'tazilite claim is that compensation must be continuous in the Hereafter.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The passage explains why interruption would lead to pain requiring further compensation, resulting in infinite regress.
4
Examine the refutation
The refutation introduces the possibility that the person may not feel the interruption, thus no pain arises.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument relates to broader theological discussions about divine justice and the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السادس : إن الله لا يفعل القبيح والجبائي وكثير من متقدميهم بل يجب أن يكون في الآخرة) الوجوب دوامه ( كالثواب ) وذلك لأن انقطاعه يوجب ألماً فيستحق بهذا الألم عوضاً آخر ويتسلسل، ورد بجواز عدم شعوره بالانقطاع . قوله : ( ويتسلسل قيل : إنه لم لا يجوز أن يعوض عن ألم الانقطاع في الآخرة، اللهم إلا أن يقال : مدعاهم وجوب كون بعض العوض في الآخرة ومدعى الفريق الأول جواز كون كله في الدنيا فإذا كان عوض ألم الانقطاع في الآخرة ثبت مدعاهم. قوله : ( ورد بجواز إلخ ) وأيضاً عوض تألم قطع العوض غير محل النزاع في غيره كما صرح به في التجريد .
The sixth observatory - the sixth goal: Indeed Allah does not do evil. And al-Jubba'i and many of their predecessors [say] rather it must be in the Hereafter, the obligation of its continuity like reward, because its interruption necessitates a pain, so he becomes deserving by this pain another compensation, and it leads to infinite regress. It was refuted by the permissibility of his not feeling the interruption. He said: (And it leads to infinite regress) it is said: why is it not permissible that he be compensated for the pain of interruption in the Hereafter? Except that it may be said: their claim is the obligation that some of the compensation be in the Hereafter, and the claim of the first party is the permissibility that all of it be in this world. So if the compensation for the pain of interruption is in the Hereafter, their claim is established. He said: (And it was refuted by the permissibility etc.) And also the compensation for the pain of cutting off the compensation is not the subject of dispute in other than it, as stated in al-Tajrid.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage introduces the first issue among the disagreements concerning compensation (al-iwad). It states the position held by al-Jubba'i and many early Mu'tazilites that compensation must be given in the Hereafter and must be continuous, like reward (al-thawab). Their proof: if compensation were interrupted, that interruption would cause pain, and that pain would require another compensation, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The refutation given is that the person might not feel the interruption, so no pain arises. The commentator then examines the infinite regress argument: one might ask why the compensation for the pain of interruption cannot itself be given in the Hereafter. The response distinguishes the two parties: one claims some compensation must be in the Hereafter, the other says all can be in this world. If compensation for pain of interruption is in the Hereafter, the first party's claim holds. Another point: compensation for the pain of cutting off compensation is a different matter and not part of the original dispute.

Correction: The argument is refuted by the possibility that the person does not feel the interruption, so no pain arises, thus no regress.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument for the continuity of compensation is based on infinite regress, but the refutation shows that lack of sensation can break the chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage reveals that this very question lies at the heart of the first disagreement, making it logically prior. Nullification of Compensation by Sins: Two Views
Sanusi presents two views on nullification of compensation by sins and provides commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions on whether sins nullify compensation (al-iwad) and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Sins may nullify compensation to avoid combining bliss and punishment, or compensation becomes an imperceptible reduction.

In this lesson, we examine the third issue concerning compensation (al-iwad) and whether sins can nullify it just as they nullify reward. Sanusi presents two views: one argues for nullification based on the impossibility of combining punishment and reward; the other argues that compensation is applied as an imperceptible reduction of punishment. The commentary elaborates on the logical premises and addresses potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Note the two distinct positions presented.
Pay attention to the structure: first the two views, then commentary.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the statements representing the first view (nullification) and the second view (no nullification, but imperceptible reduction). Understand the underlying logic of each.
The first view relies on the impossibility of combining bliss and punishment simultaneously.
3
Analyze the commentary on the first view
Read the commentary that explains the premise of the first group: compensation must be in the afterlife and continuous, making withholding it impossible.
The commentator asks you to reflect on this inference.
4
Examine the objection and response
The commentary addresses an objection: what if alleviation were continuous? It responds by distinguishing the sects and discussing infinite regress.
Understand why continuity of compensation is not required by the second group.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The commentary suggests the real reason for hiding alleviation is to avoid joy combining with punishment. Consider how this resolves the conflict.
This reinforces the logical consistency of the second view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : هل يحبط العوض بالذنوب كما يحبط الثواب ) أو لا فمن قال بالإحباط تمسك بأنه لولاه لكان الفاسق والكافر في كل وقت من أوقات الآخرة في نعيم العوض وعقاب الفسق أو الكفر ، والجمع بينهما محال، ومن لم يقل به ذهب إلى أن عوض أهل النار بإسقاط جزء من عقابهم بحيث لا يظهر لهم التخفيف وذلك بتفريق الجزء الساقط على الأوقات كيلا يتألم بانقطاع التخفيف . قوله : ( في كل وقت من أوقات الآخرة إلخ المتمسك المذكور من القائلين بوجوب كون العوض في الآخرة وبوجوب دوامه وبه يظهر وجه الاستدلال لأن منع المستحق عن المستحق ولو في أول أوقات الآخرة قبيح عندهم، فتأمل. قوله : (كيلا يتألم بانقطاع التخفيف فإن قلت يجوز أن لا ينقطع التخفيف فلا يتألم قلت : دوام العوض غير واجب عند هذه الفرقة بل ينقطع بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه لا محذور التألم بانقطاع التخفيف إذ على تقدير تسليم لزوم التسلسل على الوجه المذكور يمنع في الدار الآخرة لأن مآله دوام العوض ولا محذور له فيها والظاهر من السياق أن منعهم ظهور التخفيف لئلا يحصل لهم السرور بحصول التخفيف فيجتمع نعيم العوض أعني التخفيف وعقاب الفسق، فتدبر
The third: Is compensation nullified by sins as reward is nullified? Or not? Those who said nullification hold that if not, then the sinner and disbeliever would be in every moment of the afterlife in the bliss of compensation and the punishment of sin or disbelief, and combining them is impossible. Those who did not say nullification held that the compensation of the people of the fire is by removing a part of their punishment such that the alleviation does not appear to them, by distributing the removed part over the times so that they do not suffer pain from the cessation of alleviation. He said: (In every moment of the afterlife etc.) The aforementioned proponent is among those who say that the compensation must be in the afterlife and must be continuous, and by it the aspect of inference appears because preventing the deserving from what he deserves even in the first moments of the afterlife is evil according to them, so reflect. He said: (so that they do not suffer pain from the cessation of alleviation) If you say: it is permissible that the alleviation does not cease, so they do not suffer pain. I say: continuity of compensation is not obligatory according to this sect, rather it ceases. There remains a discussion: there is no harm in suffering pain from the cessation of alleviation because on the supposition of accepting the necessity of infinite regress in the mentioned manner, it is prevented in the abode of the afterlife because its consequence is the continuity of compensation and there is no harm in it there. And the apparent from the context is that their prevention of the appearance of alleviation is so that they do not experience joy from the occurrence of alleviation, thus the bliss of compensation (i.e., alleviation) and the punishment of sin would be combined, so ponder.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage addresses the third issue: whether sins nullify compensation (al-iwad) just as they nullify reward (al-thawab). Two positions are presented. The first position: sins do nullify compensation. The proof: if not, then a sinful believer or a disbeliever would simultaneously experience the bliss of compensation and the punishment for sin or disbelief, which is impossible. The second position: sins do not nullify compensation. Instead, for the inhabitants of Hellfire, compensation is applied by reducing a portion of their punishment, but this alleviation is not made perceptible to them. The reduction is spread out over time so that they do not feel pain from its cessation. The commentator then elaborates on the proof of the first group, noting that their premise that compensation must be in the afterlife and continuous leads to the impossibility of withholding the deserved compensation even at the beginning. The commentator also addresses an objection: what if the alleviation were continuous? He explains that according to this sect compensation is not obligatory continuously, but if it were continuous, there would be no harm. He concludes by interpreting the reason for hiding the alleviation: to avoid combining the pleasure of compensation with the punishment of sin, which would be contradictory.

The Fifth Issue: Is Pain Inflicted for Compensation or as a Favor?
Discussion on the purpose of inflicting pain among those who permit it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among those who permit inflicting pain for compensation, and how the discussion shifts from the occurrence of pain to its purpose and justification.

Core Idea
Pain's purpose matters: is it solely for compensation, or can it be a favor or a lesson?

This passage from al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle theological question among those who accept that God may inflict pain: is pain always followed by compensation, or could it be that God inflicts pain as a favor without prior compensation? The discussion reveals deeper reasoning about divine wisdom and the nature of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Identify the two views: one says pain is inflicted only for compensation, the other says it can be by way of favor without compensation.
Notice the phrase 'مخالفاً للحكمة' which criticizes the second view.
2
Analyze the objection and the response
The objection says if compensation can be given without pain, then pain is pointless. The response says pain makes compensation more delightful by removing the sense of obligation.
Think about why removing obligation is considered more delightful.
3
Understand the shift from occurrence to justification
The commentator notes that since pain is observed, the real debate is not about whether it occurs but about its justification: whether pain is permissible only for compensation or also as a lesson (عبرة).
This mirrors the disagreement among those who prohibit inflicting pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الخامس على الجواز : هل يؤلم ليعوض أو يكون ذلك مع إمكان الابتداء به على طريق التفضل ) مخالفاً للحكمة . قوله : ( مخالفاً للحكمة) لأن الابتداء بالعوض جائز فتوسيط الألم عبث والآخرون قالوا : إذا وسط الألم يكون العوض غير مشوب بالمنة فيكون ألذ . واعلم أن المفهوم من ظاهر ما ذكر في الاختلاف الخامس اختلاف المجوزين في وقوع الإيلام وعدم وقوعه لكن وقوعه مشاهد فيجب أن يوجه بجواز الإيلام بمجرد التعويض أو مع كونه عبرة له ولغيره كاختلاف المانعين، صرح به الأبهري.
The fifth: On the permissibility: Is he pained to be compensated, or is that with the possibility of starting with it by way of favor? Contrary to wisdom. He said: (Contrary to wisdom) because beginning with compensation is permissible, so intermediating pain is pointless. And others said: If pain is intermediated, the compensation becomes untainted by obligation, so it is more delightful. And know that the understood from the apparent of what was mentioned in the fifth disagreement is the disagreement of the permitters about the occurrence of pain and its non-occurrence, but its occurrence is observed, so it must be directed by the permissibility of pain solely for compensation or with it being a lesson for him and others, like the disagreement of the prohibiters. al-Abhari stated this.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth issue, which arises among those who permit (al-mujawwizun) inflicting pain for compensation. The question is whether pain is inflicted solely for the purpose of giving compensation, or whether it could also be that God begins with the compensation by way of favor without prior pain. The text notes that the latter is considered contrary to wisdom. The commentator explains: if starting with compensation is permissible, then introducing pain as an intermediary is pointless. Others argue that mediating pain makes the compensation more valuable because it is not tainted by a sense of obligation. The commentator then observes that the fifth disagreement actually appears to be about whether pain actually occurs (since compensation without pain is possible), but since pain is observed in reality, the debate must be about its justification: whether pain is permissible only for compensation or also as a lesson. This mirrors the disagreement among the prohibiters (al-mani'un).

تعويض
تفضل
حكمة
عبرة
عبث
منة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the fifth issue is about the permissibility of inflicting pain in general.
Correction: The issue is specifically about the purpose behind inflicting pain when compensation is involved, not about the permissibility itself.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to inflicting pain as a means to give compensation?
A) Pain is never permissible
B) Compensation could be given without pain, so pain is absurd
C) Pain cannot be compensated
D) Compensation is only for the Hereafter

Key Takeaway
The debate over inflicting pain for compensation hinges on whether pain serves a purpose beyond mere compensation, such as making the compensation more delightful or serving as a lesson.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever suffered something that later made a blessing seem sweeter because it felt undeserved? How does the argument that pain removes the sense of obligation (minnah) resonate with your own experience of hardship?
?
The passage considers that some pain may be a lesson (ʿibrah). What painful experience in your life might God be using to teach you something right now? Are you actively trying to learn from it, or are you resisting the lesson?
?
If you truly believe that nothing God allows is pointless, how must you change your attitude toward ongoing pain or difficulty that you cannot explain? What would it look like to trust that there is a purpose—either for compensation or a lesson—even when you cannot see it?

The Two Views Among the Prohibiters on Pain Without Compensation
Sanusi presents the sixth issue and the initial split among the prohibiters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even among those who prohibit bestowing favor without compensation, there were differing views on whether pain could be inflicted without any compensation or only with added compensation as a deterrent.

Core Idea
Prohibiters of mere favor split: some allow pure pain, others require added compensation and deterrence.

This lesson introduces the sixth issue concerning inflicting pain for compensation. The author begins by presenting the question and immediately notes that the 'prohibiters'—those who deny that God can bestow favor without compensation—disagree among themselves. Some permit pure pain without any compensation, while others insist on extra compensation that also serves as a deterrent. This first pass establishes the conceptual framework for the subsequent detailed views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation to understand the two positions.
Pay attention to the terms 'prohibiters', 'compensation', and 'deterrent grace'.
2
Identify the two groups
Find where the text describes the first group (pure pain without compensation) and the second group (pain with extra compensation as a deterrent). Note that both are within the 'prohibiters'.
3
Understand the condition of extra compensation
The second group insists that compensation must be 'extra' (za'id). Why? Read the explanation: it must satisfy every rational person to endure the pain for it.
This condition ensures the compensation is greater than the pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس على المنع هل يؤلم ليعوض عوضاً زائداً ليكون لطفاً له ولغيره هو يصير ذلك ) الإيلام ) عبرة له تزجره عن القبيح يعني أن المانعين من جواز التفضل بمثل ما يوصل عوضاً اختلفوا فجوز بعضهم الإيلام المجرد عن التعويض واعتبر آخرون أن يكون مع التعويض شيء آخر وهو أن يكون لطفاً زاجراً له ولغيره، وقيد العوض بالزائد لأنهم صرحوا بأن العوض من الله يجب أن يكون زائداً بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجل ذلك العوض، هذا والمذكور في كلام الآمدي أن المانعين من جواز التفضل جوزوا الآلام لمجرد التعويض كالجبائي وأبي الهذيل وقدماء المعتزلة والمجوزين له لم يجوزوا الآلام إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بتلك الآلام وكونها ألطافاً في زجرها عن غوايته، وذهب عباد الضميري إلى جواز الآلام لمحض الاعتبار من غير تعويض وذهب أبو هاشم إلى أن الآلام لا تحسن لمجرد التعويض مع القدرة على التفضل بمثل العوض إلا إذا علم الله أنه لا ينفعه إلا بجهة التعويض فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب . قوله تزجره عن (القبيح) فإن قلت : الزجر عندما كان الألم في مقابلة سيئة ولا يجب العوض حينئذ، كما مر اللهم إلا أن يقال : يكفي في الزجر ترتب الألم على السيئة ترتباً ظاهرياً وإن لم يكن في مقابلتها في نفس الأمر قلت : لا نسلم أنه يجب في الزجر كون الألم في السيئة، نعم يرد أن العوض إذا كان بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجله يحصل الانزجار، اللهم إلا أن يقال : ينزجر بالألم لعدم الشعور بالعوض . قوله : ( وذهب عباد الضميري في أكثر النسخ تقديم الياء المثناة من تحت على الميم وصحح بعضهم عكسه على أن الضميري منسوب إلى ضمير تصغير ضمر وهو قرية من قرى الشام كذا في المغرب . قوله : (فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب يعني أنه غير مطابق له لأن الظاهر من كلام الآمدي اتفاق المانعين على جواز الإيلام بمجرد التعويض واتفاق المجوزين على عدم جوازه إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بذلك الإيلام، والمفهوم من نقل المصنف هو في كل من الفريقين، اللهم إلا أن يحمل المانعون والمجوزون في كلام الآمدي على جمهورهم ولا يخفى أنه خلاف الظاهر.
The sixth: On the prohibition: Is he pained for a compensation that is extra to be a grace for him and others. This means that the prohibiters of the permissibility of favor with something like that which is given as compensation differed: some permitted pure pain without compensation, others considered that with compensation there should be something else, namely it being a deterrent grace for him and others. They restricted compensation to being extra because they stated that compensation from Allah must be extra such that every rational person would endure that pain for that compensation. And what is mentioned in the speech of al-Amidi is that the prohibiters of the permissibility of favor permitted pains for mere compensation, like al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, and the early Mu'tazila, and those who permitted it did not permit pains except on condition of compensation and consideration of others with those pains and their being graces in deterring from error. And 'Abbad al-Damiri went to the permissibility of pains for pure consideration without compensation. And Abu Hashim went to pains not being good for mere compensation with ability to bestow favor with like compensation unless Allah knows that he will only benefit by way of compensation. So you must reflect on its conformity with what is in the book. He said: (deters him from evil) For if you say: deterrence is when the pain is in exchange for an evil and compensation is not required then, as passed. Except that it may be said: it suffices in deterrence the apparent ordering of pain upon evil even if not in exchange in reality. I say: we do not accept that it is necessary in deterrence that the pain be for evil. Yes, it is objected that if compensation is such that every rational person would endure that pain for it, deterrence occurs. Except that it may be said: he is deterred by pain due to lack of awareness of compensation. He said: (And 'Abbad al-Damiri) in most copies the ya' precedes the mim, and some corrected the opposite, and al-Damiri is attributed to Damir, diminutive of Damar, a village in Syria. He said: (So you must reflect on its conformity with what is in the book) meaning it does not conform, because the apparent from al-Amidi's speech is the agreement of the prohibiters on permitting pain for mere compensation, and the agreement of the permitters on not permitting it except with compensation and consideration of others with that pain, while the understanding from the author's transmission is the opposite in each of the two groups. Except if the prohibiters and permitters in al-Amidi's speech are interpreted as the majority, and it is obvious that this is contrary to the apparent.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This long passage covers the sixth issue, which is considered among those who prohibit (al-mani'un) inflicting pain for compensation. The question is whether pain is inflicted for an extra compensation that serves as a grace (lutf) for the person and others. The text explains that those who prohibit favoring someone with compensation have differing views: some allow pure pain without any compensation, while others require that the pain be accompanied by something else, namely a deterrent grace. They specify that the compensation must be extra, sufficient to satisfy any rational person. The passage then contrasts this with the account from al-Amidi, who reports that the prohibiters (of favor) actually allowed pains for mere compensation (like al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, etc.), while those who permitted favor did not allow pains except with compensation and consideration of others as a deterrent. 'Abbad al-Damiri went further, allowing pains purely for deterrent consideration without compensation. Abu Hashim held that pains are not good for mere compensation if one can bestow favor with the same thing, unless God knows the person will only benefit through compensation. The commentator then addresses an objection about deterrence: if pain deters from evil, then compensation might not be required, but the reply is that even if pain is not actually in exchange for evil, the apparent ordering suffices for deterrence. The commentator also clarifies a textual variant regarding 'Abbad al-Damiri's name and concludes by noting a discrepancy between the author's summary and al-Amidi's account, suggesting the author may have meant the majority views.

إيلام
عوض زائد
لطف
زاجر
عبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All prohibiters completely reject any form of pain without compensation.
Correction: The text shows that some prohibiters actually allowed pure pain without any compensation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition did the second group of prohibiters impose on inflicting pain?
A) Pain must be without any compensation
B) Pain must be accompanied by extra compensation that serves as a deterrent grace
C) Pain must be inflicted only on believers
D) Pain must be equal to the compensation received

Key Takeaway
Among those who prohibit bestowing favor without compensation, some permit pure pain while others require extra compensating deterrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you consider that it might be a deterrent (zājir) from Allah to prevent you from ugly deeds, or do you see it merely as punishment? How does this lesson change your perspective on trials?
?
The second group says God's compensation must be so great that any rational person would endure the pain for its sake. Are you that rational person? Can you honestly say you would endure any pain for the compensation of Jannah? What holds you back?
?
The first group allows pure pain without compensation. Does that make you fear Allah? How does that affect your reliance on His mercy? Or do you find comfort in the second group's view—and why?

Al-Amidi's Reversed Classification of the Groups
Sanusi presents al-Amidi's account that reverses the earlier classification of views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Amidi, the groups are reversed: the so-called 'prohibiters' allowed pain for mere compensation, while those who permitted favor required compensation and deterrent.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi reverses the classification: prohibiters allowed mere compensation, permitters added deterrent.

In this lesson, we see a different account from al-Amidi that flips the earlier classification. According to al-Amidi, the 'prohibiters' (of favor) actually permitted pains for mere compensation, citing al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, and early Mu'tazila. In contrast, those who permitted favor (al-mujawwizun) did not allow pains unless accompanied by compensation and consideration of others as a deterrent. This discrepancy is important for understanding the broader debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is attributed to al-Amidi and presents a different alignment.
2
Identify the two groups in al-Amidi's account
Find the description of the 'prohibiters' (al-mani'un) and the 'permitters' (al-mujawwizun) and note their positions on pain and compensation.
Notice that al-Amidi says prohibiters allowed mere compensation, while permitters required compensation and deterrent.
3
Contrast with the previous lesson
Compare this with the earlier split. In the previous lesson, the 'prohibiters' had two views; here they are unified. This sets up a tension that the author later addresses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Do you live as if your trials are only for your own benefit, or do you consider how your patience or struggle might affect those around you? What would change if you actively saw your pain as both compensated and a grace for others?

The Positions of 'Abbad al-Damiri and Abu Hashim
Sanusi reports the views of 'Abbad al-Damiri and Abu Hashim on pain and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced views of 'Abbad al-Damiri and Abu Hashim regarding inflicting pain for compensation and deterrence.

Core Idea
'Abbad allowed pain purely for deterrence; Abu Hashim required lack of ability to bestow favor without compensation.

This lesson presents two further positions within the debate. 'Abbad al-Damiri went so far as to allow pain purely for deterrent consideration without any compensation. Abu Hashim, on the other hand, held that pain is not good for mere compensation if God is able to bestow favor with the same thing, unless God knows that the person will only benefit through compensation. These views refine the spectrum of opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the two distinct positions: 'Abbad's and Abu Hashim's.
2
Compare 'Abbad's view with earlier ones
'Abbad allows pain without compensation, solely as a deterrent. How does this differ from the first group in Lesson 1?
The first group in Lesson 1 allowed pure pain without compensation, but didn't specify deterrence; 'Abbad specifically says for pure consideration (deterrence).
3
Understand Abu Hashim's condition
Abu Hashim says pain is not good for mere compensation if God can bestow favor with the same compensation, unless God knows that compensation is the only way to benefit. This ties into the broader issue of theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس على المنع هل يؤلم ليعوض عوضاً زائداً ليكون لطفاً له ولغيره هو يصير ذلك ) الإيلام ) عبرة له تزجره عن القبيح يعني أن المانعين من جواز التفضل بمثل ما يوصل عوضاً اختلفوا فجوز بعضهم الإيلام المجرد عن التعويض واعتبر آخرون أن يكون مع التعويض شيء آخر وهو أن يكون لطفاً زاجراً له ولغيره، وقيد العوض بالزائد لأنهم صرحوا بأن العوض من الله يجب أن يكون زائداً بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجل ذلك العوض، هذا والمذكور في كلام الآمدي أن المانعين من جواز التفضل جوزوا الآلام لمجرد التعويض كالجبائي وأبي الهذيل وقدماء المعتزلة والمجوزين له لم يجوزوا الآلام إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بتلك الآلام وكونها ألطافاً في زجرها عن غوايته، وذهب عباد الضميري إلى جواز الآلام لمحض الاعتبار من غير تعويض وذهب أبو هاشم إلى أن الآلام لا تحسن لمجرد التعويض مع القدرة على التفضل بمثل العوض إلا إذا علم الله أنه لا ينفعه إلا بجهة التعويض فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب . قوله تزجره عن (القبيح) فإن قلت : الزجر عندما كان الألم في مقابلة سيئة ولا يجب العوض حينئذ، كما مر اللهم إلا أن يقال : يكفي في الزجر ترتب الألم على السيئة ترتباً ظاهرياً وإن لم يكن في مقابلتها في نفس الأمر قلت : لا نسلم أنه يجب في الزجر كون الألم في السيئة، نعم يرد أن العوض إذا كان بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجله يحصل الانزجار، اللهم إلا أن يقال : ينزجر بالألم لعدم الشعور بالعوض . قوله : ( وذهب عباد الضميري في أكثر النسخ تقديم الياء المثناة من تحت على الميم وصحح بعضهم عكسه على أن الضميري منسوب إلى ضمير تصغير ضمر وهو قرية من قرى الشام كذا في المغرب . قوله : (فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب يعني أنه غير مطابق له لأن الظاهر من كلام الآمدي اتفاق المانعين على جواز الإيلام بمجرد التعويض واتفاق المجوزين على عدم جوازه إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بذلك الإيلام، والمفهوم من نقل المصنف هو في كل من الفريقين، اللهم إلا أن يحمل المانعون والمجوزون في كلام الآمدي على جمهورهم ولا يخفى أنه خلاف الظاهر.
The sixth: On the prohibition: Is he pained for a compensation that is extra to be a grace for him and others. This means that the prohibiters of the permissibility of favor with something like that which is given as compensation differed: some permitted pure pain without compensation, others considered that with compensation there should be something else, namely it being a deterrent grace for him and others. They restricted compensation to being extra because they stated that compensation from Allah must be extra such that every rational person would endure that pain for that compensation. And what is mentioned in the speech of al-Amidi is that the prohibiters of the permissibility of favor permitted pains for mere compensation, like al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, and the early Mu'tazila, and those who permitted it did not permit pains except on condition of compensation and consideration of others with those pains and their being graces in deterring from error. And 'Abbad al-Damiri went to the permissibility of pains for pure consideration without compensation. And Abu Hashim went to pains not being good for mere compensation with ability to bestow favor with like compensation unless Allah knows that he will only benefit by way of compensation. So you must reflect on its conformity with what is in the book. He said: (deters him from evil) For if you say: deterrence is when the pain is in exchange for an evil and compensation is not required then, as passed. Except that it may be said: it suffices in deterrence the apparent ordering of pain upon evil even if not in exchange in reality. I say: we do not accept that it is necessary in deterrence that the pain be for evil. Yes, it is objected that if compensation is such that every rational person would endure that pain for it, deterrence occurs. Except that it may be said: he is deterred by pain due to lack of awareness of compensation. He said: (And 'Abbad al-Damiri) in most copies the ya' precedes the mim, and some corrected the opposite, and al-Damiri is attributed to Damir, diminutive of Damar, a village in Syria. He said: (So you must reflect on its conformity with what is in the book) meaning it does not conform, because the apparent from al-Amidi's speech is the agreement of the prohibiters on permitting pain for mere compensation, and the agreement of the permitters on not permitting it except with compensation and consideration of others with that pain, while the understanding from the author's transmission is the opposite in each of the two groups. Except if the prohibiters and permitters in al-Amidi's speech are interpreted as the majority, and it is obvious that this is contrary to the apparent.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This long passage covers the sixth issue, which is considered among those who prohibit (al-mani'un) inflicting pain for compensation. The question is whether pain is inflicted for an extra compensation that serves as a grace (lutf) for the person and others. The text explains that those who prohibit favoring someone with compensation have differing views: some allow pure pain without any compensation, while others require that the pain be accompanied by something else, namely a deterrent grace. They specify that the compensation must be extra, sufficient to satisfy any rational person. The passage then contrasts this with the account from al-Amidi, who reports that the prohibiters (of favor) actually allowed pains for mere compensation (like al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, etc.), while those who permitted favor did not allow pains except with compensation and consideration of others as a deterrent. 'Abbad al-Damiri went further, allowing pains purely for deterrent consideration without compensation. Abu Hashim held that pains are not good for mere compensation if one can bestow favor with the same thing, unless God knows the person will only benefit through compensation. The commentator then addresses an objection about deterrence: if pain deters from evil, then compensation might not be required, but the reply is that even if pain is not actually in exchange for evil, the apparent ordering suffices for deterrence. The commentator also clarifies a textual variant regarding 'Abbad al-Damiri's name and concludes by noting a discrepancy between the author's summary and al-Amidi's account, suggesting the author may have meant the majority views.

Key Terms
عباد الضميري
أبو هاشم
محض الاعتبار
التفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both 'Abbad and Abu Hashim agree that compensation is unnecessary.
Correction: 'Abbad rejects compensation entirely, but Abu Hashim allows compensation only under specific conditions.

Checkpoint
Under what condition did Abu Hashim deem inflicting pain for compensation to be good?
A) Always good if compensation is given
B) Never good
C) Good only if God knows the person will benefit only through compensation
D) Good only if the pain is severe

Key Takeaway
'Abbad al-Damiri allowed pain purely for deterrence; Abu Hashim allowed it only when compensation is the exclusive means of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face undeserved pain, do you immediately demand compensation from Allah? This lesson presents two theological positions. Which one resonates with your heart, and why? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom? Will you choose to surrender your demand today?
?
'Abbad al-Damiri says pain can be purely for deterrence. Have you ever considered that your suffering might be a lesson for others? How can you find meaning in pain without demanding repayment? What would it cost you to let go of that demand?
?
Abu Hashim conditions pain on Allah's knowledge of benefit. In a moment of hardship, can you truly surrender your demand for compensation and trust that Allah's knowledge of your benefit is sufficient? What is holding you back from that trust right now?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Objections and Responses on Deterrence Through Pain
Sanusi presents an objection and response about the relationship between deterrence and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that deterrence does not require compensation and the subsequent response that deterrence can be achieved through apparent ordering or through sufficient compensation.

Core Idea
Deterrence can occur even without actual exchange if the pain appears consequent to evil, or through compensation that satisfies any rational person.

This lesson examines an objection and its response regarding the role of deterrence. The objector argues that if pain is intended to deter from evil, then no compensation is required because deterrence is the proper response to a wrong act. The responder counters that deterrence can be achieved by an apparent ordering of pain after evil, even if not actual exchange, or that a sufficiently generous compensation itself deters. This dialectic sharpens the definition of deterrence in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the objection starting with 'fa-in qulta' and the response starting with 'qultu'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that deterrence (zajr) occurs when pain is in exchange for evil, and compensation is not required then. Why does he think compensation is unnecessary?
Because deterrence is a natural consequence of evil, not a transaction.
3
Analyze the response
The responder makes two points: First, deterrence can be achieved by mere apparent ordering of pain after evil, even if not actual exchange. Second, if compensation is so attractive that any rational person would endure pain for it, then the pain itself deters because the person is unaware of compensation at the moment of deterrence.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deterrence always requires an actual exchange of pain for evil.

Checkpoint
What is one of the responses given to the objection about deterrence?
A) Deterrence is impossible without compensation
B) Apparent ordering of pain after evil suffices for deterrence
C) Deterrence only applies to believers
D) Pain must be equal to the compensation

Key Takeaway
Deterrence can occur through apparent ordering of pain after evil or through compensation that rational persons would endure, even without actual exchange.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you immediately assume it is a punishment for a specific sin? How does this lesson’s distinction between apparent ordering and actual desert change what you think about your own hardships? What would change if you genuinely believed that every pain you endure is compensated in a way that any rational person would accept?
?
Are you more deterred from sin by fear of punishment or by hope of reward? Which motive dominates your daily choices? How can you train your heart to be more aware of the compensation Allah has prepared, so that you are not merely deterred but also motivated?
?
If you knew that a trial you are facing right now is only apparently connected to your actions—that the real cause is beyond your understanding—would you still trust Allah’s justice? What would it take for you to surrender your need to see a clear cause-and-effect relationship between your deeds and your circumstances?

Textual Notes and Conformity with Al-Amidi's Account
Sanusi provides a textual note on 'Abbad's name and a critique of the conformity between the author's summary and al-Amidi's account.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the textual variant regarding 'Abbad al-Damiri's name and the commentator's observation that the author's summary does not fully match al-Amidi's account.

Core Idea
The author's summary and al-Amidi's account conflict; the commentator suggests interpreting al-Amidi's remarks as referring to the majority views.

In this final lesson, the commentator addresses two secondary but important points. First, a textual variant in the name of 'Abbad al-Damiri: whether it is spelled with ya' before mim or mim before ya', and its origin in a Syrian village. Second, and more significantly, the commentator notes that the author's presentation of views seems to contradict al-Amidi's account: the author implied that the prohibiters and permitters each had two positions, but al-Amidi's account suggests each group had a unified position. The commentator suggests interpreting al-Amidi's remarks as applying only to the majority of each group.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. First part deals with the name of 'Abbad al-Damiri; second part discusses the inconsistency with al-Amidi.
2
Understand the textual variant
The commentator notes that most manuscripts have ya' before mim in 'al-Damiri', but some reverse it. The name is derived from 'Damir', a village in Syria.
3
Analyze the conformity issue
The commentator states that the author's transmission does not conform to al-Amidi's account. In al-Amidi, the prohibiters agreed on allowing pain for mere compensation, and the permitters agreed on requiring compensation and deterrent. But the author's summary suggested both groups had internal splits. The commentator suggests that al-Amidi may have been referring to the majority of each group.
This is a meta-commentary on the accuracy of the source.

السادس على المنع هل يؤلم ليعوض عوضاً زائداً ليكون لطفاً له ولغيره هو يصير ذلك ) الإيلام ) عبرة له تزجره عن القبيح يعني أن المانعين من جواز التفضل بمثل ما يوصل عوضاً اختلفوا فجوز بعضهم الإيلام المجرد عن التعويض واعتبر آخرون أن يكون مع التعويض شيء آخر وهو أن يكون لطفاً زاجراً له ولغيره، وقيد العوض بالزائد لأنهم صرحوا بأن العوض من الله يجب أن يكون زائداً بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجل ذلك العوض، هذا والمذكور في كلام الآمدي أن المانعين من جواز التفضل جوزوا الآلام لمجرد التعويض كالجبائي وأبي الهذيل وقدماء المعتزلة والمجوزين له لم يجوزوا الآلام إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بتلك الآلام وكونها ألطافاً في زجرها عن غوايته، وذهب عباد الضميري إلى جواز الآلام لمحض الاعتبار من غير تعويض وذهب أبو هاشم إلى أن الآلام لا تحسن لمجرد التعويض مع القدرة على التفضل بمثل العوض إلا إذا علم الله أنه لا ينفعه إلا بجهة التعويض فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب . قوله تزجره عن (القبيح) فإن قلت : الزجر عندما كان الألم في مقابلة سيئة ولا يجب العوض حينئذ، كما مر اللهم إلا أن يقال : يكفي في الزجر ترتب الألم على السيئة ترتباً ظاهرياً وإن لم يكن في مقابلتها في نفس الأمر قلت : لا نسلم أنه يجب في الزجر كون الألم في السيئة، نعم يرد أن العوض إذا كان بحيث يرضى كل عاقل يتحمل ذلك الألم لأجله يحصل الانزجار، اللهم إلا أن يقال : ينزجر بالألم لعدم الشعور بالعوض . قوله : ( وذهب عباد الضميري في أكثر النسخ تقديم الياء المثناة من تحت على الميم وصحح بعضهم عكسه على أن الضميري منسوب إلى ضمير تصغير ضمر وهو قرية من قرى الشام كذا في المغرب . قوله : (فعليك بالتأمل في مطابقته لما في الكتاب يعني أنه غير مطابق له لأن الظاهر من كلام الآمدي اتفاق المانعين على جواز الإيلام بمجرد التعويض واتفاق المجوزين على عدم جوازه إلا بشرط التعويض واعتبار الغير بذلك الإيلام، والمفهوم من نقل المصنف هو في كل من الفريقين، اللهم إلا أن يحمل المانعون والمجوزون في كلام الآمدي على جمهورهم ولا يخفى أنه خلاف الظاهر.

This long passage covers the sixth issue, which is considered among those who prohibit (al-mani'un) inflicting pain for compensation. The question is whether pain is inflicted for an extra compensation that serves as a grace (lutf) for the person and others. The text explains that those who prohibit favoring someone with compensation have differing views: some allow pure pain without any compensation, while others require that the pain be accompanied by something else, namely a deterrent grace. They specify that the compensation must be extra, sufficient to satisfy any rational person. The passage then contrasts this with the account from al-Amidi, who reports that the prohibiters (of favor) actually allowed pains for mere compensation (like al-Jubba'i, Abu al-Hudhayl, etc.), while those who permitted favor did not allow pains except with compensation and consideration of others as a deterrent. 'Abbad al-Damiri went further, allowing pains purely for deterrent consideration without compensation. Abu Hashim held that pains are not good for mere compensation if one can bestow favor with the same thing, unless God knows the person will only benefit through compensation. The commentator then addresses an objection about deterrence: if pain deters from evil, then compensation might not be required, but the reply is that even if pain is not actually in exchange for evil, the apparent ordering suffices for deterrence. The commentator also clarifies a textual variant regarding 'Abbad al-Damiri's name and concludes by noting a discrepancy between the author's summary and al-Amidi's account, suggesting the author may have meant the majority views.

Key Terms
عباد الضميري
الآمدي
مطابقة
جمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's summary and al-Amidi's account are perfectly consistent.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states they are not consistent and offers a possible but unlikely interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the author's transmission not conform to al-Amidi's account?
A) Because al-Amidi's account has more details
B) Because the author's transmission suggests splits within each group, while al-Amidi suggests unity
C) Because al-Amidi only discusses one group
D) Because the author misquoted the names

Key Takeaway
The author's summary and al-Amidi's account conflict; the commentator proposes a minority interpretation to reconcile them.

Compensation for Animals: A Chain of Questions
List of sub-questions on animal compensation and a note on denial of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the series of theological sub-questions regarding whether and how animals are compensated for their suffering, and the role of intellect in Paradise.

Core Idea
Animal suffering raises a cascade of questions about divine justice and the afterlife.

This final issue in the section addresses a sensitive topic: do animals receive compensation for their pains? The passage unfolds as a logical sequence of questions, each leading to the next, revealing the depth of theological reflection. It also notes that some theologians even deny animal pain to avoid the implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the questions being asked.
Notice the parentheses indicating incomplete sentences in the OCR.
2
Identify each sub-question
List the four sub-questions: (1) Are animals compensated? (2) When? (3) Where? (4) With intellect?
3
Understand the objection
Notice the final remark: some deny animal pain to avoid necessitating Paradise and intellect for animals.
This is a theological evasion.
4
Reflect on the cascade
Consider why each question depends on the previous one, forming a logical chain.

The seventh: Are animals compensated for the pains and hardships during their lives, and are distinguished by them from their likes that do not suffer similar? Or are they not compensated? And if they are compensated, is that compensation in this world or the Hereafter? And if in the Hereafter, is it in Paradise or elsewhere? These are their disagreements, although among them some denied the occurrence of pain to animals and children out of obstinacy and to escape from necessitating their entry into Paradise and the creation of mind in them.

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh and final issue in this section: compensation for animals (al-baha'im). It lists a series of sub-questions. First, are animals compensated for the pains and hardships they endure during their lives, and are they thereby distinguished from other animals that do not suffer such pains? Second, if they are compensated, does that compensation occur in this world or the Hereafter? Third, if in the Hereafter, is it in Paradise or elsewhere? Fourth, if in Paradise, will God create intellect in them so that they can understand that this is a reward, and that it is eternal and unceasing? The text notes that these are points of disagreement among theologians. It also mentions that some people, to avoid the implication that animals must enter Paradise and receive intellect, stubbornly deny that animals and children experience pain altogether.

Determinism Makes Obligation Impossible
Introduction to second group and first proof against obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against obligation based on determinism (jabr) and its two sub-arguments: the powerlessness of the servant and divine foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Determinism renders obligation impossible; it would be commanding another's act.

The second group of thinkers held that prophethood necessarily entails obligation. The first proof against obligation appeals to determinism: if all actions are from God and the servant has no power, then obligating the servant to act is like obligating another, which is beyond capacity. Additionally, if actions are foreknown, they are either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage establishing the second group's view
Start with the opening line that defines this group's position.
Notice the phrase 'by agreement and consensus'.
2
Follow the determinism argument
Identify the two prongs: (1) the servant has no power, so obligation is to another's act; (2) all acts are foreknown, making them necessary or impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that obligation is evil (qabih) because it is an impossible demand, so it cannot be attributed to God.
4
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Note that this argument assumes the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb is not in play – that will be addressed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة الثانية: من قال البعثة لا تخلو عن التكليف لأن فائدتها التي لا تخلو هي عنها وأيضاً هي لا تخلو عن لازمها التكليف باتفاق وإجماع من القائلين بها ثم إن التكليف الذي هو ممتنع لوجوه: الأول: ثبت الجبر وعدم الاختيار في الأفعال وذلك لما تبين من أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله إذ لا تأثير لقدرة العبد عندكم أصلاً والتكليف بفعل الغير تكليف بما لا يطاق ومن أن الفعل إما معلوم الوقوع من العبد أو معلوم اللاوقوع منه فعلى الأول يكون ضرورياً وعلى الثاني ممتنعاً ولا قدرة على شيء منهما والتكليف حينئذ أي حين إذ ثبت الجبر قبيح فيكون ممتنعاً. الثاني: التكليف إضرار بالعبد لما يلزمه من التعب بالفعل إذا أقدم عليه أو العقاب بالترك إذا أحجم عنه وهو أي الإضرار قبيح والله تعالى منزه عنه. الثالث: التكليف إما لا لغرض وهو عبث قبيح أو لغرض يعود إلى الله وهو محال لأنه تعالى منزه عن الأغراض كلها من جلب المنافع ودفع المضار أو إلى العبد وهو إما إضرار وهو منتف بالإجماع أو نفع وتكليف جلب النفع والتعذيب بعدمه بخلاف المعقول فإنه منزلة أن يقال له حصل المنفعة لنفسك وإلا عذبتك أبدا الآباد، ولا شك أن أهم مصالحه ترك التعذيب ثم إنه أي النفع الذي يتضمنه التكليف للمؤمنين المطيعين معارض بما فيه من المضرة العظيمة بالكفار والعصاة ولا شك أن إضرار جماعة لمنفعة آخرين ظلم قبيح. الرابع: التكليف بإيقاع الفعل إما مع وجود الفعل ولا فائدة فيه أصلا لوجوبه وتعين صدوره حينئذ فيكون عبثاً قبيحاً، وكذا الحال إذا كان التكليف بعد الفعل مع أنه تكليف بتحصيل الحاصل. وأما قبل وجود الفعل وإنه تكليف بما لا يطاق لأن الفعل قبل الفعل محال إذ لا يمكن وجود الشيء حال عدمه والتكليف بما لا يطاق باطل عند لا من يجوزه وهو ظاهر، وأما من جوزه فإنه لا يقول بوقوعه ولا أن كل تكليف كذلك أي تكليف بما لا يطاق والتكليف بالفعل قبله يستلزم وقوع التكليف بما لا يطاق ويستلزم أن كل تكليف من هذا القبيل فيكون هذا القسم باطلاً عنده أيضاً، وإذا بطلت الأقسام الحاضرة امتنع التكليف مطلقاً. الخامس: وهو لبعض الصوفية من أهل الإباحة أن التكليف بالأفعال الشاقة البدنية يشغل الباطن عن التفكر في معرفة الله تعالى وما يجب له من الصفات ويجوز ويمتنع عليه من الأفعال ولا شك أن المصلحة المتوقعة من هذا الفائت وهو النظر فيما ذكر تربي أي تزيد وتفضل على ما يتوقع مما كلف به فكان ممتنعاً عقلاً، وجواب الأول: ما مر في مسألة خلق الأعمال من أن قدرة العبد وإن كانت غير مؤثرة إلا أن لها تعلقاً بالفعل يسمى كسباً، وباعتباره جاز التكليف به فلا يكون تكليفاً بما لا يطاق بالكلية. وجواب الثاني: أن يقال: ما في التكاليف
The second group: those who said that prophethood is not free from obligation, because its benefit is not free from it, and also it is not free from its necessary concomitant, obligation, by agreement and consensus of those who affirm it. Then obligation, which is impossible for several reasons: First: Determinism and lack of choice in actions are established, because it is clear that the action of the servant occurs by God's power, for the servant's power has no effect at all according to you, and obligation to do another's act is an obligation beyond capacity; and because the action is either known to occur from the servant or known not to occur; in the first case it is necessary, in the second impossible, and there is no power over either. Then obligation, when determinism is established, is evil, so it is impossible. Second: Obligation harms the servant, because it entails fatigue from the act if he performs it, or punishment for omission if he refrains, and harm is evil, and God is transcendent above it. Third: Obligation is either without purpose, which is futile and evil; or for a purpose that returns to God, which is impossible because He is transcendent above all purposes, such as obtaining benefits or repelling harms; or for a purpose that returns to the servant, which is either harm, negated by consensus, or benefit. And obligation that brings benefit and torment for its omission is contrary to reason, for it is like saying to him: 'Get the benefit for yourself, or I will torment you forever and ever.' There is no doubt that the most important of his interests is to avoid torment. Moreover, the benefit that obligation contains for the obedient believers is counterbalanced by the great harm it inflicts on the disbelievers and sinners. There is no doubt that harming a group for the benefit of others is unjust and evil. Fourth: Obligation to perform an act is either with the existence of the act, and there is no benefit in it at all because of its necessity and predetermined occurrence; then it is futile and evil. Similarly, if the obligation is after the act, as it is an obligation to obtain what has already been obtained. As for before the existence of the act, it is an obligation beyond capacity, because the act before its existence is impossible, since a thing cannot exist when it is non-existent. Obligation beyond capacity is invalid for those who do not allow it, and this is apparent. As for those who allow it, they do not affirm its occurrence, nor that every obligation is like that, i.e., obligation beyond capacity. Obligation to an act before it necessitates the occurrence of obligation beyond capacity, and necessitates that every obligation of this kind [is invalid]; thus this division is also invalid according to them. And when the present divisions are invalid, obligation is absolutely impossible. Fifth: This is from some Sufis of the licentious ones, that obligation to strenuous physical acts distracts the inner self from contemplation on knowledge of God and what is necessary for Him of attributes and what is possible and impossible for Him of actions. There is no doubt that the expected benefit from this missed [contemplation]—namely, reflection on what was mentioned—is greater than what is expected from what one is obligated to do, so it is rationally impossible. The answer to the first: What was mentioned in the issue of creation of actions, that the servant's power, although not effective, has a relation to the act called acquisition (kasb), and by virtue of it obligation to it is permissible, so it is not entirely obligation beyond capacity. And the answer to the second: It is said: what is in the obligations...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the second group who argue that prophethood necessarily entails obligation (taklif), and then lists five proofs that obligation is impossible. The first proof is based on determinism (jabr): if all actions are from God and the servant has no power, then obligation is to another's act, which is impossible. Also, if an action is known to occur or not, it is either necessary or impossible, and the servant has no power over either. Thus obligation after determinism is evil. The second proof states that obligation harms the servant (idrar) through fatigue or punishment, and harm is evil, so God is above it. The third proof divides purposes: if no purpose, it is futile; if a purpose returns to God, that is impossible; if to the servant, either harm (negated) or benefit. But benefit accompanied by eternal torment for failure is unreasonable, and the harm to disbelievers outweighs benefit to believers. The fourth proof examines temporal divisions: obligation with the act is futile (since already necessary); after the act is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); before the act is obligation beyond capacity (because the act does not yet exist). The fifth proof, from some Sufis, argues that obligation to physical acts distracts from contemplation of God, and the benefit of contemplation outweighs that of the acts. The answer to the first proof refers to the doctrine of kasb (acquisition), where the servant's power, though not effective, has a relation to the act that makes obligation permissible. The answer to the second proof begins but is cut off.

Key Terms
الجبر
الاختيار
التكليف بما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The determinism argument only relies on God's power; it does not involve foreknowledge.
Correction: The argument explicitly includes divine foreknowledge as a second strand: actions are either known to occur or not, making them necessary or impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why is obligation impossible when determinism is established?
A) Because the servant has no power over his actions
B) Because God changes His mind
C) Because obligation distracts from contemplation
D) Because it harms the servant

Key Takeaway
The first argument uses both powerlessness and foreknowledge under determinism to claim that obligation is impossible because it demands the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake in a religious obligation, do you ever attribute it to 'God's decree' as a way to avoid feeling guilty? How does this lesson challenge that tendency by showing that such thinking makes obligation meaningless?
?
If you truly believed that your actions are predetermined, would you still feel a personal responsibility to improve your character? How does the argument in this passage refute that belief and call you to take full ownership of your choices?
?
Consider the last time you procrastinated in performing a good deed. Did you subconsciously think, 'If it was meant to happen, it will'? How does the logical conclusion of this passage expose that as a dangerous excuse that undermines the entire structure of divine obligation?

Harm and Purpose: Second and Third Arguments
Second and third arguments against obligation: harm and purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third arguments against obligation: that it harms the servant, and that any purpose for it is either futile, impossible, or unjust.

Core Idea
Obligation harms the servant and serves no justifiable purpose.

Having dispensed with determinism, the second argument focuses on the direct harm of obligation: fatigue if obeyed, punishment if disobeyed. Since God is transcendent above causing harm, obligation cannot be from Him. The third argument examines the purpose of obligation: if no purpose, it is futile; if a purpose returns to God, that is impossible; if to the servant, it is either harm (already negated) or benefit, but the benefit is outweighed by the immense harm to disbelievers and sinners, making it unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument on harm
Notice how obligation entails either fatigue from action or punishment from omission – both harmful.
The argument concludes that harm is evil, so God cannot command it.
2
Read the third argument on purpose
Follow the three-way division of purposes: none, to God, to servant.
Each branch is shown to be problematic.
3
Understand the 'benefit' branch
Even if obligation is for the servant's benefit, the threat of eternal torment is unreasonable, and the harm to disbelievers outweighs any benefit.
This uses the principle that harming one group for another's benefit is unjust.

Obligation to an act before it necessitates the occurrence of obligation beyond capacity, and necessitates that every obligation of this kind [is invalid]; thus this division is also invalid according to them. And when the present divisions are invalid, obligation is absolutely impossible. Fifth: This is from some Sufis of the licentious ones, that obligation to strenuous physical acts distracts the inner self from contemplation on knowledge of God and what is necessary for Him of attributes and what is possible and impossible for Him of actions. There is no doubt that the expected benefit from this missed [contemplation]—namely, reflection on what was mentioned—is greater than what is expected from what one is obligated to do, so it is rationally impossible. The answer to the first: What was mentioned in the issue of creation of actions, that the servant's power, although not effective, has a relation to the act called acquisition (kasb), and by virtue of it obligation to it is permissible, so it is not entirely obligation beyond capacity. And the answer to the second: It is said: what is in the obligations...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the second group who argue that prophethood necessarily entails obligation (taklif), and then lists five proofs that obligation is impossible. The first proof is based on determinism (jabr): if all actions are from God and the servant has no power, then obligation is to another's act, which is impossible. Also, if an action is known to occur or not, it is either necessary or impossible, and the servant has no power over either. Thus obligation after determinism is evil. The second proof states that obligation harms the servant (idrar) through fatigue or punishment, and harm is evil, so God is above it. The third proof divides purposes: if no purpose, it is futile; if a purpose returns to God, that is impossible; if to the servant, either harm (negated) or benefit. But benefit accompanied by eternal torment for failure is unreasonable, and the harm to disbelievers outweighs benefit to believers. The fourth proof examines temporal divisions: obligation with the act is futile (since already necessary); after the act is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); before the act is obligation beyond capacity (because the act does not yet exist). The fifth proof, from some Sufis, argues that obligation to physical acts distracts from contemplation of God, and the benefit of contemplation outweighs that of the acts. The answer to the first proof refers to the doctrine of kasb (acquisition), where the servant's power, though not effective, has a relation to the act that makes obligation permissible. The answer to the second proof begins but is cut off.

Key Terms
الإضرار
العبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what is one reason the 'benefit' purpose is rejected?
A) Because benefit is impossible
B) Because the threat of eternal torment is unreasonable
C) Because God doesn't want to benefit anyone
D) Because it contradicts determinism

Key Takeaway
Obligation is impossible because it causes harm and any conceivable purpose is either futile, impossible, or unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's obligation inevitably causes harm—fatigue in action or punishment in omission—how do you reconcile your daily worship with this argument? Does your faith rest on a conviction that God's commands are ultimately good despite the cost? What would you say to someone who uses this argument to reject belief in God's justice?
?
The argument compares the benefit-threat structure of obligation to extortion: 'Get the benefit or I will torment you forever.' Does this challenge your understanding of God's mercy? How do you personally experience the tension between threat and love in your relationship with God?
?
Consider the millions who never accept Islam—does the harm that obligation brings them (eternal punishment) trouble you? How does this lesson affect your view of God's fairness? What do you truly believe about God's justice when faced with this theological difficulty?

8 min
Temporal and Sufi Arguments: Fourth and Fifth
Fourth and fifth arguments: temporal cases and distraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument (temporal divisions of obligation) and the fifth argument (distraction from contemplation) against obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation is impossible because it either demands what already exists, what has already occurred, or what does not yet exist—and physical acts distract from higher spiritual goals.

The fourth argument examines the timing of obligation relative to the act: if the act already exists, obligation is futile; if after the act, it is impossible (تحصيل الحاصل); if before the act, it demands something that does not exist, which is beyond capacity. Additionally, a fifth argument from some Sufis claims that bodily obligations distract from contemplation of God, which is a greater good. Thus, obligation is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument's three cases
Identify the three temporal positions: with the act, after the act, before the act.
Note the key concept 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is already obtained).
2
Understand the problem with each case
For each case, the argument shows why obligation is futile or impossible.
3
Read the fifth argument from some Sufis
Notice that this argument prioritizes spiritual contemplation over physical acts.
The argument claims that the benefit of contemplation far exceeds that of obligations.
4
Synthesize the overall conclusion
Both arguments independently conclude that obligation is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة الثانية: من قال البعثة لا تخلو عن التكليف لأن فائدتها التي لا تخلو هي عنها وأيضاً هي لا تخلو عن لازمها التكليف باتفاق وإجماع من القائلين بها ثم إن التكليف الذي هو ممتنع لوجوه: الأول: ثبت الجبر وعدم الاختيار في الأفعال وذلك لما تبين من أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله إذ لا تأثير لقدرة العبد عندكم أصلاً والتكليف بفعل الغير تكليف بما لا يطاق ومن أن الفعل إما معلوم الوقوع من العبد أو معلوم اللاوقوع منه فعلى الأول يكون ضرورياً وعلى الثاني ممتنعاً ولا قدرة على شيء منهما والتكليف حينئذ أي حين إذ ثبت الجبر قبيح فيكون ممتنعاً. الثاني: التكليف إضرار بالعبد لما يلزمه من التعب بالفعل إذا أقدم التعذيب ثم إنه أي النفع الذي يتضمنه التكليف للمؤمنين المطيعين معارض بما فيه من المضرة العظيمة بالكفار والعصاة ولا شك أن إضرار جماعة لمنفعة آخرين ظلم قبيح. الرابع: التكليف بإيقاع الفعل إما مع وجود الفعل ولا فائدة فيه أصلا لوجوبه وتعين صدوره حينئذ فيكون عبثاً قبيحاً، وكذا الحال إذا كان التكليف بعد الفعل مع أنه تكليف بتحصيل الحاصل. وأما قبل وجود الفعل وإنه تكليف بما لا يطاق لأن الفعل قبل الفعل محال إذ لا يمكن وجود الشيء حال عدمه والتكليف بما لا يطاق باطل عند لا من يجوزه وهو ظاهر، وأما من جوزه فإنه لا يقول بوقوعه ولا أن كل تكليف كذلك أي تكليف بما لا يطاق والتكليف بالفعل قبله يستلزم وقوع التكليف بما لا يطاق ويستلزم أن كل تكليف من هذا القبيل فيكون هذا القسم باطلاً عنده أيضاً، وإذا بطلت الأقسام الحاضرة امتنع التكليف مطلقاً. الخامس: وهو لبعض الصوفية من أهل الإباحة أن التكليف بالأفعال الشاقة البدنية يشغل الباطن عن التفكر في معرفة الله تعالى وما يجب له من الصفات ويجوز ويمتنع عليه من الأفعال ولا شك أن المصلحة المتوقعة من هذا الفائت وهو النظر فيما ذكر تربي أي تزيد وتفضل على ما يتوقع بالكلية. وجواب الثاني: أن يقال: ما في التكاليف
The second group: those who said that prophethood is not free from obligation, because its benefit is not free from it, and also it is not free from its necessary concomitant, obligation, by agreement and consensus of those who affirm it. Then obligation, which is impossible for several reasons: First: Determinism and lack of choice in actions are established, because it is clear that the action of the servant occurs by God's power, for the servant's power has no effect at all according to you, and obligation to do another's act is an obligation beyond capacity; and because the action is either known to occur from the servant or known not to occur; in the first case it is necessary, in the second impossible, and there is no power over either. Then obligation, when determinism is established, is evil, so it is impossible. Second: Obligation harms the servant, because it entails fatigue from the act if he performs it, or punishment for omission if he refrains, and harm is evil, and God is transcendent above it. Third: Obligation is either without purpose, which is futile and evil; or for a purpose that returns to God, which is impossible because He is transcendent above all purposes, such as obtaining benefits or repelling harms; or for a purpose that returns to the servant, which is either harm, negated by consensus, or benefit. And obligation that brings benefit and torment for its omission is contrary to reason, for it is like saying to him: 'Get the benefit for yourself, or I will torment you forever and ever.' There is no doubt that the most important of his interests is to avoid torment. Moreover, the benefit that obligation contains for the obedient believers is counterbalanced by the great harm it inflicts on the disbelievers and sinners. There is no doubt that harming a group for the benefit of others is unjust and evil. Fourth: Obligation to perform an act is either with the existence of the act, and there is no benefit in it at all because of its necessity and predetermined occurrence; then it is futile and evil. Similarly, if the obligation is after the act, as it is an obligation to obtain what has already been obtained. As for before the existence of the act, it is an obligation beyond capacity, because the act before its existence is impossible, since a thing cannot exist when it is non-existent. Obligation beyond capacity is invalid for those who do not allow it, and this is apparent. As for those who allow it, they do not affirm its occurrence, nor that every obligation is like that, i.e., obligation beyond capacity. Obligation to an act before it necessitates the occurrence of obligation beyond capacity, and necessitates that every obligation of this kind [is invalid]; thus this division is also invalid according to them. And when the present divisions are invalid, obligation is absolutely impossible. Fifth: This is from some Sufis of the licentious ones, that obligation to strenuous physical acts distracts the inner self from contemplation on knowledge of God and what is necessary for Him of attributes and what is possible and impossible for Him of actions. There is no doubt that the expected benefit from this missed [contemplation]—namely, reflection on what was mentioned—is greater than what is expected from what one is obligated to do, so it is rationally impossible. The answer to the first: What was mentioned in the issue of creation of actions, that the servant's power, although not effective, has a relation to the act called acquisition (kasb), and by virtue of it obligation to it is permissible, so it is not entirely obligation beyond capacity. And the answer to the second: It is said: what is in the obligations...
