Checkpoint
In the fourth argument, why is obligating an act after it has occurred considered impossible?
A) Because it is futile
B) Because it is an obligation to obtain what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل)
C) Because it distracts from contemplation
D) Because it harms the servant

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows all temporal positions fail, and the fifth argues that physical obligations distract from greater spiritual benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fifth argument says that physical acts distract from contemplation of God. Do you find your own acts of worship—prayer, fasting, charity—draw you closer to God or do they sometimes become mechanical routines that distract from deeper reflection? What would it mean for you to transform a ritual into a moment of true contemplation? Commit to one change in your next prayer to focus on God's attributes.
?
The fourth argument claims that if an act is predetermined, obligation is futile. Do you live as if your actions are predetermined—excusing yourself from effort because 'it is written'? Or do you act with full responsibility, knowing that every choice matters? Examine one area where you have been passive, and decide to act with intention today.
?
According to the Sufi argument, reflection on God's attributes is of greater benefit than physical acts. How much time do you allocate to pure contemplation compared to outward deeds? If you are honest, does your spiritual life reflect this priority? What would it take for you to carve out just ten minutes daily for silent reflection on who God is?

Responses to Objections: Kasb and Beyond
Answers to first two objections, focusing on kasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reply to the first objection using the doctrine of kasb (acquisition), and note the beginning of the response to the second objection.

Core Idea
Kasb solves determinism: the servant's power relates to the act, making obligation permissible.

The author now begins to answer the five objections. The first reply invokes the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition): although the servant's power is not effective in creation, it has a relational 'talluq' to the act, which makes the act attributable to the servant and thus obligation meaningful. The answer to the second objection is cut off but starts to address the harm argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the first objection
Identify the key concept: 'talluq' (relation) called kasb.
Kasb is the Ash'ari solution to reconcile divine power with human responsibility.
2
Understand how kasb resolves the determinism problem
Even though God creates the act, the servant's kasb makes the act his own, so obligation is not to another's act.
The text says 'it is not entirely obligation beyond capacity'.
3
Note the incomplete answer to the second objection
The text begins 'It is said: what is in the obligations...' and is cut off.
We do not have the full response.
4
Reflect on the method of rebuttal
The author uses established kalam doctrines (kasb) to counter the objections.

Key Terms
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The reply to the determinism argument simply denies determinism.
Correction: The reply accepts determinism (servant's power not effective) but adds the concept of kasb to retain responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, why is obligation not entirely beyond capacity?
A) Because the servant's power is effective
B) Because of the doctrine of kasb (acquisition)
C) Because God changes His decree
D) Because the act does not exist

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari concept of kasb is used to answer the determinism objection by attributing the act to the servant relationally, allowing obligation without it being entirely beyond capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe your choices matter, or do you sometimes use qadar as an excuse to avoid responsibility? How does the concept of kasb challenge that mindset and call you to a higher level of accountability?
?
If your actions are only relationally yours (kasb), does that diminish your sense of personal guilt or effort before Allah? Why or why not—and what does your answer reveal about your understanding of tawheed?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt burdened by an obligation you thought was beyond your capacity. How does knowing that Allah does not obligate you with anything entirely beyond your capacity (in the sense of this lesson) change how you approach that command? Will you commit to trusting His wisdom and acting accordingly?

Mu'tazila on Small Sins: Deserving and Forgiveness
Critical commentary on Mu'tazila view of small sins and deserving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced Mu'tazila position on whether small sins incur deserving punishment, and how their interpretation of Quranic verses and denial of intercession shape that view.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila may differentiate between pure small sins and those mixed with major ones—only the latter deserve punishment.

This passage delves into a subtle debate about the Mu'tazila stance on small sins. The commentator examines whether they uniformly deny deserving punishment for all small sins, or distinguish between pure small sins and those associated with major sins. This distinction has implications for their proof based on Quranic verses and their denial of intercession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the commentator's critical tone and the alternative interpretations offered.
The passage begins with 'قوله : ( ولا يقولون به في غير صورة النزاع فيه بحث...'.
2
Identify the key positions
The passage presents two possible Mu'tazila positions: (1) No deserving for pure small sins, but deserving for small sins mixed with major ones. (2) No deserving for any small sin at all. Find these in the text.
Look for the phrases 'الصغائر الصرفة' and 'الصغائر المقرونة بالكبائر'.
3
Connect to the first proof
The commentator links this discussion to the Mu'tazila's use of Quran 4:31 as a proof. Understand how the interpretation of that verse affects their stance on small sins.
The verse is 'إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفر عنكم سيئاتكم'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this nuance matters for the broader theological argument about forgiveness and intercession. How does the Mu'tazila denial of intercession inform their view on small sins?
The commentator indicates that denial of intercession implies denial of deserving for all small sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا يقولون به في غير صورة النزاع فيه بحث إذ لعلهم يقولون لا استحقاق بالصغائر الصرفة كما يدل عليه استدلالهم بقوله تعالى : وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفر عنكم سيئاتكم ﴾ [النساء : ٣١] ، وأما الصغائر المقرونة بالكبائر ففيها الاستحقاق عندهم أيضاً، فيجوز أن يعفو الله تعالى عنها ويمكن أن يقال : لم يثبت منهم القول باستحقاق العقاب بارتكاب الصغيرة أصلاً، وإن أوهم به في الجملة استدلالهم على نفيه بالآية الكريمة ويدل على إنكارهم استحقاق العقاب بها مطلقاً إنكارهم الشفاعة لدرء العقاب كما لا يخفى على المنصف
His statement: 'And they do not affirm it except in the case of dispute' – there is discussion. For perhaps they say: No deserving by pure small sins, as indicated by their argument using His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden, We will expiate for you your evil deeds' [4:31]. As for small sins coupled with major ones, there is deserving according to them as well, so it is permissible that God forgive them. And it can be said: It has not been established from them the statement of deserving punishment for committing a small sin at all, although their argument for its negation by the noble verse seems to imply it. And their denial of intercession for warding off punishment indicates their denial of deserving punishment by small sins absolutely, as is not hidden from the fair-minded.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the first proof of the previous lesson. The author points out that the claim that Mu'tazila do not affirm deserving punishment except in the disputed case (major sins before repentance) is debatable. Because they might say that pure small sins (not associated with major sins) do not incur deserving, as their use of 4:31 suggests. But small sins associated with major sins do incur deserving according to them, so God may forgive them. Then the commentator offers a possible resolution: it has not been established that Mu'tazila affirm deserving for any small sin at all, despite the implication of their argument. Their denial of intercession for removing punishment indicates they deny deserving for all small sins. This clarifies the nuance of the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
الصغائر الصرفة
الصغائر المقرونة بالكبائر
الاستحقاق
الشفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila uniformly deny that any small sin deserves punishment.
Correction: The commentary shows two possibilities: they may deny deserving only for pure small sins, or they may deny it altogether. Their use of Quran 4:31 and denial of intercession supports the latter.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the possible Mu'tazila position on small sins coupled with major ones?
A) They deserve punishment
B) They do not deserve punishment
C) They are forgiven automatically
D) They are not sins

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view on small sins is nuanced; they may only affirm deserving for small sins when coupled with major sins, or they may deny deserving altogether, as indicated by their denial of intercession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a small sin as trivial, assuming God will automatically forgive it? How does this lesson's discussion of deserving (istihqaq) challenge you to take every sin seriously, even those you call 'small'?
?
If the Mu'tazila, known for their rationalism, still held nuanced views on forgiveness, what does that say about your own tendency to oversimplify God's justice? Are you comfortable with the uncertainty of whether your 'pure' small sins might still require accountability?
?
The passage shows that even scholars debated the exact position of the Mu'tazila. When you encounter ambiguity in theological issues, do you humbly seek to understand the depth, or do you cling to simplistic labels? How can this lesson push you to be more fair-minded in your own beliefs?

Divine Decree

Second Disagreement: Denial of God's Creation of Actions
The author presents and refutes two arguments for declaring Mu'tazila disbelievers for denying God's creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's denial that God creates human actions, the two arguments used to declare them disbelievers, and the scholarly responses refuting each argument.

Core Idea
Denying God creates actions does not automatically necessitate disbelief; careful reasoning about implication is crucial.

This passage continues the discussion of grounds for declaring the Mu'tazila disbelievers. The second ground is their denial that God creates the servant's actions. The author presents two arguments for why this is disbelief and then refutes both, showing that the charge of disbelief is not straightforward and must be examined carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two distinct arguments and the author's responses.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two arguments for takfir
The passage presents two reasons to declare the Mu'tazila disbelievers. Locate them: (1) limiting God's power resembles Magian dualism; (2) their view contradicts the Muslim consensus on supplication.
The first argument is introduced with 'أما أولاً' and the second with 'وأما ثانياً'.
3
Analyze the first argument and refutation
The author argues that by denying God's power over human actions, the Mu'tazila effectively posit a partner similar to Magian dualism. Then he refutes: the Magians were not declared disbelievers for that specific point but for other reasons.
Compare the analogy carefully.
4
Analyze the second argument and refutation
The second argument is that the Mu'tazila view makes supplication asking God for faith pointless, contradicting consensus. The author responds that breaking consensus is not disbelief unless it is decisive consensus, and furthermore the Mu'tazila may not realize that their view implies breaking consensus.
Note the distinction between explicit doctrine and necessary consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من تلك الأمور إنكارهم إيجاد الله لفعل العبد وأنه كفر ، أما أولاً فلأنهم جعلوه غير قادر على فعل العبد إما على عينه كالجبائية وإما على مثله كالبلخي وأتباعه وإما على القبيح مطلقا كالنظام ومتابعيه ( وجعلوا العبد غير قادر على فعله تعالى فهو إثبات للشريك مذهب المجوس) حيث أثبتوا له شريكاً لا يقدر أحدهما على مقدور الآخر. هو كما أن من الله ( وأما ثانياً : فللإجماع المنعقد من الأمة ( على التضرع) والابتهال (إلى في يرزقهم الإيمان ) ويجنبهم عن الكفر ) وهم ينكرونه لأنهم يقولون : قد فعل الله اللطف ما أمكن لوجوبه عليه، وأما نفس الإيمان فليس من فعله تعالى بل من فعل العباد كالكفر، فلا فائدة في ذلك الابتهال المجمع عليه ( قلنا : المجوس لم يكفروا بقولهم : إن الله لا يقدر على فعل الشيطان بل ( كفروا بغيره)، وهو قولهم بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى وعجزه عن دفع الشيطان واحتياجه في دفعه إلى الاستعانة بالملائكة (و) أيضاً (خرق الإجماع) مطلقاً، ليس بكفر) بل خرق الإجماع القطعي الذي صار من ضرورات الدين (ثم إن سلمنا أن خرق الإجماع الذي ذكرتموه كفر قلنا : ذلك الخرق ليس مذهبهم بل غايته أنه لازم منه ، و ( من يلزمه الكفر ولا يعلم به لم قلتم أنه كافر؟
The second of those matters: Their denial of God's creation of the servant's action, and that it is disbelief. First, because they made Him not powerful over the servant's action—either over its essence like al-Juba'i, or over its like like al-Balkhi and his followers, or over evil absolutely like al-Nazzam and his followers—and they made the servant not powerful over His action, so it is an establishment of a partner, the doctrine of the Magians, since they established for Him a partner with neither able to do what the other can do. It is as if from God. And second, because of the consensus of the community on supplication and earnest prayer to (God) that He grant them faith and keep them away from disbelief, while they deny it because they say: God has done the grace as much as possible due to its obligation on Him, but as for faith itself, it is not from His action but from the action of servants, like disbelief, so there is no benefit in that agreed-upon earnest prayer. We say: The Magians did not disbelieve by their saying that God is not able to act on Satan, but they disbelieved by something else, i.e., their saying that God's powers are finite and that He is unable to repel Satan and needs the help of angels to repel him. And also, breaking consensus absolutely is not disbelief, but breaking decisive consensus that has become a necessity of religion. Then, if we grant that breaking the consensus you mentioned is disbelief, we say: That breaking is not their doctrine; rather, it is a necessary consequence of it. And (if someone) is necessitated disbelief but does not know it, why do you say he is a disbeliever?
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the second ground for declaring Mu'tazila disbelievers: their denial that God creates the servant's actions. The author gives two arguments for why this is disbelief. First, by limiting God's power over actions (some Mu'tazila deny God's power over the servant's actions directly, others over their like, others over evil), they effectively posit a partner with God, resembling the Magian doctrine of two creators. The author then refutes this by noting that the Magians were not declared disbelievers for denying God's power over Satan specifically, but for other reasons (like finitude of God's power). So analogizing to Magians may not hold. Second, the Mu'tazila's view contradicts the Muslim consensus on supplication asking God to grant faith, because they say faith is from the servant, not from God, making supplication pointless. The author responds that breaking consensus is not disbelief unless it is decisive consensus that is a necessity of religion. Moreover, even if it were disbelief, the Mu'tazila do not explicitly hold that they are breaking consensus; it is a necessary implication of their view, but they may not realize it. And one who is led to disbelief inadvertently without knowledge is not to be declared disbeliever.

Key Terms
إيجاد الله لفعل العبد
المجوس
شريك
إجماع
اللطف
لازم المذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying God's creation of actions is automatically shirk and makes one a disbeliever without further analysis.
Correction: The passage shows that the charge must be examined carefully; the arguments for takfir are not decisive, and necessary implications that the person is unaware of do not warrant takfir.

Checkpoint
According to the author's second response, why is breaking consensus not automatically disbelief?
A) Because breaking consensus is always permissible
B) Because only breaking decisive consensus that is a necessity of religion is disbelief
C) Because the Mu'tazila never broke consensus
D) Because consensus is not a valid proof

Key Takeaway
Charging takfir requires careful examination of both the explicit doctrine and its necessary implications, and even then, unintentional consequences may not warrant takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been quick to judge someone's faith because of a belief they hold, without examining whether they truly understand the implications? How can this passage change your approach?
?
Do you truly believe that God creates all your actions, including your sins? How does that shape your view of your own responsibility and your need for His mercy?
?
If you were to realize that a belief you hold necessarily implies something contrary to Islam, would you be willing to abandon that belief, or would you defend it? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to truth vs. your ego?

Divine Decree

The Fourth Point: Refuting the Denial of God's Will and Decree
Sanusi refutes the opponents' claim of consensus on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the opponents' appeal to consensus on God's will and why opposing such a consensus does not automatically constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Consensus on God's will is not absolute, and denying it is not automatic disbelief.

Sanusi continues his refutation of those who deny God's will and decree. The opponents claim consensus and a hadith that God's will is always effective, yet they themselves allow exceptions. Sanusi counters by questioning the validity of the claimed consensus and clarifying that even if it were valid, opposing it does not warrant takfir. This lesson unpacks this precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of objection and response.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and inconsistency
The opponents claim consensus that whatever God wills happens, but then suggest exceptions. Spot this contradiction.
This inconsistency is the target of Sanusi's refutation.
3
Understand Sanusi's twofold response
Sanusi gives two responses: (1) denial of the consensus itself, (2) denial that opposing it leads to disbelief. Distinguish these two parts.
The second part is crucial for understanding the limits of takfir.
4
Reflect on the implications for takfir
Think about why denying a consensus on a non-fundamental issue does not make one a disbeliever.
This lesson guards against extreme positions in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قد أجمع من قبلهم من الأمة على أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، وقد ورد في الحديث أيضاً وهم ينكرونه حيث يدعون أنه قد يشاء الله شيئاً ولا يكون وقد لا يشاء ويكون، قلنا : نمنع الإجماع وعلى تقدير ثبوته نمنع كون مخالفه كافراً كما عرفت.
The fourth: They claimed consensus from the earlier community that whatever God wills occurs and whatever He does not will does not occur, and it has also been reported in the hadith. And they deny it, claiming that God may will something and it does not happen, and may not will and it happens. We say: We deny the consensus, and if it were established, we deny that its opponent is a disbeliever, as you have known.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponents claim consensus that God's will is always effective, but they themselves deny it by asserting that God may will something and it doesn't happen. The response is twofold: first, the consensus is not admitted; second, even if it were valid, opposing it does not necessarily entail disbelief, as such issues are not among the fundamentals that require takfir.

Key Terms
الإجماع
المشيئة
التكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying a consensus on a theological point automatically makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if a consensus were established, opposing it does not necessarily entail takfir because the conditions for takfir are strict and not all theological issues are fundamental.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first response to the opponents' argument about consensus?
A) Accept the consensus as valid
B) Deny the existence of such a consensus
C) Declare the opponents disbelievers
D) Cite a hadith to support them

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the opponents' claim of consensus by denying its validity and arguing that even if valid, opposing it does not warrant takfir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a widely held belief among Muslims is automatically correct and binding, without examining the evidence yourself? How does Sanusi's denial of consensus challenge you to verify before accepting? What specific belief in your own life might you need to re-examine with this same critical caution?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you quickly judge the other side as misguided or even outside the fold of Islam? How does Sanusi's careful approach to takfir in this passage call you to humility and restraint? Will you commit to withholding judgment until you understand the full evidence?
?
In your own life, do you hold a view about God's will and decree that contradicts what you actually observe in your experiences? How does the opponents' contradictory claim—that God may will something and it doesn't happen—mirror your own inner contradictions? What would it mean for you to consistently affirm that God's will is always effective, even when you don't understand His wisdom? Can you surrender to that truth today?
